Real and Relative Species in Plants
Al-'Attar explains real and relative species using Indian wheat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real species (naw' haqiqi) and a relative species (naw' idafi) using the example of Indian wheat.

Core Idea
A species can be both real and relative depending on perspective.

In this passage, al-'Attar uses the example of Indian wheat to clarify that a species can be simultaneously real (having only individuals beneath it) and relative (falling under a higher genus). This dual categorization is essential for understanding the hierarchical nature of the five universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example
The commentator uses Indian wheat (al-qamḥ al-hindī) as a concrete example. Note that it is a specific type of wheat.
2
Check what is below it
Below Indian wheat are individual plants (ashkhāṣ). It is the complete quiddity of those individuals, making it a real species (naw' ḥaqīqī).
3
Identify what is above it
Indian wheat falls under absolute wheat (muṭlaq qamḥ). This makes it a relative species (naw' iḍāfī) because it is related to a higher genus.
Real and relative are not contradictory; they are two aspects of the same term.
4
Generalize the principle
All lowest species are real species. Many are also relative if there is a higher genus. This perspectival nature applies throughout the hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نوضح لك المقام بأمثلة جامعة غير ما لاكثه الألسنة ومجته الأسماع من تخصيص التمثيل بحيوان وجوهر لأسفل الأجناس وأعلاها، وبجسم وإنسان لأعلى الأنواع وأسفلها، وبما بين الأوَّلين من الجسم النَّامي والجسم للمتوسط من الأجناس، وبما بين الآخرين من الجسم النامي والحيوان للمتوسط من الأنواع حتى يظن الناظر إليه أنهم لم يعثروا على مثال آخر. فنقول وبالله التوفيق: النبات جنس تحته أنواع كثيرة لا يحصيها الإنسان من قمح، وذرة، وأرز وبلح وقطن وبقل وزهر ... إلخ، وكل هذه الأنواع تحته أنواع كثيرة، فمطلق القمح تحته الهندي والبلدي والإسترالي ونحوها، والذرة تحته البلدي والعويجة وناب الجمل ونحوها، والأرز تحته اليباني والسلطاني وعين البنت ونحوها والبلح تحته الزغلول والسماني والحياني ونحوها، والقطن تحته السكلاريدي والجيزة والأشموني والبقل تحته الجزر واللفت والفجل ونحوها، والزهر تحته الورد والنرجس والفل ونحوها فالقمح الهندي كالإنسان نوع حقيقي وإضافي، أما كونه حقيقياً؛ فلأنَّ ما تحته أشخاص، وهو تمام ماهيته، وأما كونه إضافياً، فلاندراجه مع بقية أنواعه تحت مطلق قمح، وما قيل في القمح يقال في الذرة والأرز... إلخ، وحينئذ فقد تبين لنا أوّلاً أن القمح الهندي والذرة العويجة وقطن جيزة ... إلخ ، أنواع سافلة كالإنسان لأنها أخص الأنواع وما تحتها أشخاص، وثانياً أن مطلق قمح ومطلق ذرة ومطلق قطن ... إلخ أجناس قريبة سافلة وأنواع متوسطة، أما كونها قريبة سافلة ؛ فلأنَّها أخص الأجناس كالحيوان، وأما كونها أنواعاً متوسطة؛ فلاندراج أنواعها الحقيقية تحتها، ولاندراجها تحت نوع ثالث أعلى منها، وهو مطلق نبات وهذا النوع نوع و جنس متوسطان لاندراج غيره تحته ولاندراجه هو تحت نوع رابع أعلى منه وهو جسم نامي وهذا النوع نوع وجنس متوسطان لاندراج غيره تحته ولاندراجه هو تحت نوع خامس أعلى منه، وهو مطلق جسم وهذا النوع أعلى الأنواع وجنس متوسط، أما كونه أعلاها فلعدم وجود نوع فوقه وأما كونه جنساً متوسطاً؛
We clarify the matter with comprehensive examples other than those that tongues have chewed and ears have tired of—restricting examples to animal and substance for the lowest and highest genera, and to body and human for the highest and lowest species, and to what is between the first two, like growing body (jism nami) and body for the intermediate genera, and to what is between the others, like growing body and animal for the intermediate species—so that a beholder might think they found no other example. So we say, and with God is success: Plant is a genus under which are many species, which man cannot count, such as wheat, maize, rice, dates, cotton, vegetables, flowers, etc. And each of these species has numerous species under it. So absolute wheat under it has Indian, local, Australian, etc. Maize under it has local, 'awayjah, nab al-jamal, etc. Rice under it has yabani, sultani, 'ayn al-bint, etc. Dates under it have zaghlul, samani, hayani, etc. Cotton under it has skalaridi, jiza, ashmuni. Vegetables under it have carrots, turnips, radishes, etc. Flowers under it have roses, narcissus, jasmine, etc. So Indian wheat, like human, is a real and relative species. As for its being real, because under it are individuals, and it is the complete quiddity. As for its being relative, because it falls with its other types under absolute wheat. And what is said about wheat is said about maize, rice, etc. Thus it becomes clear to us first that Indian wheat, 'awayjah maize, jiza cotton, etc. are lowest species like human, because they are the most specific species and under them are individuals. Second, that absolute wheat, absolute maize, absolute cotton, etc. are proximate genera—which are also lowest genera—and intermediate species. As for their being proximate and lowest, because they are the most specific genera, like animal. As for their being intermediate species, because their real species fall under them, and because they themselves fall under a third type higher than them, namely absolute plant; and this type is an intermediate species and genus, because others fall under it and it falls under a fourth type higher than it, which is growing body; and this type is an intermediate species and genus, because others fall under it and it falls under a fifth type higher than it, which is absolute body; and this type is the highest of species and an intermediate genus: as for its being the highest, because no type exists above it; as for its being an intermediate genus...
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage from al-'Attar's commentary expands on the hierarchy of genera and species by providing a fresh, detailed example from the plant kingdom. The commentator criticizes the overuse of standard examples (animal, human, body) and offers a more concrete illustration. Starting with the genus 'plant', he lists many species like wheat, maize, etc., each with further subspecies. He then analyzes 'Indian wheat' as both a real species (true species) because it has individuals under it, and a relative species because it falls under 'absolute wheat'. This leads to a classification: specific types like Indian wheat are lowest species (naw' sāfil); absolute wheat is a proximate (lowest) genus (jins qarīb sāfil) and also an intermediate species because it falls under 'plant', which itself is both a species and genus under 'growing body', and so on up to 'absolute body', which is the highest species (a'lā al-anwā') and an intermediate genus (jins mutawassiṭ) because it has species below it but nothing above it in this chain. The example concretely shows how one entity can be simultaneously a genus and species depending on perspective.

Key Terms
النوع الحقيقي
النوع الإضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, Indian wheat is a real species because:
A) It falls under absolute wheat.
B) Under it are only individuals and it is the complete quiddity.
C) It is a type of plant.
D) It is the highest species.

Key Takeaway
A species is real if it has only individuals below it, and relative if it falls under a higher genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Hierarchy of Genera and Species in Plants
Al-'Attar illustrates the hierarchy from lowest species to highest species using plants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the levels in the hierarchy of genera and species (lowest species, proximate genus, intermediate species, highest species) using the plant example.

Core Idea
The same term can be both a genus and a species, depending on its position in the hierarchy.

Al-'Attar traces a chain from the lowest species (e.g., Indian wheat) up to the highest species (absolute body), showing how each intermediate level functions simultaneously as a genus for what is below and a species for what is above. This hierarchical view is fundamental to understanding the five universals.

Guided Path
1
List the levels from bottom to top
Start with Indian wheat (lowest species), then absolute wheat (proximate genus & intermediate species), then absolute plant (intermediate), then growing body (intermediate), then absolute body (highest species).
2
Identify the lowest species
Indian wheat and similar examples (e.g., 'awayjah maize) are the most specific; under them are only individuals. They are called 'lowest species' (anwā' sāfila).
Like 'human' in the standard hierarchy.
3
Identify the proximate genera
Absolute wheat, absolute maize, etc. are the immediate genera above the lowest species. They are also called 'lowest genera' (ajinās qarība sāfila) because no lower genus exists under them.
4
Trace the chain upward
Above absolute wheat is absolute plant, which is a genus for wheat and a species for growing body. Then growing body is a species for absolute body. Absolute body is the highest species (a'lā anwā') because nothing is above it.
5
Understand the dual role of intermediate levels
Each level except the extremes (lowest species and highest species) acts as both a genus (to what is below) and a species (to what is above). For example, absolute wheat is a genus for Indian wheat and a species for absolute plant.
This is the key insight: hierarchy is relational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نوضح لك المقام بأمثلة جامعة غير ما لاكثه الألسنة ومجته الأسماع من تخصيص التمثيل بحيوان وجوهر لأسفل الأجناس وأعلاها، وبجسم وإنسان لأعلى الأنواع وأسفلها، وبما بين الأوَّلين من الجسم النَّامي والجسم للمتوسط من الأجناس، وبما بين الآخرين من الجسم النامي والحيوان للمتوسط من الأنواع حتى يظن الناظر إليه أنهم لم يعثروا على مثال آخر. فنقول وبالله التوفيق: النبات جنس تحته أنواع كثيرة لا يحصيها الإنسان من قمح، وذرة، وأرز وبلح وقطن وبقل وزهر ... إلخ، وكل هذه الأنواع تحته أنواع كثيرة، فمطلق القمح تحته الهندي والبلدي والإسترالي ونحوها، والذرة تحته البلدي والعويجة وناب الجمل ونحوها، والأرز تحته اليباني والسلطاني وعين البنت ونحوها والبلح تحته الزغلول والسماني والحياني ونحوها، والقطن تحته السكلاريدي والجيزة والأشموني والبقل تحته الجزر واللفت والفجل ونحوها، والزهر تحته الورد والنرجس والفل ونحوها فالقمح الهندي كالإنسان نوع حقيقي وإضافي، أما كونه حقيقياً؛ فلأنَّ ما تحته أشخاص، وهو تمام ماهيته، وأما كونه إضافياً، فلاندراجه مع بقية أنواعه تحت مطلق قمح، وما قيل في القمح يقال في الذرة والأرز... إلخ، وحينئذ فقد تبين لنا أوّلاً أن القمح الهندي والذرة العويجة وقطن جيزة ... إلخ ، أنواع سافلة كالإنسان لأنها أخص الأنواع وما تحتها أشخاص، وثانياً أن مطلق قمح ومطلق ذرة ومطلق قطن ... إلخ أجناس قريبة سافلة وأنواع متوسطة، أما كونها قريبة سافلة ؛ فلأنَّها أخص الأجناس كالحيوان، وأما كونها أنواعاً متوسطة؛ فلاندراج أنواعها الحقيقية تحتها، ولاندراجها تحت نوع ثالث أعلى منها، وهو مطلق نبات وهذا النوع نوع و جنس متوسطان لاندراج غيره تحته ولاندراجه هو تحت نوع رابع أعلى منه وهو جسم نامي وهذا النوع نوع وجنس متوسطان لاندراج غيره تحته ولاندراجه هو تحت نوع خامس أعلى منه، وهو مطلق جسم وهذا النوع أعلى الأنواع وجنس متوسط، أما كونه أعلاها فلعدم وجود نوع فوقه وأما كونه جنساً متوسطاً؛
We clarify the matter with comprehensive examples other than those that tongues have chewed and ears have tired of—restricting examples to animal and substance for the lowest and highest genera, and to body and human for the highest and lowest species, and to what is between the first two, like growing body (jism nami) and body for the intermediate genera, and to what is between the others, like growing body and animal for the intermediate species—so that a beholder might think they found no other example. So we say, and with God is success: Plant is a genus under which are many species, which man cannot count, such as wheat, maize, rice, dates, cotton, vegetables, flowers, etc. And each of these species has numerous species under it. So absolute wheat under it has Indian, local, Australian, etc. Maize under it has local, 'awayjah, nab al-jamal, etc. Rice under it has yabani, sultani, 'ayn al-bint, etc. Dates under it have zaghlul, samani, hayani, etc. Cotton under it has skalaridi, jiza, ashmuni. Vegetables under it have carrots, turnips, radishes, etc. Flowers under it have roses, narcissus, jasmine, etc. So Indian wheat, like human, is a real and relative species. As for its being real, because under it are individuals, and it is the complete quiddity. As for its being relative, because it falls with its other types under absolute wheat. And what is said about wheat is said about maize, rice, etc. Thus it becomes clear to us first that Indian wheat, 'awayjah maize, jiza cotton, etc. are lowest species like human, because they are the most specific species and under them are individuals. Second, that absolute wheat, absolute maize, absolute cotton, etc. are proximate genera—which are also lowest genera—and intermediate species. As for their being proximate and lowest, because they are the most specific genera, like animal. As for their being intermediate species, because their real species fall under them, and because they themselves fall under a third type higher than them, namely absolute plant; and this type is an intermediate species and genus, because others fall under it and it falls under a fourth type higher than it, which is growing body; and this type is an intermediate species and genus, because others fall under it and it falls under a fifth type higher than it, which is absolute body; and this type is the highest of species and an intermediate genus: as for its being the highest, because no type exists above it; as for its being an intermediate genus...
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage from al-'Attar's commentary expands on the hierarchy of genera and species by providing a fresh, detailed example from the plant kingdom. The commentator criticizes the overuse of standard examples (animal, human, body) and offers a more concrete illustration. Starting with the genus 'plant', he lists many species like wheat, maize, etc., each with further subspecies. He then analyzes 'Indian wheat' as both a real species (true species) because it has individuals under it, and a relative species because it falls under 'absolute wheat'. This leads to a classification: specific types like Indian wheat are lowest species (naw' sāfil); absolute wheat is a proximate (lowest) genus (jins qarīb sāfil) and also an intermediate species because it falls under 'plant', which itself is both a species and genus under 'growing body', and so on up to 'absolute body', which is the highest species (a'lā al-anwā') and an intermediate genus (jins mutawassiṭ) because it has species below it but nothing above it in this chain. The example concretely shows how one entity can be simultaneously a genus and species depending on perspective.

In the hierarchy of universals, each term (except extremes) is both a genus for what is below and a species for what is above.

Understanding the Highest Genus: Substance as Genus of Genera
Author defines ascending genera and identifies جوهر as genus of genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the highest genus in the category of substance and explain why broader concepts like 'thing' are not true genera but accidental predicates.

Core Idea
Substance is the highest genus because broader concepts are understood through it, not the other way around.

In this passage, the author establishes a hierarchical order of genera, culminating in substance as the genus of genera. This is a foundational concept in Islamic logic for understanding how definitions are structured. You will learn to trace the ascending chain and analyze a key argument about why universal concepts like 'existent' cannot be true genera for substance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ascending chain of genera
The passage mentions 'above it is a genus, which is the growing body, and above it is body, and above it is substance.' Note the progression from the original term (likely a species) up to substance. This shows how genera become more general.
The original term is not specified but is implied from context.
2
Locate the main conclusion
The phrase 'فالجوهر جنس الأجناس' (so substance is the genus of genera) is the core claim. Underline it in the Arabic.
3
Understand the objection
The objector asks: How can substance be the highest genus when there are concepts like 'existent' or 'thing' that seem broader? This challenges the claim because those concepts include substance.
The objection is based on apparent generality.
4
Analyze the response
The author argues that those broader concepts are not true genera because (a) they are understood without substance, and (b) if they were genera, understanding substance would depend on them, but the opposite is true. Therefore, they are general accidents (عرض عام), not genera.
This reasoning hinges on the definition of genus as something that answers 'what is it?' and must be essential.
5
Apply the test of essential predication
Test whether 'existent' could be a genus: ask 'What is substance?' If you answer 'existent', does that capture its essence? No, because 'existent' applies to many things that are not substances (accidents). Hence it is an accidental predicate.
This test helps identify true genera in any definitional hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات فوقه جنس، هو الجسمُ النَّامي، وفوقه الجسم، وفوقه الجوهر، فالجوهر جنسُ الأجناس . (وَ) كما أنَّ الأجناس قد تترتَّبُ مُتصاعدة، كذلك (الأَنْوَاعُ) الإضافيَّةُ الدسوقي فما يكونُ أعمَّ مِنَ الكلِّ؛ يكون جنس الأجناس، وما يكون أخص الكلِّ، وهو ما كانَ تحتها ؛ يُسمَّى بالجنس السافل . (قَوْلُهُ: فَالْجَوْهَرُ جِنْسُ الْأَجْنَاسِ لا يُقالُ: كيف يكون كذلك مع كونه تحت شيء، ومذكور وموجود؟ ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : مَا ذكر لا يصلح أن يكون جنساً عالياً للجوهر؛ لفهمه دونَه، ولو كانَ جنساً له ؛ لتوقف فهمه على فَهُم مَا ذكرَ ؛ ضرورةً توقُفِ فَهُم المركّب على فَهُم أجزائه، وحينَئِذٍ فَمَا ذكرَ عرضُ عام للجوهر .
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. Above it is a genus, which is the growing body, and above it is body, and above it is substance, so substance is the genus of genera. (And) just as genera may be arranged ascendingly, likewise relative species... al-Dasuqi: So what is more general than everything is the genus of genera, and what is more specific than everything, which is what is under it, is called the lowest genus. (His saying: So substance is the genus of genera) It is not said: How can it be so while it is under something, namely the mentioned and existent? Because we say: What was mentioned cannot be a higher genus for substance, because it is understood without it, and if it were a genus for it, its understanding would depend on understanding what was mentioned, necessarily because understanding the composite depends on understanding its parts. Therefore, what was mentioned is a general accident for substance.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage defines the ascending order of genera and identifies the highest genus (جنس الأجناس). The author begins by stating that above كل (the term under discussion) there is a genus: the growing body (الجسم النامي), above that is body (الجسم), and above that is substance (الجوهر). Thus, substance is the genus of genera. Then al-Dasuqi's commentary clarifies that what is most general is the genus of genera, and what is most specific is the lowest genus. The objection asks how substance can be the genus of genera when it falls under something else (like 'existent' or 'thing'). The response explains that those higher concepts cannot be true genera for substance because understanding them requires understanding substance? Actually, the argument is that those concepts (e.g., 'thing') are understood without substance, so they are not genera; rather, they are general accidents (عرض عام) for substance. This preserves substance as the highest genus in the category of substance.

جنس الأجناس
الجوهر
الجسم النامي
الجسم
عرض عام

Key Takeaway
The highest genus is determined not by mere breadth but by whether it is predicated essentially in response to 'what is it?' without depending on a broader essential concept.

Descending Species and Ascending Genera
Author explains directional arrangement of species and genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the descending order of species from the ascending order of genera and identify the highest genus in a given hierarchy.

Core Idea
Species descend into specificity; genera ascend to generality.

In this passage, the author clarifies the directional logic behind classifying things: we move downward for species (more specific) and upward for genera (more general). This foundational principle helps us understand the structure of definitions and the ultimate category 'genus of genera'. Let's unpack this step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting thing
Begin with any concrete entity, for example 'human'. According to the passage, we first suppose a thing and then consider its species (which is more specific).
The starting thing is the subject of classification.
2
Trace the descending chain of species
For that species, we can then find a further species under it, continuing downward. Each step moves to a more specific category. The lowest in the chain is called 'type of types'.
Species are arranged 'on the way of descent' (على سبيل التنازل).
3
Contrast with ascending genera
Now for the same starting thing, consider its genus (more general). Then find a genus above that, continuing upward. Each step moves to a more general category. The highest is called 'genus of genera'.
Genera are arranged 'on the way of ascent' (على سبيل التصاعد).
4
Name the extremes
The lowest species in a chain is 'نوع الأنواع' (type of types); the highest genus is 'جنس الأجناس' (genus of genera). Recognize these as limit points in the hierarchy.
These terms are used to denote the endpoints of classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التَّصورات وإنَّما اعتُبِرَتِ الأنواع بحسَب التَّنازُلِ؛ لأنّا إذا فرضْنَا شيئاً وفرضْنَا نوعه يكون ذلكَ النَّوعُ تحتَهُ، ثمَّ إذا فرضنا لذلك النوع نوعاً آخر؛ يكون تحت ذلك النوع. فلهذا كان ترتُبُ الأنواع على سبيل التنازل، ويسمَّى السَّافلُ منها نوع الأنواع، أما إذا فرضنا شيئاً، وفرضنا له جنساً؛ يكون جنسه فوقه، ثمَّ إذا فرضنا له جنساً، يكون فوق ذلك الجنس، وهَلُمَّ جرا . فَلِهذا كان ترتُبُ الأجناس على سبيل التصاعد، ويسمى العالي منها جنس الأجناس.
Chapter: On the principles of conceptions. Species are considered only according to descent; because if we suppose a thing and suppose its species, that species is under it, then if we suppose for that species another species, it is under that species. Thus the arrangement of species is in descending order, and the lowest of them is called the type of types. As for if we suppose a thing and suppose for it a genus, its genus is above it, then if we suppose for it a genus, it is above that genus, and so on. Thus the arrangement of genera is in ascending order, and the highest of them is called the genus of genera.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section from a chapter on the principles of conceptions (mabadi' al-tasawwurat). The author explains the logic behind classifying species and genera in hierarchical order. For species, we start with a given thing and identify its species, which is more specific and thus 'under' it. Then we can identify a species of that species, further down. Therefore, the arrangement of species is descending (tanazzul), and the lowest is 'type of types'. Conversely, for genera, we start with a thing and identify its genus (more general), then a genus of that genus, moving upward. Thus genera ascend (tasā'ud), and the highest is 'genus of genera' (jins al-ajnās). This mirrors the example from earlier: human is the lowest species, while substance would be the highest genus. The reasoning is based on the relative specificity and generality.

Key Terms
جنس الأجناس
مبادئ التصورات
التنازل
التصاعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) In ascending order, with the highest called 'genus of genera'.
C) In no particular order.
D) In a circular arrangement.

Key Takeaway
Genera ascend in generality, culminating in the genus of genera; species descend in specificity, culminating in the type of types.

Identifying the Complete Common Element Between Species
Author defines the complete common element and illustrates it with man and horse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the complete common element (tamam al-mushtarak) between two species and distinguish it from the differentia.

Core Idea
The complete common element is the most specific shared attribute, beyond which no further commonality exists.

In this lesson, we dissect the concept of 'tamam al-mushtarak' as explained in the commentary on the Sullam. Using the example of man and horse, you will learn how to determine the exact level at which two species share an attribute, which is crucial for correctly identifying the differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two species under comparison
In the passage, the species are man (insān) and horse (faras). These are the two entities whose commonalities are examined.
Always start by naming the species.
2
List the shared attributes from broad to specific
The author lists: substance (jawhar), body (jism), growing body (jism nāmī), sentient growing body (jism nāmī hassās), and animal (hayawān). Each step is more specific than the previous.
This hierarchy is known as the 'tree of Porphyry' in Islamic logic, but here it is used to find the common element.
3
Determine the level beyond which sharing stops
Man and horse share in animal, but there is no shared attribute more specific than animal that both possess. Thus, animal is the complete common element.
If they shared a further attribute, that would be the complete common element instead.
4
Contrast with the differentia
The differentia, like rationality (nāṭiq) for man, is not shared at all or not completely. It distinguishes the species from others within the genus.
The differentia is what remains after removing the complete common element.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات. بخلافِ الجنسِ كالحيوانِ مثلاً، فإنَّه تمام المشترك بين الإنسانِ والفرس؛ إذْ لَا جزء مشترك بينهما. بيانُهُ: أَنَّ الإنسان والفرسَ مُشتركانِ فيما هو أخصُّ من الجوهر؛ وهو جسم، ومُشتركان أيضاً فيما هو أخصُّ مِنَ الجسم؛ وهو الجسمُ النَّامي، ومُشتركان أيضاً فيما هو أخصُّ مِنَ الجسمِ النامي، وهو جسم نام حسّاس، ومُشتركان أيضاً فيما هو أخصُّ مِنَ الجسمِ النَّامي الحسّاس؛ وهو الحيوان ولا يتأتى الاشتراك فيما هو أخصُّ مِن حيوان، فظهر من هذا أنَّ الحيوانَ تمام المشترك بين الإنسان والفرس. (قَوْلُهُ : إِذْ لَا جُزْءَ) علَّةٌ لِكَونِ الحيوانِ تمام المشترك بين الإنسان والفرس؛ أي: لأَنَّهُ لا جزء للماهِيَّةِ مُشترك؛ أي: وقعَ اشتراكُهُمَا فِيهِ. إلَّا وهو نفس الحيوان أو جزؤه، وإنَّما كان الجزء الذي ليس تمام المشترك فصلاً؛ لأنَّه إذا لم يكن تمام المشترك بين الماهية ونوع آخر، فإما أن لا يكون مشتركاً أصلاً بين الماهية ونوع ما. وحينئذ يميز الماهية عن جميع ما عداها، فيكون فصلاً مطلقاً، (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ جُزْؤُهُ) أي كـ،جسم ونامي وحساس؛ أي: ولا يتأتى اشتراكهما فيما هو أخص من حيوان، وحينئذ؛ فالحيوان تمام المشترك بينهما. (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الْجُزْءُ الَّذِي لَيْسَ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ) أي: بين الماهية ونوع آخر. (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهُ) أي: الجزء. (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُوْنَ) أي: ذلك الجزء مُشتركاً أصلاً؛ أي: كَناطق، فإِنَّهُ جزء لماهية الإنسان، وليس فيه اشتراك بين الإنسان وغيره. (قَوْلُهُ : عَنْ جَمِيعِ مَا عَدَاهَا) أي: مِمَّا شاركَهَا في الوجود، أو شاركها في الجنس، وذلك لأنَّ فصلَ الشَّيء إنْ كانَ مُختصا بجنسِهِ؛ كَانَ مُميَّزاً لهُ عَمَّا شارَكَهُ في الوجودِ وإِنْ كانَ غيرَ مُختص بجنسِهِ؛ كانَ مُميّزاً لهُ عَمَّا شَارِكَهُ في جنسِهِ، فالنُّطقُ إنْ كانَ مُختصاً بالحيوان؛ كانَ مُميّزاً للإنسانِ عمَّا شاركه في الوجود، وإن كان غير مُختص بالحيوان؛ لأنَّهُ يُقالُ على الملائكة؛ كانَ مُميّزاً لِلإِنسانٍ عَمَّا شاركَهُ في الحيوان فقط، لا عن كلِّ مَا شاركه في الوجود. (قَوْلُهُ : فَصْلَاً مُطْلَقَاً) أي: مُميَّزاً تمييزاً مُطلقاً؛ أي: غير مُقيَّد بالتمييز عن ماهيَّةٍ دونَ أُخرى، بل هو مُميَّز عن جميع الماهيَّاتِ :: ناطق، ويُسمَّى الفصل القريب.
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. Contrary to genus like animal for example, for it is the complete common element between man and horse; since there is no common part between them. Its explanation: That man and horse share in what is more specific than substance; it is body, and they also share in what is more specific than body; it is growing body, and they also share in what is more specific than growing body; it is sentient growing body, and they also share in what is more specific than sentient growing body; it is animal, and sharing does not occur in what is more specific than animal. So it appears from this that animal is the complete common element between man and horse. (His saying: 'since there is no part') is the reason for animal being the complete common element between man and horse; meaning: because there is no part of the quiddity that is shared; i.e., their sharing occurs in it. Except that it is animal itself or a part of it, and the part that is not the complete common element is only a differentia; because if it is not the complete common element between the quiddity and another species, then either it is not shared at all between the quiddity and some species. And then it distinguishes the quiddity from everything other than it, so it becomes an absolute differentia. (His saying: 'or a part of it') i.e., like body, growing, and sentient; meaning: their sharing does not occur in what is more specific than animal, and then animal is the complete common element between them. (His saying: 'and only the part that is not the complete common element') i.e., between the quiddity and another species. (His saying: 'because it') i.e., the part. (His saying: 'then either it is not') i.e., that part is not shared at all; i.e., like rational, for it is a part of the quiddity of man, and there is no sharing in it between man and other than him. (His saying: 'from everything other than it') i.e., from what shares with it in existence or shares with it in genus. That is because the differentia of a thing, if it is specific to its genus, it distinguishes it from what shares with it in existence; and if it is not specific to its genus, it distinguishes it from what shares with it in genus only. So rationality, if it is specific to animal, it distinguishes man from what shares with him in existence; and if it is not specific to animal, because it is said of angels, it distinguishes man from what shares with him in animal only, not from everything that shares with him in existence. (His saying: 'an absolute differentia') i.e., a distinguishing absolutely; i.e., not restricted to distinguishing from one quiddity without another, but it distinguishes from all quiddities: rational, and it is called the proximate differentia.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage from the commentary (sharh) on al-Akhdari's Sullam defines the differentia (fasl) and clarifies its relationship to the genus. The author begins by stating the chapter heading 'On the principles of concepts', then immediately contrasts differentia with genus using the example of 'animal' as the complete common element (tamam al-mushtarak) between man and horse. The commentary explains that man and horse share in a hierarchy of more specific attributes: substance, body, growing body, sentient growing body, and finally animal. No sharing occurs beyond animal, so animal is the complete common element. The differentia, by contrast, is not the complete common element; it is a part of the quiddity that is either not shared at all (like 'rational' for man) or is shared only in a restricted sense. The passage then discusses the two cases: if the part is not shared at all, it becomes an absolute differentia (fasl mutlaq) that distinguishes the quiddity from everything else. If the part is shared but not completely, it may still distinguish from some species. The example of 'rational' (natiq) is given: if it is specific to animal, it distinguishes man from all other existents; if it is also applied to angels, it only distinguishes man from other animals. The final point identifies the absolute differentia as the proximate differentia (fasl qarib), which is the last differentia in the definition.

Key Takeaway
The complete common element is the most specific shared attribute that two species possess, and it serves as the genus in a definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Differentia: What It Is and Its Two Types
Author explains the condition of differentia and its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the differentia (fasl) and distinguish between an absolute differentia (fasl mutlaq) and a relative differentia.

Core Idea
The differentia is an essential part that is not the complete common element, and it can be absolute (distinguishing from all) or relative (distinguishing only from some).

Having understood the complete common element, we now turn to the differentia. The passage explains that the differentia is a part of the quiddity that is not the complete common element. It then distinguishes two cases: when the part is not shared at all (absolute differentia) and when it is shared but not completely (relative differentia).

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of differentia
The author states: 'the part that is not the complete common element is only a differentia.' So the differentia is a part of the quiddity that is not shared in its entirety.
Differentia is one of the five universals.
2
Identify the two scenarios
If the part is not shared at all (like rational for man), it becomes an absolute differentia. If it is shared but not completely (like some attribute shared with another species but not all), it is a relative differentia.
Absolute differentia distinguishes from everything; relative only from some.
3
Understand the example of rational (nāṭiq)
Rational is a part of the quiddity of man. If rationality is specific to animals, it distinguishes man from all non-animals. If it is also said of angels, then it distinguishes man only from other animals, not from angels.
Thus, the scope of distinction depends on whether the differentia is exclusive to the genus.
4
Recognize the absolute differentia as the proximate differentia
The passage concludes that the absolute differentia is called the proximate differentia (al-fasl al-qarīb), which is the last differentia in a definition.
For example, in the definition of man as 'rational animal', rational is the proximate differentia.

Definition of Differentia (Fasl) and Its Types
Authors define fasl and distinguish absolute differentia from partial differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given part of an essence as an absolute differentia, a partial differentia, or not a differentia, by applying the condition of complete commonality.

Core Idea
A differentia must not be the complete commonality; if unshared, it is absolute; if shared partially, it is remote.

In this passage from Tadhheeb al-Mantiq, the authors explain what makes a part of an essence a differentia. The key is that it must not be the entire commonality between two species. Depending on whether it is shared at all, it becomes an absolute differentia (like 'rational' for humans) or a partial differentia (like 'growing' shared with plants). This lesson breaks down the logical condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the part under consideration
Locate the part of the essence being tested as a potential differentia. For example, 'rational' in the definition of human.
2
Check if it is the complete commonality
Determine whether this part represents the entire set of essential attributes shared with another species. If yes, it is a genus, not a differentia.
The complete commonality is the genus; e.g., 'animal' is the complete commonality between human and horse.
3
Determine if it is shared at all
If not the complete commonality, see whether the part is shared with any other species. If it is not shared at all with any other species, it is an absolute differentia.
Example: 'rational' is not shared with any other animal.
4
Classify as absolute or partial
If it is shared with some species but not completely, it is a partial (remote) differentia. If not shared at all, it is absolute.
Example: 'growing' is shared with plants but not completely, so it is a partial differentia for humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنَّما كان الجزء الذي ليس تمام المشترك فصلاً؛ لأنَّه إذا لم يكن تمام المشترك بين الماهية ونوع آخر، فإما أن لا يكون مشتركاً أصلاً بين الماهية ونوع ما. وحينئذ يميز الماهية عن جميع ما عداها، فيكون فصلاً مطلقاً. أو كان مُشتركاً بين الماهية ونوع آخَرَ كالنَّامي. لكن لا يكون تمام المشترك، فهذا الجزء لا يمكن أن يكون مشتركاً بين الماهية وجميع ما عداها.
Indeed, the part that is not the complete commonality is a differentia, because if it is not the complete commonality between a quiddity and another species, then either it is not at all common between the quiddity and any species. In that case, it distinguishes the quiddity from all others, thus it is an absolute differentia. Or it is common between the quiddity and another species, like 'growing' (al-nami). But it is not the complete commonality, so this part cannot be common between the quiddity and everything else.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage defines the differentia (fasl), which is a part of the essence that distinguishes a species from others. The key condition is that the part must not be the complete commonality (tamam al-mushtarak) between two species. If it is not the complete commonality, two cases arise: first, the part is not shared at all with any other species—like natiq (rational) for humans—then it distinguishes the species from absolutely everything else; this is called the absolute differentia (fasl mutlaq) or the proximate differentia. Second, the part is shared with another species but is not the complete commonality—like 'growing' (nami) which is shared between humans and plants, but growing is not the complete commonality because humans also have other differentiae. In this case, the part distinguishes the species from some others but not all; it is a remote differentia. The passage focuses on the logical definition, showing how the part functions as a differentia based on its relation to other species. The commentators elaborate with examples: natiq for absolute differentia, and namii/hassas for partial differentia. The discussion is foundational for understanding how definitions are constructed in Islamic logic.

الفصل
تمام المشترك
الفصل المطلق
الفصل البعيد

A) Absolute differentia (fasl mutlaq)

C) Remote differentia (fasl ba'eed)

Key Takeaway
The differentia must not be the complete commonality; if it is not shared at all, it is absolute; if shared but not completely, it is partial.

The Differentia: Distinguishing in General
Refining the definition of differentia to include remote differentiae

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a universal qualifies as a differentia (fasl) by applying the condition that it distinguishes the essence in general, not necessarily from all other essences.

Core Idea
A differentia need only distinguish from some essences, especially simple ones.

The classical definition of differentia (fasl) as 'that which distinguishes the essence' can be misinterpreted as requiring exclusion of all other essences. This passage clarifies that a differentia may distinguish only 'in general' (fi al-jumla), meaning from at least some essences. This refinement is crucial for understanding remote differentiae and for correctly applying the five universals in Islamic disciplines like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Essence and Its Parts
Start with a given mahiyya (essence). Determine its parts (ajza') if any. Not all essences have parts; simple essences (basita) have no parts.
This step is foundational before evaluating whether a part can serve as differentia.
2
Check Complete Commonality
If a part is the complete commonality (tamam al-mushtarak) between the essence and all other distinct types, it is a genus (jins), not a differentia. If it is not fully common, it may be a differentia.
A part that is shared with some but not all is a candidate for differentia.
3
Apply the 'In General' Criterion
According to the passage, a differentia suffices if it distinguishes the essence from at least some essences, e.g., simple essences. Even if the part is shared with some essences, it can still be a differentia because it distinguishes from those that lack that part.
This is the key refinement: the differentia need not exclude everything; partial distinction is enough.
4
Test Against Simple Essences
Simple essences have no parts, so any part of a composite essence will necessarily distinguish it from all simple essences. This proves that a part not fully common can function as a differentia.
The existence of simple essences is argued via analysis: every composite must resolve into simples.

إذْ مِنَ المَاهِيَّاتِ مَا تَكُونُ بَسِيْطَةً لَا جُزْءَ لَهَا . وَلِذلكَ قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : وَعَلَى هَذَا ؛ فَالْفَصْلُ هُوَ الَّذِي يُمَيِّزُ الْمَاهِيَةَ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ، وَلَا يَكُونُ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ غَيْرِهَا، وَلَا جُزْءَهُ، وَلَا يَرِدُ الْجِنْسُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ تَمَامُ الْمُشْتَرَكِ، وَلَا مِثْلُ الْجَوْهَرِ النَّاطِقِ ؛ لِأَنَّ الْكَلَامَ فِي الْأَجْزَاءِ الْمُفْرَدَةِ . (قَوْلُهُ : إِذْ مِنَ الْمَاهِيَّاتِ مَا تَكُونُ بَسِيْطَةٌ) الْمُرَادُ أَنَّ ذَلِكَ الْأَمْرَ لَازِمٌ عَلَى كُلِّ حَالٍ فَالتَّمْيِيزُ عَنِ الْمَاهِيَةِ الْبَسِيْطَةِ لَا يَتَخَلَّفُ، وَلَيْسَ الْمُرَادُ أَنَّهُ لَا يَكُونُ مُمَيَّزاً إِلَّا وَالْحَاصِلُ أَنَّ هَذَا الْفَصْلَ مُمَيَّزُ عَمَّا شَارَكَهُ فِي الْوُجُودِ كَالْمَاهِيَاتِ الْبَسِيْطَةِ الَّتِي لَا جِنْسَ لَهَا قَطْعاً، ثُمَّ قَدْ يَكُونُ مُمَيَّزاً عَمَّا شَارَكَهُ فِي الْجِنْسِ إِنْ كَانَ هُنَاكَ إِنْ ذَاكَ، وَوُجُودُ الْمَاهِيَةِ الْبَسِيْطَةِ مُحَقَّقٌ، فَإِنَّ الْمُرَكَّبَ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَنْتَهِيَ بِالتَّحْلِيلِ إِلَى الْبَسِيْطِ؛ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ كَثْرَةٍ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ؛ لَا بُدَّ لَهَا مِنَ الْوَاحِدِ لِأَنَّهُ مَبْدَؤُهَا، فَلَوِ انْتَفَى الْوَاحِدُ؛ انْتَفَى الْكَثِيرُ لِانْتِفَاءِ مَبْدَئِهِ وَلِذَا قَالَ فِي التَّجْرِيدِ: وُجُودُ الْبَسِيْطِ وَالْمُرَكَّبِ مَعْلُومٌ بِالضَّرُورَةِ .ا.هـ. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ مَا ذَكَرَهُ الشَّارِحُ هُنَا مَأْخُوذٌ مِنْ كَلَامِ السَّيِّدِ فِي حَاشِيَةِ الْقُطْبِ، فَإِنَّهُ قُدِّسَ سِرُّهُ بَعْدَ أَنْ نَاقَشَ دَلِيلَ الشَّارِحِ الْقُطْبِ وَاعْتَرَضَهُ؛ قَالَ : وَهَذَا الْاِعْتِرَاضُ مِمَّا لَا مَدْفَعَ لَهُ إِلَّا إِذَا ثَبَتَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ لِمَاهِيَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ جِنْسَانِ لَا يَكُونُ أَحَدُهُمَا جُزْءاً لِلْآخَرِ، وَلَمْ يَثْبُتْ هَهُنَا؛ أَيْ: فِي حَصْرِ جُزْءِ الْمَاهِيَةِ فِي الْجِنْسِ وَالْفَصْلِ، فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَرْكِ هَذَا الدَّلِيلِ وَالتَّمَسُّكِ بِدَلِيلٍ آخَرَ وَهُوَ أَنْ يُقَالَ: جُزْءُ الْمَاهِيَةِ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مَا مِنَ الْأَنْوَاعِ الْمُبَايِنَةِ لَهَا؛ فَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكاً أَصْلًا بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعٍ مُبَايِنٍ لَهَا؛ فَيَكُونُ فَصْلاً لِلْمَاهِيَّةِ مُمَيِّزاً لَهَا عَنْ جَمِيعِ الْمُبَايِنَاتِ، وَأَمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكاً بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ غَيْرِهَا؛ لَكِنْ لَا يَكُونُ تَمَامَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهَا، فَهَذَا الْجُزْءُ لَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُشْتَرَكاً بَيْنَ الْمَاهِيَةِ وَبَيْنَ جَمِيعِ مَا عَدَاهَا؛ إِذْ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ الْمَاهِيَاتِ مَاهِيَّةٌ بَسِيْطَةٌ لَا جُزْءَ لَهَا، فَيَكُونُ هَذَا الْجُزْءُ مُمَيِّزاً لِلْمَاهِيَّةِ عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّاتِ الَّتِي لَا تُشَارِكُهَا فِي هَذَا الْجُزْءِ؛ فَيَكُونُ فَصْلاً لِلْمَاهِيَةِ ا.هـ. فَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ ذَلِكَ الْجُزْءُ مُمَيِّزاً لِلْمَاهِيَةِ عَنِ الْمَاهِيَّاتِ الْبَسِيْطَةِ، فَيَكُونُ هَذَا الْجُزْءُ فَصْلاً لِلْمَاهِيَّةِ، لِأَنَّا لَا نَعْنِي بِالْفَصْلِ إِلَّا مَا يُمَيِّزُ الْمَاهِيَةَ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ . (وَعَرَّفُوا الْفَصْلَ بِأَنَّهُ) : (هُوَ الْمَقُولُ) . الدَّسُوقِيُّ: قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّا لَا نَعْنِي عَلَّةٌ لِقَوْلِهِ : فَيَكُونُ هَذَا الْجُزْءُ فَصْلاً. قَوْلُهُ: فِي الْجُمْلَةِ أَيْ: مَا يُمَيِّزُهَا عَنْ بَعْضِ الْمَاهِيَّاتِ؛ لَا عَنْ كُلِّهَا، وَهَذَا هُوَ الْفَصْلُ الْبَعِيدُ، وَالْأَوْلَى أَنْ يَقُولَ: لِأَنَّا لَا نَعْنِي بِالْفَصْلِ إِلَّا مَا يُمَيِّزُ الْمَاهِيَةَ، وَلَوْ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ؛ لِيَشْمَلَ الْفَصْلَ الْقَرِيبَ كَالْبَعِيدِ. قَوْلُهُ: وَعَرَّفُوا الْفَصْلَ إِلَخ لَمْ يُقَدَّرْ مِثْلُ ذَلِكَ فِي كَلَامِ الْمُؤَلِّفِ السَّابِقِ وَاللَّاحِقِ، وَلَعَلَّهُ لَا دَاعِيَ لِذَلِكَ. قَوْلُهُ: الْمَقُولُ أَيْ: الْمَحْمُولُ بِالْفِعْلِ وَبِالْإِمْكَانِ. الْعَطَّارُ الْمُصَنِّفُ قَوْلُهُ: فِي الْجُمْلَةِ أَيْ: عَنْ بَعْضِ الْمَاهِيَّاتِ؛ لَا عَنْ جَمِيعِهَا، وَهُوَ الْفَصْلُ الْبَعِيدُ. قَوْلُهُ: وَعَرَّفُوا الْفَصْلَ لَمْ يُقَدَّرْ مِثْلُ ذَلِكَ فِي كَلَامِ الْمُصَنِّفِ السَّابِقِ وَاللَّاحِقِ، وَلَعَلَّهُ لَا دَاعِيَ لِذَلِكَ، وَاعْتَذَرَ عَنْهُ الْبَعْضُ بِأَنَّهُ لِلْإِيمَاءِ إِلَى أَنَّ الْمُصَنِّفَ لَمْ يَتَصَرَّفْ فِي تَعْرِيفِهِمْ لِلْفَصْلِ بِمَا يُخْرِجُهُ عَنْ نِسْبَتِهِ لَهُمْ بِخِلَافِ تِلْكَ التَّعَارِيفِ، وَهُوَ اعْتِذَارٌ بَارِدٌ، كَيْفَ وَقَدْ تَصَرَّفَ الْمُصَنِّفُ فِي عِبَارَةِ الْأَصْلِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الْفَصْلِ . قَوْلُهُ: وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ وَقَعَ فِي الرِّسَالَةِ تَعْرِيفُهُ بِأَنَّهُ كُلِّيٌّ يَحْمِلُ إِلَخ، فَقَالَ فِي شَرْحِهِ: إِنَّمَا قَالَ: يَحْمِلُ دُونَ يُقَالُ كَمَا فِي سَائِرِ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ؛ لِأَنَّهُمْ ذَكَرُوا أَنَّ الْفَصْلَ عِلَّةٌ لِحِصَّةِ النَّوْعِ مِنَ الْجِنْسِ، فَكَانَ مَظِنَّةً أَنْ يُتَوَهَّمَ أَنَّ الْفَصْلَ لَا يَحْمِلُ عَلَيْهِ؛ لِامْتِنَاعِ حَمْلِ الْعِلَّةِ عَلَى الْمَعْلُولِ، فَصَرَّحَ بِلَفْظِ الْحَمْلِ إِزَالَةً لِهَذَا الْوَهْمِ .ا.هـ. وَكَأَنَّهُ لَمْ يَلْتَفِتْ لِذَلِكَ هُنَا، إِمَّا لِأَنَّ الْقَوْلَ وَالْحَمْلَ بِمَعْنَى وَاحِدٍ، أَوْ لِأَنَّ تِلْكَ النُّكْتَةَ ضَعِيفَةٌ؛ تَأَمَّلْ، وَمَعْنَى كَوْنِ الْفَصْلِ عِلَّةً لِحِصَّةِ النَّوْعِ؛ هُوَ مَا نَقَلَهُ شَارِحُ الْمَطَالِعِ عَنِ الشَّيْخِ فِي الشِّفَاءِ أَنَّ الْفَصْلَ إِذَا اقْتَرَنَ بِطَبِيعَةِ الْجِنْسِ؛ أَفْرَزَهَا وَعَيَّنَهَا وَقَوَّمَهَا نَوْعاً، وَبَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَلْزَمُهَا مَا يَلْزَمُهَا، وَيَعْرِضُهَا مَا يَعْرِضُهَا، فَإِنَّهَا وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَعَ الْفَصْلِ؛ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ يُلْقَى أَوَّلاً طَبِيعَةَ الْجِنْسِ وَيُحَصِّلُهَا، وَتِلْكَ الْعَوَارِضُ إِنَّمَا ...
Because among essences there are simple ones that have no part. And therefore some said: According to this, the differentia is that which distinguishes the essence in general, and it is not the complete commonality between it and other things, nor a part of it, and it is not genus because that is the complete commonality, and not like 'rational animal' because the discussion is about single parts. (His statement: 'Because among essences there are simple ones') The intended meaning is that that matter is necessary in any case, so distinguishing from the simple essence does not fail; it is not intended that it is not distinguishing except... and the result is that this differentia distinguishes from what shares existence with it, like simple essences that have no genus at all; then it may distinguish from what shares genus with it if there is that. And the existence of simple essence is realized, because the composite must by analysis end at the simple, because every multiplicity, even if infinite, must have a single [principle] because it is its origin; so if the single is negated, the many is negated due to the negation of its origin. That is why he said in al-Tajrid: the existence of the simple and composite is known necessarily. End. And know that what the commentator mentioned here is taken from the words of al-Sayyid in the margin of al-Qutb, for he, after discussing and objecting to the al-Qutb commentator's proof, said: This objection is unavoidable unless it is established that it is not permissible for one essence to have two genera not being one a part of the other, and it is not established here, i.e., in the enumeration of the part of essence into genus and differentia. So this proof must be abandoned and another proof adopted, which is to say: a part of essence, if it is not the complete commonality between it and some type of types that are distinct from it, then either it is not common at all between it and a distinct type, in which case it is a differentia for the essence distinguishing it from all distinct things; or it is common between it and other things, but not the complete commonality between them, then this part cannot be common between the essence and all others, because among the essences there is a simple essence without parts, so this part distinguishes the essence from essences that do not share this part, so it becomes a differentia for the essence. End. So then that part distinguishes the essence from simple essences, so this part becomes a differentia for the essence, because we mean by differentia nothing but what distinguishes the essence in general. (And they defined the differentia as): (it is the predicate). Al-Dasuqi: His statement 'because we do not mean' is a cause for his statement 'so this part becomes a differentia'. His statement 'in general' means: what distinguishes it from some essences, not from all of them, and this is the remote differentia. It would be better if he said: because we mean by differentia nothing but what distinguishes the essence, even if in general, to include the proximate differentia like the remote. His statement 'and they defined the differentia etc.' was not estimated in the author's preceding and subsequent speech, and perhaps there is no need for it. His statement 'the predicate' means: what is predicated actually and potentially. Al-‘Attar al-Musannif: His statement 'in general' means: from some essences, not from all of them, and it is the remote differentia. His statement 'and they defined the differentia' was not estimated in the author's preceding and subsequent speech, and perhaps there is no need for it, and some excused it by indicating that the author did not alter their definition of differentia in a way that removes attribution to them, unlike those definitions, and this is a feeble excuse. How could he not have altered the original wording in defining differentia? His statement 'and it is the predicate' occurs in the Risala as definition: 'it is a universal that is predicated' etc. He said in his commentary: he only said 'is predicated' instead of 'is said' as in the other universals, because they mentioned that the differentia is a cause for the particularization of the species from the genus, so it was a likely place for the misconception that the differentia is not predicated of it, due to the impossibility of predicating the cause of the effect. So he explicitly used the term 'predication' to remove this illusion. End. And it seems he did not pay attention to that here, either because 'saying' and 'predication' have the same meaning, or because that point is weak; reflect. And the meaning of the differentia being a cause for the particularization of the species is what the commentator of al-Matali‘ reported from the Shaykh in al-Shifa': that the differentia, when combined with the nature of the genus, isolates it, specifies it, and constitutes it as a species; and after that, what follows follows it and what occurs occurs to it. For although it is with the differentia, yet the nature of the genus is first considered and obtained, and those accidents only...
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of the differentia (fasl), one of the five universals. The discussion begins with a statement that simple essences (without parts) exist, and from this the commentator derives a key point: the differentia does not need to be the complete commonality between the essence and others; it suffices that it distinguishes the essence 'in general' (fi l-jumla). This means that a part of the essence that is not shared with all other essences can still serve as a differentia, because it at least distinguishes the essence from simple essences that lack that part. The commentator then provides a proof from the existence of simple essences: composites must resolve into simples, so the existence of simples is necessary. He quotes a proof from al-Tajrid. The argument then shifts to a refined proof from al-Sayyid al-Sharif, which avoids a problematic assumption about two genera for one essence. The refined proof states: if a part of the essence is not fully common with all distinct types, then either it is not common at all (making it a differentia) or it is common but not fully; in the latter case, it cannot be common with everything because simple essences lack that part, so it distinguishes from them. Thus the part is a differentia. The commentator then defines the differentia as 'that which is predicated' (al-maqul) and further distinguishes between proximate and remote differentia, noting that the definition 'in general' covers both. He also critiques the author's choice to include a separate definition, considering it unnecessary. The passage ends with a discussion of why the differentia is defined as 'predicated' rather than 'said', to avoid the illusion that the differentia, as a cause, cannot be predicated of its effect (the species). The commentator notes that this nuance is weak, and that 'saying' and 'predication' are essentially the same. Overall, the passage refines the classical definition of differentia, emphasizing that its function is to distinguish the essence, not necessarily from all other things, but at least from some (especially simple essences).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimal condition for a part of essence to be a differentia (fasl)?
A) It must be predicated of the species.
B) It must distinguish the essence from all other things.
C) It must distinguish the essence from simple essences that lack that part.
D) It must be the complete commonality between the essence and other types.

Key Takeaway
A differentia is not required to exclude all other essences; it suffices that it separates the essence from at least some, such as simple essences that lack that part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the essence of 'human being' (al-insan), analyze whether the universal 'rational' (al-natiq) qualifies as a differentia according to the 'in general' criterion. Identify which essences it distinguishes from and whether it satisfies the passage's condition.
?
In a kalam debate, a mutakallim claims that a valid differentia must exclude all other essences. Using the passage's proof, construct a counterexample showing that a differentia can be partial, such as 'walking' distinguishing humans from plants while being shared with animals.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
8 min
Understanding the Definition of al-Fasl (Differentia)
Author defines fasl and its two types, with commentary refining the definition's scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct definition of fasl and explain why the qualification 'only' with respect to the remote genus is necessary to avoid including near differentiae.

Core Idea
Fasl distinguishes a species from all partners in the remote genus – not just the proximate one.

The differentia (fasl) is one of the five universals and plays a key role in definition. In this passage, the matn defines fasl as that which distinguishes a species from its partners in the remote genus. However, the commentators al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar point out a crucial refinement: without the word 'only', the definition would also apply to near differentiae, which also happen to distinguish from remote genus partners. Understanding this nuance ensures precise usage of fasl in logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of fasl in the matn
Read the matn statement: 'ميَّزَ النَّوعَ عن مشارِكِهِ في الجنسِ البَعِيدِ'. This is the core definition: fasl distinguishes the species from its partner in the remote genus.
The remote genus (jins ba'id) is the highest genus in the hierarchy (e.g., 'growing body' for humans).
2
Identify the two types of fasl: muqawwim and muqassim
The matn states that fasl is either muqawwim (constitutive, essential part of the essence) or muqassim (divisive, distinguishing species under a genus but not necessarily constitutive).
Muqawwim is necessary for the species' existence, while muqassim is a property that distinguishes without being essential.
3
Analyze al-Dasuqi's refinement
Al-Dasuqi argues that the phrase 'in the remote genus' should be qualified with 'only' (فقط). Without it, the definition would also apply to near differentiae like 'rational' (natiq), because 'rational' distinguishes humans not only from other animals (partners in the proximate genus) but also from trees (partners in the remote genus). Thus 'only' restricts the definition to the remote genus, ensuring it covers all differentiae.
This shows the precision required in definitions: a small omission can make a definition too broad.
4
Apply the refined definition to examples
Test with 'sensitive' (hassas) as a differentia for humans: it distinguishes from trees (remote genus) as well as from animals? Actually 'sensitive' is shared with animals, so it is not a proper differentia. The commentators also explain that fasl must be attributed to a specific nature (mahiyya naw'iyya), not individuals like Zayd or sub-species.
This ensures the definition applies to the species itself, not to accidental instances.

(And when the differentia is attributed) to what it distinguishes; Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'in the remote genus' – it would be better to add 'only', so that the definition derived from the division does not apply to the near [genus]; for there is no near differentia except that it distinguishes from every partner in the remote genus. And our saying: 'when attributed to a specific nature' excludes the nature of Zayd and the sub-species, unless it is said that they are included in the specific nature.) Commentary
This passage defines the differentia (fasl) and its two types. The matn states that fasl distinguishes the species from its partners in the remote genus (jins ba'id). The commentator al-Dasuqi stresses that the phrase 'remote genus' must be qualified with 'only' to exclude the near genus, because even a near differentia like 'rational' distinguishes from partners in the remote genus (e.g., trees). Then he explains that the attribution of 'fasl' is to a specific nature (mahiyya naw'iyya), not to individuals like Zayd or sub-species, though they may be included. Finally, al-‘Attar echoes the need for 'only' to avoid the definition applying to near differentiae. This clarifies the precise scope of the definition.

The Three Relations of the Differentia (Fasl)
Author explains three relations of fasl using examples from Sharh al-Matali'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three logical relations of the differentia (to species, genus, and share) and apply them to definitions in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The differentia has three distinct logical functions: constitutive for species, divisive for genus, and efficient cause for the genus-share.

In the study of definitions (hadd), the differentia (fasl) plays a central role. But understanding its function requires seeing it from three perspectives. This lesson, drawn from Sharh al-Matali', unpacks these relations with concrete examples and shows how later commentators refined the concepts. By mastering these relations, you will be able to analyze any definition's logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three relations
In the source passage, locate the three relations of the differentia as listed: relation to the species, relation to the genus, and relation to the 'share' of the species from the genus.
The Arabic term for relation is 'nisba' (نسبة).
2
Examine the constitutive relation
The differentia is constitutive (muqawwim) for the species. For example, 'rational' is constitutive for 'human' – it enters into the essence of human.
Constitutive means it is a part of the essence, without which the species would not be what it is.
3
Examine the divisive relation
The differentia divides the genus into species. 'Rational' divides 'animal' into 'human' and 'non-rational animal' (like horse).
Divisive here is muqassim – it shows the differentia's role in classification.
4
Examine the efficient cause relation
The differentia is declared an efficient cause for the existence of the 'share' (hissa) of the genus in the species. For instance, 'rationality' brings about the specific animality in humans, while 'neighing' brings about the specific animality in horses.
This is transmitted from the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) by the Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi).
5
Apply to a new definition
Take the definition: 'Khamr (wine) is an intoxicating beverage.' Identify 'beverage' as genus and 'intoxicating' as differentia. Then explain the differentia's three relations: it is constitutive for khamr, divides beverage into khamr and other drinks, and is the efficient cause of the specific 'beverage-ness' in khamr.
Ensure you can articulate each relation clearly.

(قَوْلُهُ : فِي قِوَامِهِ) أي : في حقيقته، وقولُهُ: (وَجُزْءٌ لَهُ); عطفُ لازم على ملزوم. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ إِلَى شَيْءٍ أي نوع فالصِّنفُ والشَّخصُ وإِنْ ميَّزَهُمَا الفصل; لكنَّهُ ليسَ مُقوّماً بالنسبة إليهِمَا , بلْ لِلنَّوعَ الصَّادقِ عليهما، وفي شرح المطالع : يمتنع أنْ يكونَ لِكُلِّ فصل فصلِّ لِوُجوبِ الانتهاء إلى فصل لا جزء لَهُ، وإلا ; لتركَّبَتِ ; الماهيَّةُ مِنْ أجزاءً غيرِ مُتَناهِيةٍ وَهُوَ مُحالٌ، فإنْ قُلْتَ: يجب أنْ يكونَ لِكُلِّ فصل فصل; لأنَّ طبيعة الفصل صادقة على النَّوعِ وعلى نَفْسِهِ، فَيَكونُ مُشارِكاً لِلنَّوعِ في طبيعتِهِ، وهُوَ مُمتاز عنهُ لِعَدَم دُخولِ الجنَسِ فيهِ، وَمَا بِهِ الامتياز فصل; فَيَكُونُ لِلفَضْل فَصل، أُجيبُ : بأنَّ عدم دخول الجنس في ماهية الفصل ليسَ فَصلاً، وإِنَّما يكون فصلاً لو كانَ ذاتيا وليس كذلك , وإِلَّا ; لَكانَ ذاتيًّا لِلنَّوعِ وَهُوَ مُحالٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : فَهُوَ فَصْلٌ مُقَوِّمٌ هذا هُوَ الاسمُ لَا مُجرَّدَ المقوّم ; لأنَّ المقوِّمَ أعم مِنَ الفصل، لأنَّ كُلَّ جزءٍ لِلماهِيَّةِ مُقوِّمٌ لَهَا، فلا يكون ذلك الجزء قِسماً للفصل، بل هو قسيم له، قال في شرح المطالع : ليسَ كُلُّ جزء جنساً أو فصلاً، فإنَّ العشرةَ مُركَّبَةٌ مِنَ الآحادِ، والبيتَ مِنَ السَّقْفِ والجدرانِ الأربع; مَعَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنْ تِلْكَ الأجزاء ليس ولا فصل بل الجزء المحمولُ إِمَّا جنس أو فصل، فليسَ كُلُّ ماهيَّةٍ مُركَّبة يكون تركُبُهَا مِنَ الجنس والفصل لِجَوازِ تركُبِهَا مِنَ الأجزاء الغير المحمولة، وَلَا كُلُّ ماهيَّةٍ مُركَّبَةٍ مِنَ الأجزاء المحمولةِ كَذلِكَ; أي: مُركَّبَةٍ مِنَ الجنس والفصل بناء على الاحتمال المذكور ....هـ. يريدُ بِهِ تَركُبَ الماهِيَّةِ مِن أَمرَيْنِ مُتساوين. (His statement: 'And when the differentia is attributed... etc.) He said in Sharh al-Matali': The differentia has three relations: relation to the species, relation to the genus, and relation to the share of the species from the genus. As for its relation to the species: it is constitutive (muqawwim) for it, like the constitution of 'rational' for human. As for its relation to the genus: it is divisive (muqassim) for it, like the division of 'rational' for animal into human. As for its relation to the share: the Imam transmitted from the Shaykh that it is an efficient cause for its existence, for example, from animal in human a share, and likewise in horse and other; and the bringer-into-existence of the animality in human and rationality and of the animality in horse is the 'neighing' quality. End. 

i.e., to a thing that the differentia distinguishes that thing; so it is constitutive (muqawwim); i.e., it is a differentia constitutive for that thing, meaning it enters into its constitution (qiwam) and is a part of it. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'i.e., to a thing') meaning a species, and his statement: (the differentia distinguishes that thing), he indicated by that that the adjective is applied to other than whom it is for, so it was upon the author to reveal the pronoun by saying: 'what distinguishes it is it', and it may be said: it is in accordance with the Kufan school with consideration of brevity, or according to those who say: the revealing is only obligatory in other than the verb, and similarly what follows. (His statement: 'in its constitution') i.e., in its reality, and his statement: (and a part of it) is an apposition of necessary to necessary. Al-‘Attar: (His statement: 'i.e., to a thing' meaning a species, so the sub-species and the individual, even if the differentia distinguishes them, it is not constitutive with respect to them, but rather for the species that is true of them. And in Sharh al-Matali': It is impossible that every differentia have a differentia, due to the necessity of stopping at a differentia that has no part; otherwise, the essence would be composed of infinite parts, which is impossible. If you say: It is necessary that every differentia have a differentia, because the nature of differentia is true of the species and of itself; so it participates with the species in its nature, and it is distinguished from it by the absence of the genus in it, and that by which it is distinguished is a differentia, so the differentia has a differentia. The answer: The absence of the genus in the essence of the differentia is not a differentia, but it would be a differentia if it were essential, and it is not; otherwise, it would be essential for the species, which is impossible. (His statement: 'i.e., it is a constitutive differentia' this is the name, not merely the constitutive, because 'constitutive' is more general than 'differentia', because every part of the essence is constitutive for it, so that part is not a division of differentia, but rather it is co-divisive with it. He said in Sharh al-Matali': Not every part is a genus or a differentia, for ten is composed of units, and a house of a roof and four walls, while none of those parts is a genus or a differentia; rather, the predicated part is either a genus or a differentia. So not every composite essence is composed of genus and differentia, because it may be composed of parts that are not predicated; and not every essence composed of predicated parts is like that either, i.e., composed of genus and differentia, based on the mentioned probability... He means by it the composition of the essence from two equal matters. (His statement: 'and a part of it' is an interpretation of what precedes.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents the three-fold relation of the differentia (fasl) as explained in Sharh al-Matali'. First, in relation to the species, the differentia is constitutive (muqawwim), such as 'rational' for human. Second, in relation to the genus, it is divisive (muqassim), like 'rational' dividing the genus 'animal' into species. Third, in relation to the 'share' (hissa) of the species from the genus, it acts as an efficient cause; for example, 'rationality' brings about the specific animality in humans, and 'neighing' brings about the specific animality in horses. The commentary then elaborates on the definition of 'muqawwim' as that which enters into the constitution (qiwam) of the thing and is part of it. Al-‘Attar clarifies that the differentia is constitutive only for the species, not for individuals or sub-species. Then a key argument is presented: It is impossible for every differentia to have its own differentia, because that would lead to an infinite regress of essential parts, making the essence composed of infinite parts, which is absurd. An objection is raised: the nature of differentia applies to itself, so it should have a differentia. The answer: the distinguishing feature (absence of genus) is not a differentia because it is not essential. Finally, it is noted that not every part of an essence is a genus or differentia; only predicated parts (al-ajza' al-mahmula) can be.

D) It is accidental.

Key Takeaway
The differentia has three logical functions: it constitutes the species, divides the genus, and efficiently brings about the species' specific share of the genus.

Why a Differentia Cannot Have Its Own Differentia (Infinite Regress)
Author refutes the possibility of a differentia for a differentia via infinite regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument against infinite regress of differentiae and refute the objection that every differentia must have a differentia.

Core Idea
A differentia cannot have a differentia, because that would lead to an infinite chain of constitutive parts, which is impossible for any finite essence.

If a differentia is itself a type of essence, could it be composed of a genus and a differentia? This would lead to a regress. In this passage, the authors present a powerful argument that an infinite regress of differentiae is impossible, and they answer a clever objection. This argument is structurally similar to the Kalam proof for a First Cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being made
The author states: 'It is impossible that every differentia have a differentia.' This is the central claim.
The claim is about 'every differentia' – it denies the possibility of a universal property.
2
Understand the reason for impossibility
If every differentia had its own differentia, then the essence of the original species would be composed of infinite parts (since each differentia would add more essential parts). An actual infinite is impossible (a standard Aristotelian/Islamic principle).
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assume the opposite, derive an impossibility, therefore the opposite is false.
3
Find the objection
The objector says: The nature of 'differentia' applies to itself (i.e., differentia is a type of essence). It shares with the species the nature of being a differentia, but it differs by not including the genus. This distinguishing feature (absence of genus) must be a differentia for the differentia.
The objection uses the principle that what distinguishes must be a differentia.
4
Analyze the answer to the objection
The answer: The absence of genus is not a differentia because it is not an essential attribute. If it were essential, it would be essential for the species as well (since the species also lacks the genus in some sense? Actually, the species has the genus as a part). The key is that the differentiating feature must be essential to the differentia, but it is not; it is a negation (absence).
The text says: 'If it were essential, it would be essential for the species, which is impossible.' This stops the regress.
5
Evaluate the overall argument
The argument successfully blocks infinite regress. The objection fails because it mistakes an accidental distinction for an essential differentia. Without essentiality, the differentia cannot be a differentia for another differentia.
This shows the importance of the condition that a differentia must be essential (dhati).

(قَوْلُهُ : وَإِذَا نُسِبَ الْفَصْلُ ... إِلَخ) قال في شرح المطالع : الفصلُ لهُ نِسَبٌ ثلاث؛ نسبةٌ لِلنَّوع ونسبةٌ لِلجنسِ ونسبةٌ إلى حِصَّةِ النَّوعِ مِنَ الجنسِ، أَمَّا نِسبتُهُ إلى النّوعِ ; فَبِأَنَّهُ مُقَوِّمٌ لَهُ كَتَقويمِ النَّاطِقِ لِلإنسان، وأمَّا نِسبته إلى الجنسِ ; فَبِأَنَّهُ مُقَسّمٌ لَهُ كتقسيم النَّاطِقِ الحيوان إلى الإنسان، وأمَّا نسبتُهُ إلى الحصّةِ; فَنَقَلَ الإِمَامُ عَنِ الشَّيخ أَنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ فاعليَّةٌ لِوجودِهَا مثلاً مِنَ الحيوان في الإنسانِ حِصَّة، وَكَذَا في الفرس; وغيره، والموجدُ للحيوانية التي في الإنسان والناطقيَّةِ وللحيوانية التي في الفرس هو الصَّاهلية ا.هـ. أي : إلى شيء يميز الفصل ذلك الشيء فمقوم؛ أي: فهو فصل مقوم لذلك الشيء، بمعنى أنه داخل في قوامه وجزء له. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : إِلَى شَيْءٍ) بمعنى نوع، وقوله : (يميّزُ الفصلُ ذلكَ الشَّيء)، أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ الصِّفةَ جرَتْ على غيرِ مَنْ هِيَ له فكانَ على المصنفِ إبراز الضمير بأن يقول : ما يميز هو إيَّاه، وقد يُقالُ : إنّهُ جارٍ على مذهب الكوفيين مع رعاية الاختصار، أو على قولِ مَن يقولُ : إِنَّ الإبراز إنَّما يجب في غيرِ الفعل، وَكَذَا يُقالُ فيما بعدَهُ. (قَوْلُهُ : فِي قِوَامِهِ) أي : في حقيقته، وقولُهُ: (وَجُزْءٌ لَهُ); عطفُ لازم على ملزوم. العطار (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ إِلَى شَيْءٍ أي نوع فالصِّنفُ والشَّخصُ وإِنْ ميَّزَهُمَا الفصل; لكنَّهُ ليسَ مُقوّماً بالنسبة إليهِمَا , بلْ لِلنَّوعَ الصَّادقِ عليهما، وفي شرح المطالع : يمتنع أنْ يكونَ لِكُلِّ فصل فصلِّ لِوُجوبِ الانتهاء إلى فصل لا جزء لَهُ، وإلا ; لتركَّبَتِ ; الماهيَّةُ مِنْ أجزاءً غيرِ مُتَناهِيةٍ وَهُوَ مُحالٌ، فإنْ قُلْتَ: يجب أنْ يكونَ لِكُلِّ فصل فصل; لأنَّ طبيعة الفصل صادقة على النَّوعِ وعلى نَفْسِهِ، فَيَكونُ مُشارِكاً لِلنَّوعِ في طبيعتِهِ، وهُوَ مُمتاز عنهُ لِعَدَم دُخولِ الجنَسِ فيهِ، وَمَا بِهِ الامتياز فصل; فَيَكُونُ لِلفَضْل فَصل، أُجيبُ : بأنَّ عدم دخول الجنس في ماهية الفصل ليسَ فَصلاً، وإِنَّما يكون فصلاً لو كانَ ذاتيا وليس كذلك , وإِلَّا ; لَكانَ ذاتيًّا لِلنَّوعِ وَهُوَ مُحالٌ . (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : فَهُوَ فَصْلٌ مُقَوِّمٌ هذا هُوَ الاسمُ لَا مُجرَّدَ المقوّم ; لأنَّ المقوِّمَ أعم مِنَ الفصل، لأنَّ كُلَّ جزءٍ لِلماهِيَّةِ مُقوِّمٌ لَهَا، فلا يكون ذلك الجزء قِسماً للفصل، بل هو قسيم له، قال في شرح المطالع : ليسَ كُلُّ جزء جنساً أو فصلاً، فإنَّ العشرةَ مُركَّبَةٌ مِنَ الآحادِ، والبيتَ مِنَ السَّقْفِ والجدرانِ الأربع; مَعَ أَنَّ شَيْئاً مِنْ تِلْكَ الأجزاء ليس ولا فصل بل الجزء المحمولُ إِمَّا جنس أو فصل، فليسَ كُلُّ ماهيَّةٍ مُركَّبة يكون تركُبُهَا مِنَ الجنس والفصل لِجَوازِ تركُبِهَا مِنَ الأجزاء الغير المحمولة، وَلَا كُلُّ ماهيَّةٍ مُركَّبَةٍ مِنَ الأجزاء المحمولةِ كَذلِكَ; أي: مُركَّبَةٍ مِنَ الجنس والفصل بناء على الاحتمال المذكور ....هـ. يريدُ بِهِ تَركُبَ الماهِيَّةِ مِن أَمرَيْنِ مُتساوين. (قَوْلُهُ : وَجُزْةٌ لَهُ تَفسيرٌ لِمَا قَبلَهُ.
(His statement: 'And when the differentia is attributed... etc.) He said in Sharh al-Matali': The differentia has three relations: relation to the species, relation to the genus, and relation to the share of the species from the genus. As for its relation to the species: it is constitutive (muqawwim) for it, like the constitution of 'rational' for human. As for its relation to the genus: it is divisive (muqassim) for it, like the division of 'rational' for animal into human. As for its relation to the share: the Imam transmitted from the Shaykh that it is an efficient cause for its existence, for example, from animal in human a share, and likewise in horse and other; and the bringer-into-existence of the animality in human and rationality and of the animality in horse is the 'neighing' quality. End. 

i.e., to a thing that the differentia distinguishes that thing; so it is constitutive (muqawwim); i.e., it is a differentia constitutive for that thing, meaning it enters into its constitution (qiwam) and is a part of it. Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'i.e., to a thing') meaning a species, and his statement: (the differentia distinguishes that thing), he indicated by that that the adjective is applied to other than whom it is for, so it was upon the author to reveal the pronoun by saying: 'what distinguishes it is it', and it may be said: it is in accordance with the Kufan school with consideration of brevity, or according to those who say: the revealing is only obligatory in other than the verb, and similarly what follows. (His statement: 'in its constitution') i.e., in its reality, and his statement: (and a part of it) is an apposition of necessary to necessary. Al-‘Attar: (His statement: 'i.e., to a thing' meaning a species, so the sub-species and the individual, even if the differentia distinguishes them, it is not constitutive with respect to them, but rather for the species that is true of them. And in Sharh al-Matali': It is impossible that every differentia have a differentia, due to the necessity of stopping at a differentia that has no part; otherwise, the essence would be composed of infinite parts, which is impossible. If you say: It is necessary that every differentia have a differentia, because the nature of differentia is true of the species and of itself; so it participates with the species in its nature, and it is distinguished from it by the absence of the genus in it, and that by which it is distinguished is a differentia, so the differentia has a differentia. The answer: The absence of the genus in the essence of the differentia is not a differentia, but it would be a differentia if it were essential, and it is not; otherwise, it would be essential for the species, which is impossible. (His statement: 'i.e., it is a constitutive differentia' this is the name, not merely the constitutive, because 'constitutive' is more general than 'differentia', because every part of the essence is constitutive for it, so that part is not a division of differentia, but rather it is co-divisive with it. He said in Sharh al-Matali': Not every part is a genus or a differentia, for ten is composed of units, and a house of a roof and four walls, while none of those parts is a genus or a differentia; rather, the predicated part is either a genus or a differentia. So not every composite essence is composed of genus and differentia, because it may be composed of parts that are not predicated; and not every essence composed of predicated parts is like that either, i.e., composed of genus and differentia, based on the mentioned probability... He means by it the composition of the essence from two equal matters. (His statement: 'and a part of it' is an interpretation of what precedes.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents the three-fold relation of the differentia (fasl) as explained in Sharh al-Matali'. First, in relation to the species, the differentia is constitutive (muqawwim), such as 'rational' for human. Second, in relation to the genus, it is divisive (muqassim), like 'rational' dividing the genus 'animal' into species. Third, in relation to the 'share' (hissa) of the species from the genus, it acts as an efficient cause; for example, 'rationality' brings about the specific animality in humans, and 'neighing' brings about the specific animality in horses. The commentary then elaborates on the definition of 'muqawwim' as that which enters into the constitution (qiwam) of the thing and is part of it. Al-‘Attar clarifies that the differentia is constitutive only for the species, not for individuals or sub-species. Then a key argument is presented: It is impossible for every differentia to have its own differentia, because that would lead to an infinite regress of essential parts, making the essence composed of infinite parts, which is absurd. An objection is raised: the nature of differentia applies to itself, so it should have a differentia. The answer: the distinguishing feature (absence of genus) is not a differentia because it is not essential. Finally, it is noted that not every part of an essence is a genus or differentia; only predicated parts (al-ajza' al-mahmula) can be.

تسلسل
