Checkpoint
According to Sharh al-Matali', why is it impossible for every differentia to have a differentia?
A) Because differentiae are not essential.
B) Because it would lead to an infinite regress of essential parts.
C) Because the genus already provides the distinction.
D) Because the differentia is unique for each species.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress of essential parts is logically impossible; therefore, there must be a stopping point at a differentia that has no differentia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the same logical structure as the differentia regress argument, construct an argument against an infinite chain of temporal events (hawadith). Show that an infinite past is impossible, therefore there must be a beginning (a First Event created by God).
?
In Usul al-Fiqh, some argued that every ruling (hukm) must have a cause ('illa), and that cause itself must have a cause, leading to infinite regress. How does the argument from this lesson help refute that regress? Identify the flaw in the analogy.

The Dual Function of the Differential (Fasl)
Author explains the dual function of the differential

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the constitutive and divisive functions of a differential and apply this distinction to any given definition.

Core Idea
A differential constitutes the species and divides the genus — but only one genuine division results.

In the classical theory of definition, the differential (fasl) plays a dual role. Depending on what it is attributed to, it either builds the species or carves up the genus. The author explains this with examples from animal and body, and then addresses a subtle objection about whether the opposite of the differential also produces a real division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two attributions
When the differential is attributed to what it distinguishes (the species), it becomes muqawwim (constitutive). When attributed to what it is distinguished from (the genus), it becomes muqassim (divisive).
For example, 'rational' constitutes 'human' and divides 'animal'.
2
Apply the example of 'rational' with 'animal'
If you join 'rational' to 'animal', you get 'rational animal', a species of animal. This is divisive because it splits 'animal' into rational and non-rational.
The opposite (non-rational) is not a real species but a correlate.
3
Analyze the objection about two sections
Someone might object that joining the differential to the genus produces two sections: one with the differential and one without. The author refutes this: the differential only generates one positive section; the negative is not a distinct division.
This prevents the fallacy of multiplying divisions.
4
Generalize the dual nature
Every differential has this dual function: constitutive relative to the species, divisive relative to the genus. One must be careful not to treat the absence of the differential as a genuine species.
This principle applies to all five universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا نُسِبَ إلى ما يُمَيِّزه عنه كالحيوانِ يكونُ مُقسماً له؛ لأنَّه إذا نُسِبَ إلى الحيوان وانضم إليه، صارَ حيواناً ناطقاً، وهو قسم من الحيوان. فإنْ قُلْتَ : إذا انضم إليهِ مَا يُمَيَّرُ عنه ؛ حصلَ قِسمان: أحدهما: ما حصل بانضمامه إلى ما يُمَيَّرُ عنه ، والآخر مُقابلُهُ فَانضمامه إلى ما يميز عنه وجوداً ؛ : مُحصِّلُ قِسمين . قُلْتُ: لَا نُسلّمُ ذلك ؛ إذ الحاصل بانضمامه المذكورِ قسم بِلَا شَكٍّ، وأمَّا حصولُ مُقابلِهِ؛ فَلَا ؛ إذ إنَّما يبقى ما يميّز عنه غير مُقيَّدٍ بِهِ وَلَا بِعَدمِهِ، وهو ليس يَمَيَّزُ بقسم ؛ بل هو قسيم . وكذلكَ النَّامي إذا نُسِبَ إلى ما يميّزه - أي: الجسم النامي ـ يكونُ مقوّماً له، وإذا نُسِبَ إلى ما يُمَيَّرُ عنه كالجسمِ؛ كانَ مقسماً له. (وَ) الفصلُ (المقَوِّمُ لِلْعَالِي)؛ قال المصنِّفُ في شرح الرسالة: الجنس العالي جازَ أنْ يكونَ لَهُ فصل يُقوِّمُهُ بناءً على جواز تركُبِهِ مِنْ أَمرَيْن مُتساويين، ويجبُّ أَنْ يكونَ لهُ فصل يَقسمُهُ ؛ ضرورةَ أنَّ تحتَهُ أنواعاً مُتمايزةً بالفصول، والنَّوعُ السَّافِلُ يجبُ أَنْ يكونَ لهُ فصل يقوِّمُهُ؛ ضَرورة أنَّ فوقَهُ جِنساً ، فَلَا بُدَّ مِن فَصْل يُميّزُهُ عَمَّا شاركَهُ فيه، ويمتنع أنْ يكونَ لهُ فصل يقسِمُهُ ؛ لامتناع أنْ يكونَ تحته نوع، والمتوسطات مِنَ الأجناس والأنواع يجبُ أنْ يكونَ لَهَا فصولٌ مُقوّمةٌ؛ ضرورةَ أَنَّ فوقها أجناساً، وفصولٌ مُقسّمةٌ؛ ضَرورة أنَّ تحتها أنواعاً.
And when it is attributed to what distinguishes it from it, like the animal, it becomes divisive for it; because if it is attributed to animal and joined to it, it becomes a rational animal, which is a division of animal. If you say: When that which distinguishes it from it joins it, two sections result: one is what results from its joining to what distinguishes it from it, and the other is its opposite; so its joining to what distinguishes it from it in existence produces two sections. I say: We do not concede that; because what results from the mentioned joining is a section without doubt, but as for the obtaining of its opposite, no; because that which distinguishes it from it remains not restricted by it nor by its absence, and it is not distinguished as a section; rather it is a correlate. And similarly the growing when attributed to what distinguishes it—i.e., the growing body—is constitutive for it, and when attributed to what it is distinguished from, like the body, is divisive for it. And (the differential) (the constitutive for the higher); the author said in the commentary on the epistle: The highest genus may have a differential that constitutes it based on the permissibility of its composition from two equal things, and it must have a differential that divides it necessarily, because under it there are species distinguished by differentials. The lowest species must have a differential that constitutes it necessarily, because above it there is a genus, so there must be a differential that distinguishes it from what shares with it, and it is impossible for it to have a differential that divides it, because it is impossible for there to be a species under it. The intermediate genera and species must have constitutive differentials necessarily, because above them are genera, and divisive differentials necessarily, because under them are species.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory text from a commentary (sharh) on the Tadhheeb. It explains the dual role of the differential (fasl) in logic. The author first states the general principle: a differential, when attributed to what it distinguishes (i.e., the species), is constitutive (muqawwim); when attributed to what it is distinguished from (i.e., the genus), it is divisive (muqassim). He gives the example of 'rational' (natiq): when joined to 'animal', it produces the species 'rational animal', which is a division of the genus 'animal'. He then anticipates an objection: one might think that joining the differential to the genus produces two sections (the positive and the negative). The author refutes this by explaining that the differential only produces one positive section; the opposite (e.g., non-rational) is not a genuine section but a correlate (qasim), as it is defined only negatively. He then provides another example, 'growing' (nami), which constitutes 'growing body' and divides 'body'. Finally, he discusses the differential for the highest genus: it may have a constitutive differential if it is composite, and it must have a divisive differential because there are species under it. The lowest species must have a constitutive differential but cannot have a divisive one. Intermediate genera and species require both. This exposition clarifies the application of the differential across the hierarchy of genera and species, a key concept in the logic of definitions.

مقوّم
مقسّم
جنس عالي
نوع سافل

According to the passage, what happens when the differential 'rational' is joined to the genus 'animal'?
A) It produces two equal species: rational and non-rational.
B) It produces one species (rational animal) and the opposite is only a correlate.
C) It only produces the species 'human' with no division of the genus.
D) It has no effect on the genus.

Key Takeaway
The differential serves two roles: constitutive for the species and divisive for the genus, but only the positive division yields a genuine species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Differential in the Hierarchy of Genera
Author explains the differential for highest genus, lowest species, and intermediates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which types of differential (constitutive and/or divisive) apply to the highest genus, lowest species, and intermediate categories.

Core Idea
The highest genus may have constitutive but must have divisive; the lowest species must have constitutive but cannot have divisive; intermediates need both.

The dual function of the differential is not uniform across all levels of the Porphyrian tree. The author applies the earlier principle to the highest genus, lowest species, and everything in between, showing how the necessity and possibility of each type depend on the position in the hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three categories
Identify the highest genus (jins 'ali), lowest species (naw' safil), and intermediate genera/species in the passage.
The highest genus has no genus above it; the lowest species has no species below it.
2
Analyze the highest genus
The highest genus may have a constitutive differential if it is composite; it must have a divisive differential because it has species under it. This ensures it can be divided into its constituent species.
For example, 'substance' (jawhar) may be composite and thus have a constitutive differential; it always has a divisive differential.
3
Analyze the lowest species
The lowest species must have a constitutive differential because it has a genus above it that it shares with other species; it cannot have a divisive differential because there is no species under it.
For example, 'human' must have the differential 'rational' to distinguish it from other animals, but it cannot divide further.
4
Analyze the intermediates
Intermediate genera and species must have both constitutive and divisive differentials: constitutive because they have a higher genus, and divisive because they have lower species.
For example, 'animal' is intermediate: it is constituted by 'growing' and divides into rational and irrational.

وإذا نُسِبَ إلى ما يُمَيِّزه عنه كالحيوانِ يكونُ مُقسماً له؛ لأنَّه إذا نُسِبَ إلى الحيوان وانضم إليه، صارَ حيواناً ناطقاً، وهو قسم من الحيوان. فإنْ قُلْتَ : إذا انضم إليهِ مَا يُمَيَّرُ عنه ؛ حصلَ قِسمان: أحدهما: ما حصل بانضمامه إلى ما يُمَيَّرُ عنه ، والآخر مُقابلُهُ فَانضمامه إلى ما يميز عنه وجوداً ؛ : مُحصِّلُ قِسمين . قُلْتُ: لَا نُسلّمُ ذلك ؛ إذ الحاصل بانضمامه المذكورِ قسم بِلَا شَكٍّ، وأمَّا حصولُ مُقابلِهِ؛ فَلَا ؛ إذ إنَّما يبقى ما يميّز عنه غير مُقيَّدٍ بِهِ وَلَا بِعَدمِهِ، وهو ليس يَمَيَّزُ بقسم ؛ بل هو قسيم . وكذلكَ النَّامي إذا نُسِبَ إلى ما يميّزه - أي: الجسم النامي ـ يكونُ مقوّماً له، وإذا نُسِبَ إلى ما يُمَيَّرُ عنه كالجسمِ؛ كانَ مقسماً له. (وَ) الفصلُ (المقَوِّمُ لِلْعَالِي)؛ قال المصنِّفُ في شرح الرسالة: الجنس العالي جازَ أنْ يكونَ لَهُ فصل يُقوِّمُهُ بناءً على جواز تركُبِهِ مِنْ أَمرَيْن مُتساويين، ويجبُّ أَنْ يكونَ لهُ فصل يَقسمُهُ ؛ ضرورةَ أنَّ تحتَهُ أنواعاً مُتمايزةً بالفصول، والنَّوعُ السَّافِلُ يجبُ أَنْ يكونَ لهُ فصل يقوِّمُهُ؛ ضَرورة أنَّ فوقَهُ جِنساً ، فَلَا بُدَّ مِن فَصْل يُميّزُهُ عَمَّا شاركَهُ فيه، ويمتنع أنْ يكونَ لهُ فصل يقسِمُهُ ؛ لامتناع أنْ يكونَ تحته نوع، والمتوسطات مِنَ الأجناس والأنواع يجبُ أنْ يكونَ لَهَا فصولٌ مُقوّمةٌ؛ ضرورةَ أَنَّ فوقها أجناساً، وفصولٌ مُقسّمةٌ؛ ضَرورة أنَّ تحتها أنواعاً.
And when it is attributed to what distinguishes it from it, like the animal, it becomes divisive for it; because if it is attributed to animal and joined to it, it becomes a rational animal, which is a division of animal. If you say: When that which distinguishes it from it joins it, two sections result: one is what results from its joining to what distinguishes it from it, and the other is its opposite; so its joining to what distinguishes it from it in existence produces two sections. I say: We do not concede that; because what results from the mentioned joining is a section without doubt, but as for the obtaining of its opposite, no; because that which distinguishes it from it remains not restricted by it nor by its absence, and it is not distinguished as a section; rather it is a correlate. And similarly the growing when attributed to what distinguishes it—i.e., the growing body—is constitutive for it, and when attributed to what it is distinguished from, like the body, is divisive for it. And (the differential) (the constitutive for the higher); the author said in the commentary on the epistle: The highest genus may have a differential that constitutes it based on the permissibility of its composition from two equal things, and it must have a differential that divides it necessarily, because under it there are species distinguished by differentials. The lowest species must have a differential that constitutes it necessarily, because above it there is a genus, so there must be a differential that distinguishes it from what shares with it, and it is impossible for it to have a differential that divides it, because it is impossible for there to be a species under it. The intermediate genera and species must have constitutive differentials necessarily, because above them are genera, and divisive differentials necessarily, because under them are species.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory text from a commentary (sharh) on the Tadhheeb. It explains the dual role of the differential (fasl) in logic. The author first states the general principle: a differential, when attributed to what it distinguishes (i.e., the species), is constitutive (muqawwim); when attributed to what it is distinguished from (i.e., the genus), it is divisive (muqassim). He gives the example of 'rational' (natiq): when joined to 'animal', it produces the species 'rational animal', which is a division of the genus 'animal'. He then anticipates an objection: one might think that joining the differential to the genus produces two sections (the positive and the negative). The author refutes this by explaining that the differential only produces one positive section; the opposite (e.g., non-rational) is not a genuine section but a correlate (qasim), as it is defined only negatively. He then provides another example, 'growing' (nami), which constitutes 'growing body' and divides 'body'. Finally, he discusses the differential for the highest genus: it may have a constitutive differential if it is composite, and it must have a divisive differential because there are species under it. The lowest species must have a constitutive differential but cannot have a divisive one. Intermediate genera and species require both. This exposition clarifies the application of the differential across the hierarchy of genera and species, a key concept in the logic of definitions.

نوع سافل
مقوّم
مقسّم

Key Takeaway
The necessity of constitutive and divisive differentials depends on the position in the hierarchy: highest genus, intermediate, or lowest species.

The Constitutive Differentia of the Higher Constitutes the Lower
The author explains the principle that the differentia of a higher genus constitutes lower species, with a chain from body to man, and denies the converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that the differentia of a higher genus constitutes all lower species, and identify cases where the converse fails, using the hierarchy of created beings.

Core Idea
A higher genus's differentia flows down to all lower species, but a lower species' differentia does not necessarily flow up.

When defining things in Islamic sciences, we often encounter hierarchies of genera and species. The differentia that distinguishes a higher genus also defines every species under it, because it is part of their essence. For example, 'composite' distinguishes body and also defines growing body, animal, and man. However, 'rational' defines only man, not the higher genera. This principle is essential for constructing accurate definitions in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hierarchy of species
List the terms in the passage from highest to lowest: body (jism), growing body (jism nāmin), animal (ḥayawān), man (insān). Note that each lower species is a type of the higher.
The hierarchy uses relative genus and species; 'body' is the highest relative species here.
2
Extract the differentia at each level
The differentia for body is 'composite' (murakkab); for growing body is 'growing' (nāmin); for animal is 'sentient' (ḥassās); for man is 'rational' (nāṭiq). Write them down.
These differentiae are constitutive, meaning they are part of the essence.
3
Verify that higher differentiae constitute lower species
Check that 'composite' is part of the definition of growing body, animal, and man. Similarly, 'growing' is part of animal and man; 'sentient' is part of man. This illustrates the principle.
The axiom 'muqawwim al-muqawwim muqawwim' supports this: what constitutes the constitutive also constitutes the thing.
4
Understand the converse denial
Note that 'rational' constitutes only man, not animal or body. Therefore, it is false that every differentia of the lower constitutes the higher. The converse holds only in the logical sense (some differentiae of the lower, specifically those of higher genera entering into the lower, do constitute the higher).
The passage distinguishes linguistic converse (complete reversal) from logical converse (particular affirmative).
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the definition of 'worship' ('ibāda) in fiqh: its genus is 'act' (fi'l) and its differentia is 'commanded with reward'. The differentia 'commanded' also defines all types of worship (e.g., prayer, fasting). But the differentia of a specific worship, like 'bowing' (rukū'), does not define all worship. Trace the hierarchy.
This mirrors the structure in the passage and helps in constructing sound definitions.

النَّوع،
لَهُ؛ تَأَمَّلْ.
Meaning: the higher (al-fawqani) of genus and species (1) is constitutive (muqawwim) for the lower (al-safil); i.e., the lower of the two. So the differentia constitutive of body is constitutive of the growing body, and the constitutive of the growing body is constitutive of the animal. Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'i.e., the higher' means: applicable to the intermediate, and by this he refutes what might be supposed, that by 'higher' is meant what has no genus or species above it. His saying: 'of genus and species' means the relative genus and species, not the real genus, to avoid problem with what preceded: that constitution (taqwim) occurs only with species, while with genus it is divisive (muqassim). In other words, his saying 'of genus' intends the relative species, because the higher of them has a differentia, and the conjunction of 'species' to 'genus' implies difference, so it would require inclusion of the higher genus, though it is simple, unless it is said that the conjunction of 'species' is explicative. That being so, the commentator's later saying 'of the two' takes into account the multiplicity of the wording. He said: His saying: 'So the constitutive differentia... etc.' Its summary: that body is the highest of the relative species, and it is a composite substance; our saying 'composite' is a differentia constitutive of body, and below it is growing body, and its constitutive differentia is 'growing', and below it is animal, and its constitutive differentia is 'sentient', and below it is man, and its constitutive differentia is 'rational'. Thus 'composite', as it constituted the higher (body), constitutes everything below it. Al-Attar: His saying: 'i.e., the higher' – this interpretation was mentioned by al-Sayyid so that the ruling would include the intermediates. (1) (The commentator's saying: 'genus and species' – it would be appropriate to replace them with 'relative species', because 'genus' if interpreted as relative species becomes redundant with what follows and vice versa, and it does not include the real genus which is lower, and what the commentator strained to say is far-fetched. His interpretation of 'higher' as 'above' to include intermediate species and the highest, and 'lower' as 'below' to clarify that what is meant here is the more specific than others, thus including intermediates, as previously it was a species of species. End of al-Sharnubi. Contrary to that. Section: On the Principles of Concepts. 387. And it is so because the higher, like body, is inside the constitution (qiwam) of the lower – i.e., the growing body – and a part of it; so the higher is constitutive of the lower. And if the higher is constitutive of the lower, then its differentia is also constitutive of the lower. Because the constitutive of the constitutive is constitutive. And when this is established, we say: Every differentia that constitutes the higher constitutes the lower, but not the converse, in the linguistic sense. Al-Dasuqi: From the growing body, animal, and man. 'Growing', as it constituted the growing body, constitutes everything below it from animal and man. 'Sentient', as it constituted animal, constitutes what is below it from man. 'Rational' constitutes only man. His saying: 'For the constitutive of the constitutive is constitutive' means: because the constitutive of the constitutive of a thing is constitutive of that thing. So 'composite', the constitutive of body, which is constitutive of the growing body, is constitutive of the growing body; and similarly for what follows: 'growing', the constitutive of the growing body, which is constitutive of animal, is constitutive of animal; and 'sentient', the constitutive of animal, which is constitutive of man, is constitutive of man. His saying: 'and not the converse' means: this universal is correct. His saying: 'in the linguistic sense' means: reversing the order, putting the first last and the last first. Al-Attar: His saying: 'in the linguistic sense' means: so it cannot be said: Every constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher, for 'rational', for example, constitutes man but not body. The restriction to the linguistic sense is to guard against the logical sense, for that is a necessary concomitant of the proposition and cannot be denied. Here it is a particular affirmative: Some constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher. That 'some' is the differentiae of the genera entering into the essence of the species. For example, man is a rational animal; 'growing' and 'sentient' enter into his essence and are constitutive of him, and they are differentiae for what is above him of genera, though insofar as they enter into him, they are not differentiae for him. Reflect.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a compressed statement in the matn: 'The higher of genus and species is constitutive of the lower.' The commentator first clarifies that 'higher' here is relative, not absolute, and includes intermediate species—those that are both higher and lower relative to others. He then illustrates with a chain: body (composite substance), growing body (body that grows), animal (growing and sentient), and man (rational animal). The differentia 'composite' constitutes body, and since body is higher than growing body, 'composite' also constitutes growing body, and so on down to man. The principle established is that the differentia of a higher genus runs through all lower species under it. The key logical axiom given is 'muqawwam al-muqawwam muqawwim' (the constitutive of the constitutive is constitutive). The passage then explicitly denies the converse in the linguistic sense: it is not true that every differentia of the lower constitutes the higher. For example, 'rational' constitutes only man, not animal or body. However, in the logical sense, the converse is a particular affirmative: some differentiae of the lower (namely those that are also differentiae of higher genera entering into the lower's essence) do constitute the higher. Al-Attar's gloss clarifies that 'growing' and 'sentient', while parts of man's essence, are differentiae for the genera above man (animal and growing body), not for man himself. Thus the passage teaches the careful distinction between the hierarchical flow of constitutive differentiae and the limits of reversal.

Key Takeaway
The differentia of a higher genus is always part of the essence of every lower species, but the differentia of the lowest species may not be part of the higher genera.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Parsing the Definition of General Accident ('Arad 'Amm)
Commentary on the definition of general accident as 'the external predicated of it and of others'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the definition of 'arad 'amm, identify its genus and differentia, explain what it excludes, and distinguish between its predication in answer to 'what is it?' and by equivalence.

Core Idea
A general accident is an external predicate shared across multiple essences, never used to answer 'what is it?' but still truly predicated of individuals.

In this lesson, we examine the precise definition of 'arad 'amm (general accident) as provided in the tradition and unpacked by commentators. The definition 'al-kharij al-maqul 'alayha wa 'ala ghayriha' contains a genus and a differentia that distinguish it from the particular property (khassa). We will parse each component, explore the different interpretations of the pronoun, and understand the rule about its predicational use. This will sharpen your ability to identify the five universals in real definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
The term is 'arad 'amm, defined as 'al-kharij al-maqul 'alayha wa 'ala ghayriha'. This is a definition by genus and differentia.
This definition is the focus of the entire passage.
2
Examine the genus 'al-kharij'
'Al-kharij' means 'the external' (non-essential). As a genus, it excludes the essential universals (genus, differentia, species). It does not by itself exclude khassa; that is done by the differentia.
Later commentators discuss whether 'al-kharij' alone can exclude khassa, but the text clarifies that the differentia is needed.
3
Analyze the differentia 'al-maqul 'alayha wa 'ala ghayriha'
This phrase means 'predicated of it and of others'. The pronoun 'it' may refer to the essence (haqiqa) or to 'what' (ma) from the definition of khassa. The two interpretations lead to slightly different scopes but both affirm that the accident is shared across multiple essences or individuals.
For example, 'created' is a general accident shared by the world and angels.
4
Understand the restriction on predication
'Arad 'amm is not said in answer to 'what is it?' (jawaab), but it can be truly predicated of individuals by equivalence (muwata'a). For instance, you cannot answer 'What is prayer?' with 'Valid', but you can say 'This prayer is valid'.
This resolves the apparent conflict that general accidents are sometimes called 'not said' while they are indeed predicated.
5
Compare with khassa (particular property)
Khassa is also an accident but is restricted to one essence, e.g., the capacity to laugh is khassa for humans. 'Arad 'amm is broader, predicated of multiple essences. Understanding this distinction helps in defining concepts correctly.
The definition's differentia 'wa 'ala ghayriha' explicitly excludes khassa.

And it is the external predicated of it and of others. So his statement: 'the external' excludes what is other than particular property. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'and it is the external... etc.' contains what has passed. And his statement: 'predicated of it' i.e., of the essence without his statement: 'one', and it is possible that the pronoun refers to 'what' from his statement: 'what is under one essence', and you consider it based on its meaning, since it applies to individuals as before. And similarly it is said in his statement: 'and of others'. The conclusion: that the pronoun in 'of it' may refer to the essence occurring in the definition of particular property, or to 'what' occurring in its definition. On the first meaning: predicated of the essence and other than it, as in 'human' and 'horse is walking'. On the second meaning: predicated of the individuals that are under an essence and the individuals that are other than it, like: 'Zaid and Amr', and 'this horse and donkey are walking'. His statement: 'predicated of it' does not contradict their statement that the general accident is not said in answer at all; because what is negated is its saying in the answer. As for his statement: 'on its individuals', i.e., its predication on them by equivalence, whether collectively or individually, is established, like 'Zaid is walking'. Al-'Attar: His statement: 'predicated of it and of others' — the two pronouns refer to 'what' in his statement in the definition of particular property: 'what is under an essence'. Al-'Isam said: The pronoun 'of it' refers to 'essence' not to 'one essence', for it is not good to conjoin 'and of others' to 'one essence', just as it is not good to say 'one man and other than him'; reflect. And know that some held that the particular property (khassa) which is one of the five universals is more general than the absolute and the relative, and his statement 'only' is interpreted as restrictive in a relative sense, not absolute; i.e., the restriction of 'walking' to human is in relation to plants. As for in relation to... End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on the definition of 'arad 'amm (general accident). The definition being commented on is: 'It is the external (al-kharij) predicated of it and of others.' The first point is that 'al-kharij' (the external) serves to exclude the particular property (khassa). Then al-Dasuqi explains that 'predicated of it' means predicated of the essence (haqiqa) without the word 'one', and the pronoun may refer to 'what' from the definition of khassa ('what is under one essence'). The meaning is clarified: either 'predicated of the essence and other than it' (e.g., 'walking' predicated of human and horse) or 'predicated of the individuals under an essence and individuals of other essences' (e.g., 'Zaid and Amr' and 'this horse and donkey'). The commentator notes that this does not conflict with the rule that general accident is not said in answer to 'what is it?' because that negation applies only to jawaab (answer), while predication on individuals (saying 'Zaid is walking') is valid. Al-'Attar adds that the two pronouns refer back to 'what' in the definition of khassa, and al-'Isam argues that the pronoun should refer to 'essence' not 'one essence' for syntactic reasons. The footnote from al-Sharnubi discusses a textual issue about the placement of 'external' as genus and differentia in the definition.

Excluding Genus, Species, and Differentia from Property
Commentators discuss which conditional excludes type, genus, and differentia from the definition of property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which conditional in the definition of property excludes genus, species, and differentia, and explain why al-Dasuqi favors assigning their exclusion to the first conditional.

Core Idea
The boundaries of property are set by conditionals that exclude what is internal to the essence.

In logic, property (khassa) is defined as a universal predicated on all individuals of a single species only. But this definition must be refined by conditionals (quyud) that exclude universals that are internal to the essence, such as genus, differentia, and species. Commentators al-Dasuqi and al-Attar debate which conditional does the excluding and how broadly. This lesson unpacks their discussion, showing how logical definitions are carefully adjusted to avoid ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditionals
The definition of property includes two restrictions: the universal must be 'external to the essence' (al-kharij) and 'not predicated on other than that species' (ala ghayriha). Locate these in the passage and note how they operate.
Al-Dasuqi refers to them as 'the first' and 'the last' conditional.
2
Understand why genus, differentia, species are problematic
Al-Dasuqi explains that genus and differentia are parts of the essence, and species is the whole essence. Therefore, they cannot be described as external or internal to the essence—the thing cannot exit from itself. This is why they must be excluded by conditionals.
3
Map exclusions to conditionals
According to al-Dasuqi, the second conditional (on other than it) only excludes real species and proximate differentia. The first conditional (external to essence) excludes them all absolutely, including relative species and remote differentia. Which exclusion is more comprehensive?
Al-Attar adds that relative species are actually genera, so the second conditional fails to exclude them.
4
Evaluate the more appropriate assignment
Al-Dasuqi states that assigning the exclusion of species and differentia to the first conditional is 'more appropriate' because it works for all types. Al-Attar refines this by suggesting that the term 'absolutely' (mutlaqan) in the matn should refer only to differentia, not to species and genera, because relative species are genera.
Consider why al-Dasuqi's view is considered more appropriate despite al-Attar's refinement.

(His statement: 'but attributing their exclusion to the first' means his statement: 'the external', and his statement: 'absolutely' refers to the three, so its meaning regarding species: whether real or relative; regarding genera: whether higher or lower; regarding differentia: whether proximate or remote. And it is possible that his statement: 'absolutely' refers only to the differentia, and that is more apparent, because relative species are genera. On this, the naming is not real but considered, based on considering the divisions with conditions of whereness, due to the combination of property and universal accident in 'the walker'. As for specifying the definition by absolute property as we previously explained, the divisions do not coincide. (His statement: 'exclusion of the species' means the real species; and his statement: 'and the differentia' means its differentia; and his statement: 'absolutely' could refer to the three, i.e., for species: real or relative; for genera: proximate or remote; and for the differentia of the species and the differentia of the genus; and it could refer only to the differentia, and that is more apparent.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of property (khassa) in logic. The original matn likely defined property as a universal predicated on all individuals of a single species only, and the commentator discusses which conditional (qayd) excludes non-property (ghayr al-khassa). The two conditionals are: 'external to the essence' and 'predicated on other than that species'. The first conditional is al-kharij (external), the second is 'ala ghayriha (on other than it). The commentators debate which one properly excludes genus, differentia, and species. Al-Dasuqi explains that genus and differentia are parts of the essence, species is the whole essence, so they cannot be described as external or internal to the essence. The last conditional ('ala ghayriha) only excludes real species and proximate differentia, not relative species or remote differentia. Al-Attar adds that if we consider the condition of haythiyya (whereness) in definitions, then property and universal accident may coincide in some subjects (like 'walker' for animals). He argues that the better view is to specify property as absolute property to avoid overlap. The passage then notes that 'absolutely' (mutlaqan) can refer to all three types (species, genera, differentia) or only to differentia, the latter being more apparent because relative species are actually genera.

قيد (qayd)
نوع (naw')
فصل (fasl)

The precise exclusion of genus, differentia, and species ensures that property remains a true exclusive predicate of a single species, preventing definitions from collapsing into accidental or essential universals.

Division of Lāzim into Lāzim al-Māhiyya and Lāzim al-Wujūd
Commentators define and debate the two types of necessary attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lāzim al-māhiyya and lāzim al-wujūd, and explain the refinement that lāzim al-wujūd is a subclass of lāzim al-māhiyya under a specific existence condition.

Core Idea
The division of necessary attributes is not a simple dichotomy but a nuanced classification refined by later commentators.

In logic, understanding necessary attributes (al-lāzim) is crucial for definitions. This passage presents the initial division of lāzim into two types, but quickly shows that the division requires refinement. We will walk through the definitions, the objections, and the final resolution that lāzim al-wujūd is actually a subset of lāzim al-māhiyya when considered under a specific mode of existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of lāzim
Locate where al-Dasūqī defines lāzim al-māhiyya (inseparable in mind and external) and lāzim al-wujūd (inseparable only in external). Note the examples: evenness for four, spatial occupation for body, universality for man.
2
Note the objection about lāzim al-shakhṣ
Al-'Attār points out that the division misses a third type: the necessary of the individual (lāzim al-shakhṣ). He argues this is not simply a subcase of lāzim al-wujūd because it involves individuation.
This objection drives the refinement.
3
Trace the response by al-Dawwānī and 'Abd al-Ḥakīm
Al-Dawwānī reinterprets the division as necessary of the species (lāzim al-naw') vs. necessary of the person (lāzim al-shakhṣ). 'Abd al-Ḥakīm explains that lāzim al-wujūd is actually lāzim al-māhiyya when considered under a specific existence condition (e.g., external existence with an accident).
Notice how the original dichotomy is preserved but deepened.
4
Apply the refined classification
Use the example of blackness for the Ethiopian: it is not necessary for man qua man (so not lāzim al-māhiyya), nor for existence absolutely (since white men exist), but it is necessary for the existence of the Ethiopian as a specific individual (so lāzim al-shakhṣ, subsumed under lāzim al-wujūd).
This shows how the refined classification handles tricky cases.

(قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى الْمَاهِيَّةِ) أي : يمتنع انفكاكُهُ عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ مُطلقاً؛ أي : بحسب كلا وُجودَيْها ، بمعنى أنَّها حيثُ وُجِدَتْ ؛ كانَتْ مُتَّصفةٌ بِهِ، وهو لازم الماهية كالزَّوجيَّةِ لِلأربعة، فإنَّ الأربعة زوج ؛ سواء كانت في الذهن أو في الخارج، أو لا يمتنع انفكاكه عنها إلا في وجودٍ خاصٌ كَالتَّحيز للجسم؛ فَإِنَّهُ إِنَّما يلزمه في الوجودِ الخارجيّ ، وَكَالكُلِّيَّةِ لِلإنسان؛ فإنَّها إِنَّما تلزمه في الوجودِ العقلي، كذا في الدَّوَّاني، قالَ الصَّفوي : وفيه نظر ظاهرُ إِنْ قُلْنَا : إنَّ الماهيَّاتِ موجودة حقيقةً في ضمن الأفرادِ ا.هـ. وجوابُهُ مَا تقرَّرَ أَنَّ الكُلِّيَّةَ مِنَ المعقولاتِ الثَّانيةِ ، فَهِيَ عارضةٌ لِلمفهوم الحاصل في العقلِ أَوَّلاً ، فَإِذا تصوَّرَ مَفهوم الحيوانِ مثلاً ؛ عرض لهُ أَنَّهُ مانعٌ مِن وقوعِ الشَّركةِ فيه، وأمَّا كون الحيوان موجوداً في الخارج أوَّلاً على الخلافِ في وجودِ الكُلِّيّ الطَّبيعي خارجاً ؛ فَشَيءٌ آخَرُ ، فَإِنَّهُ وَلَو قُلْنَا بِوجودِهِ خارجاً؛ لا يَتَّصِفُ بِكُلِّيَّةٍ وَلَا جزئيّةٍ ؛ لأنَّهُمَا إِنَّما يعرضان للمفاهيم عقلاً لا خارجاً، ويلزم على هذا التحقيق أنْ لا يكونَ السَّوادُ لازماً لوجودِ الإنسان؛ لأنَّهُ لا يلزمُ الإنسان في وجودِهِ الخارجي بل صنفاً منه، وأشار المصنِّفُ في شرح الرّسالة إلى الجوابِ بقوله : وإِنْ كانَ امتناع انفكاكِهِ عَنِ الماهية بعارض مخصوص، ويمكن انفكاكُهُ عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ مِنْ حيثُ هِيَ هي ؛ فَهُوَ لازم الوجودِ مَعَ الماهِيَّةِ ، كَالسَّوادِ للحبشي، وَمَا قالَهُ شيخُ الإسلام بعد قول المصنفِ : ما يمتنع انفكاكُهُ عَنِ الشَّيء ؛ سواء كانَ الشَّيءُ ماهيَّةٌ مُجرَّدةً أو مخلوطةٌ بالوجودِ الذهني أو الخارجيّ؛ تَبِعَ فيهِ جَدَّهُ في شرح الرّسالة حيث قال : وإِنَّما أَخْذُنَا الماهيَّةَ في تفسير اللازم أعمُّ مِنَ المجرَّدة والمخلوطة؛ لتصح جعلُ لازم الوجود قسماً منه ا . هـ. قال عبد الحكيمِ : وَهُوَ عَجيب ؛ إذْ ليس المراد بالماهية من حيثُ هي الماهية . المجردة؛ لامتناع عروض شيءٍ لها ؛ فَضْلاً عَنِ اللُّزومِ ا.هـ. وفي شرحِ الرَّازِيِّ على الشَّمسيَّةِ : اللَّازِمُ إِمَّا لازم الوجودِ كَالسَّوادِ لِلحَبشي ، فإنَّهُ لَازمٌ لِوجودِهِ وشخصِهِ؛ لَا لِمَا هِيَّتِهِ ؛ لأنَّ ما هيَّتَهُ الإنسانُ ، ولو كانَ السَّوادُ لازماً للإنسان؛ لكانَ كلَّ إنسان أسودَ ، وليس كذلك، وإمَّا لازم الماهية كالزَّوجيَّةِ للأربعة . . .هـ. قالَ الدَّوَّاني : وأنت تعلم أنَّ السَّوادَ كَمَا لا يلزمُ ماهيَّةَ الإنسان؛ لا يلزم وجودَهُ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ الإنسان الأبيض كثير، بلْ إِنَّما يلزمُ الماهيَّةَ الصنفيَّةَ، أعني : الحبشي، بحسب وجودِهَا في الخارج، فَيَصيرُ كلامُهُ بِحَسبِ الظَّاهِرِ في قوَّةِ السَّوادِ ليس لازماً لِمَاهِيَّةِ الإنسان، بل هو لازم لوجودِ الصِّنْفِ الَّذي تحتَهَا ، وَلَا يَخفَى عدم انتظامه وفواتُ المقابلة بين لازم الماهية ولازم الوجودِ، فإنَّ اللَّائِقَ بالمقام إيراد أمرٍ لا يكونُ لازماً للماهية ويكونُ لازِمَاً لوجودِ تلكَ الماهية ا.هـ. وأجاب عبد الحكيم بأنَّ مَعنى لازم الوجودِ؛ لازم الماهية باعتبار وجودِهَا الخارجيّ إمَّا مُطلقاً : كَالتَّحيُّ ، أو مأخوذاً بعارض : كَالسَّوادِ لِلحبشي ؛ فَإِنَّهُ لازم لِمَاهِيَّةِ الإنسان باعتبار وجودِهِ وتَشخُصِهِ الصِّنفي؛ لَا لِلمَاهِيَّةِ مِنْ حيثُ هِيَ، وَلَا مِنْ حيثُ الوجودُ مُطلقاً، وإلا ؛ لكان جميع أفرادِهِ أسود، أو باعتبار وجودِهَا الذهني؛ بأنْ يكونَ إدراكُهَا مُستلزماً لإدراكه ؛ إمَّا مُطلقاً، وإمَّا مَأخوذاً باعتبارِ عارض خارج عَنِ الماهيّة، وإنَّما لم يتعرّض لاستيفاء أقسام لازم الوجود، بل اكتفى بإيراد مثال للازم الوجود الخارجي المخصوص الَّذي هُوَ أَخفَى ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ مَظَنَّةُ الحِكْمَةِ، لا يتعلَّقُ غرضُ المنطقي ؛ أعني : الاكتساب بِهِ فإنَّ الكاسبَ لازم الماهيَّةِ، إِذْ هو المستعمل في الحدودِ وإِنَّما ذكر لازم الوجودِ استطراداً . .ا.هـ. ثمَّ قالَ الدَّوَّانِيُّ : والتَّحقيقُ أَنَّهُ يريدُ بِلازم الماهيَّة ؛ لازمَ النَّوع ، وَبِلازم الوجودِ ؛ لازم الشَّخصِ، فَإِنَّ السَّوادَ لِلحبشي إنَّما يلزمُ صنفيَّتَهُ الَّتي هِيَ مِنْ جملةِ مَا اعتبر في تَشخُصِهِ، فيكونُ لازماً لِتَشخُصِهِ؛ لَا
Either with respect to essence; al-Dasuqi: His saying 'Either with respect to essence... or existence.' So lāzim al-māhiyya is that which does not separate from the thing in the mind nor in the external. And lāzim al-wujūd is that which does not separate from the thing only in the external. al-'Attār: The deficiency is in the individual, and that the division did not include him. And his saying: 'Indeed the necessary of the individual is included in the necessary of existence' is prevented. For what is meant by the necessary of the individual is that which is necessary to it from the perspective of its individuation. And this is an additional restriction beyond what is considered in the essence of existence. And there will come a supplement for it, so wait. (His saying: 'Either with respect to essence') i.e., its separation from the essence is impossible absolutely; that is, according to both of its existences, meaning that wherever it exists, it is qualified by it. And that is lāzim al-māhiyya, like evenness for four, for four is even whether in the mind or in the external. Or its separation from it is only impossible in a specific existence, like spatial occupation for body, for it only is necessary to it in external existence; and like universality for man, for it only is necessary to it in intellectual existence. Thus in al-Dawwānī. al-Ṣafawī said: In it is an apparent consideration if we say that essences are actually existent within individuals. End quote. And its answer is what has been established: that universality is among the secondary intelligibles, so it is an accident of the concept obtained in the mind first. So when one conceives the concept of animal, for example, it acquires that it prevents sharing in it. As for the animal being existent externally, first, on the disagreement about the existence of the natural universal externally, that is another matter. For even if we say it exists externally, it is not qualified by universality or particularity, because they only accrue to concepts mentally, not externally. And it follows from this verification that blackness is not necessary for the existence of man, because it does not follow man in his external existence but rather a species thereof. And the author indicated in the Commentary on the Epistle the answer by his saying: 'And if the impossibility of its separation from the essence is due to a specific accident, and its separation from the essence insofar as it is itself is possible, then it is a necessary of existence with the essence, like blackness for the Ethiopian.' And what Shaykh al-Islam said after the author's statement: 'What is impossible to separate from the thing, whether the thing is a pure essence or mixed with mental or external existence' followed his grandfather in the Commentary on the Epistle, where he said: 'We only took essence in the interpretation of the necessary to be more general than pure and mixed in order to make the necessary of existence a division of it.' End quote. 'Abd al-Ḥakīm said: 'And it is strange, because what is meant by essence insofar as it is essence is not the pure essence, for it is impossible for an accident to occur to it, let alone necessity.' End quote. And in al-Rāzī's commentary on al-Shamsiyya: 'The necessary is either necessary of existence, like blackness for the Ethiopian, for it is necessary to his existence and his person, not to his essence; because his essence is man, and if blackness were necessary for man, then every man would be black, and that is not so. Or necessary of essence, like evenness for four...' End quote. Al-Dawwānī said: 'And you know that blackness, just as it is not necessary for the essence of man, is also not necessary for his existence; because the white man is abundant. Rather, it is only necessary for the specific essence, I mean the Ethiopian, according to its existence externally. So his speech, in appearance, amounts to blackness not being necessary for the essence of man, but it is necessary for the existence of the species that is under it. And it is not hidden that it lacks order and misses the comparison between necessary of essence and necessary of existence, for what is appropriate for the context is to present something that is not necessary for the essence and is necessary for the existence of that essence.' End quote. And 'Abd al-Ḥakīm answered: 'The meaning of necessary of existence is the necessary of essence considering its external existence, either absolutely, like spatial occupation, or taken with an accident, like blackness for the Ethiopian; for it is necessary for the essence of man considering his existence and his specific individuation, not for the essence insofar as it is, nor for existence absolutely; otherwise all its individuals would be black. Or considering its mental existence, such that its understanding necessitates its understanding, either absolutely or taken considering an accident external to the essence.' And he did not cover all divisions of necessary of existence, but sufficed with presenting an example of the specific external necessary of existence which is more hidden; because that is a place of wisdom; the logician's purpose, i.e., acquisition, is not attained by it, because the acquisitive is the necessary of essence, since it is used in definitions, and he only mentioned necessary of existence tangentially... End quote. Then al-Dawwānī said: 'And the verification is that he intends by necessary of essence the necessary of the species, and by necessary of existence the necessary of the person. For blackness for the Ethiopian is only necessary for his specific nature, which is part of what is considered in his individuation, so it is necessary for his individuation, not...'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of necessary attributes (al-lāzim) into two types: lāzim al-māhiyya (necessary of essence) and lāzim al-wujūd (necessary of existence). The discussion begins with al-Dasuqi explaining that lāzim al-māhiyya is an attribute inseparable from the thing in both mind and external reality, while lāzim al-wujūd is inseparable only in external existence. Al-'Attār then raises an objection: the division as stated is incomplete because it does not account for the necessary of the individual (lāzim al-shakhs). He argues that the necessary of the individual is not simply included in lāzim al-wujūd because it involves an additional restriction (qayd) of individuation. The author then explains the definition in more detail, providing examples: evenness for four is lāzim al-māhiyya because four is even in both mind and external; spatial occupation (al-taḥayyuz) for body is lāzim al-wujūd because it only applies externally; universality for man is also lāzim al-wujūd (specifically mental existence). Al-Ṣafawī raises a problem: if essences exist in individuals, then universality would apply externally? The response is that universality is a secondary intelligible ('āriḍ 'aqli) that only applies to concepts in the mind, not to external realities. This leads to a further puzzle: if universality is only mental, then blackness would not be necessary for man's existence at all (it is only for a sub-species). The author's answer is that blackness is necessary for the Ethiopian's existence but not for the essence of man. The discussion then cites al-Rāzī and al-Dawwānī. Al-Rāzī defines lāzim al-wujūd as necessary for existence/person, not essence, else every man would be black. Al-Dawwānī objects: blackness is not necessary for man's existence generally (since many men are white) but only for the Ethiopian species. This undermines the neat division. 'Abd al-Ḥakīm resolves this by redefining lāzim al-wujūd as lāzim al-māhiyya considered under a specific existence condition (e.g., external existence with an accident like 'being Ethiopian'). He clarifies that lāzim al-wujūd is not a separate category but a subset of lāzim al-māhiyya under a specific aspect. Finally, al-Dawwānī states that the true intention is: lāzim al-māhiyya = necessary of the species (al-naw'), and lāzim al-wujūd = necessary of the person (al-shakhṣ). This resolves the confusion by aligning the division with the level of generality. The passage thus refines the initial definition through objections and responses.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of 'lāzim al-māhiyya' as defined by al-Dasūqī?
A) Spatial occupation for body
B) Evenness for the number four
C) Universality for man
D) Blackness for an Ethiopian

Key Takeaway
The division of lāzim is refined from a simple dichotomy into a nuanced classification where lāzim al-wujūd is understood as lāzim al-māhiyya under a specific condition of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the attribute 'being in Arabic' for the Quran is lāzim al-māhiyya or lāzim al-wujūd, and explain your reasoning.
?
Construct an example from fiqh where an attribute is necessary for a specific individual (e.g., fasting of Ramadan for a specific day) but not for the essence of the obligation.

Hadd and Rasm
advanced
15 min
Dividing the Necessary Concomitant: Existence vs. Quiddity
Author defines the two types of necessary concomitant with examples and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a concomitant of quiddity and a concomitant of existence using the criteria of essential vs. accidental necessity, and explain the refinements made by later commentators.

Core Idea
A quiddity-concomitant follows from essence; an existence-concomitant follows from individual existence or accident.

This lesson tackles a key division in Islamic logic: the distinction between what necessarily accompanies a thing because of its very essence (quiddity concomitant) versus what accompanies it only because of its existence in the external world or as an individual (existence concomitant). The author provides examples, but al-Dawwani and Abd al-Hakim raise important objections that refine the division. By understanding this, you will sharpen your ability to analyze definitions and identify which attributes are truly essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the opening definition: the necessary concomitant (al-lāzim) is divided into concomitant of existence (lāzim al-wujūd) and concomitant of quiddity (lāzim al-māhiyya). Note the examples: blackness for the Abyssinian (existence), evenness for four (quiddity).
Existence here includes external and mental existence.
2
Examine the existence-concomitant example
The author argues that blackness is not part of the quiddity of man (otherwise all men would be black), so it must be a concomitant of existence. But al-Dawwani objects: blackness does not even accompany all existence of man (white men exist). So the example is problematic.
Think about what 'existence' means: individual existence or species existence?
3
Follow al-Dawwani's critique
Al-Dawwani suggests that blackness is actually a concomitant of the specific quiddity (the species Abyssinian) under external existence. This breaks the neat division, as the example now seems to mix both types.
Notice how the critique forces a more precise understanding of the categories.
4
Understand Abd al-Hakim's defense
Abd al-Hakim reinterprets 'concomitant of existence' as the concomitant of quiddity considered under the condition of external existence. He gives examples: occupying space (absolute) or blackness for the Abyssinian (with an accident). He also clarifies that the logician mainly cares about quiddity-concomitants for definitions.
This explanation reconciles the author's division with al-Dawwani's objection.
5
Grasp al-Dawwani's final refinement
Al-Dawwani then proposes that 'concomitant of quiddity' means concomitant of the species (naw'), and 'concomitant of existence' means concomitant of the person (shakhs). Blackness for the Abyssinian is thus a concomitant of the species (Abyssinian) and hence of the person, not of the quiddity of man.
This introduces a new division: concomitant necessary for both existences (mental and external) vs. for a specific existence.

اللَّازِمُ إِمَّا لازِم الوجودِ كَالسَّوادِ لِلحَبشي ، فَإِنَّهُ لَازمٌ لِوجودِهِ وشخصِهِ؛ لَا لِمَا هِيَّتِهِ ؛ لأنَّ ما هيَّتَهُ الإنسانُ ، ولو كانَ السَّوادُ لازماً للإنسان؛ لكانَ كلَّ إنسان أسودَ ، وليس كذلك، وإمَّا لازِم الماهية كالزَّوجيَّةِ للأربعة . . .هـ. قالَ الدَّوَّاني : وأنت تعلم أنَّ السَّوادَ كَمَا لا يلزمُ ماهيَّةَ الإنسان؛ لا يلزم وجودَهُ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ الإنسان الأبيض كثير، بلْ إِنَّما يلزمُ الماهيَّةَ الصِنفيَّةَ، أعني : الحبشي، بحسب وجودِهَا في الخارج، فَيَصيرُ كلامُهُ بِحَسبِ الظَّاهِرِ في قوَّةِ السَّوادِ ليس لازماً لِمَاهِيَّةِ الإنسان، بل هو لازم لوجودِ الصِّنْفِ الَّذي تحتَهَا ، وَلَا يَخفَى عدم انتظامه وفواتُ المقابلة بين لازم الماهية ولازم الوجودِ، فإنَّ اللَّائِقَ بالمقام إيراد أمرٍ لا يكونُ لازماً للماهية ويكونُ لازِمَاً لوجودِ تلكَ الماهية ا.هـ. وأجاب عبد الحكيم بأنَّ مَعنى لازم الوجودِ؛ لازم الماهية باعتبار وجودِهَا الخارجيّ إمَّا مُطلقاً : كَالتَّحيُّ ، أو مأخوذاً بعارض : كَالسَّوادِ لِلحبشي ؛ فَإِنَّهُ لازم لِمَاهِيَّةِ الإنسان باعتبار وجودِهِ وتَشخُصِهِ الصِّنفي؛ لَا لِلمَاهِيَّةِ مِنْ حيثُ هِيَ، وَلَا مِنْ حيثُ الوجودُ مُطلقاً، وإلا ؛ لكان جميع أفرادِهِ أسود، أو باعتبار وجودِهَا الذهني؛ بأنْ يكونَ إدراكُهَا مُستلزماً لإدراكه ؛ إمَّا مُطلقاً، وإمَّا مَأخوذاً باعتبارِ عارض خارج عَنِ الماهيّة، وإنَّما لم يتعرّض لاستيفاء أقسام لازم الوجود، بل اكتفى بإيراد مثال للازم الوجود الخارجي المخصوص الَّذي هُوَ أَخفَى ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ مَظَنَّةُ الحِكْمَةِ، لا يتعلَّقُ غرضُ المنطقي ؛ أعني : الاكتساب بِهِ فإنَّ الكاسبَ لازم الماهيَّةِ، إِذْ هو المستعمل في الحدودِ وإِنَّما ذكر لازم الوجودِ استطراداً . .ا.هـ. ثمَّ قالَ الدَّوَّانِيُّ : والتَّحقيقُ أَنَّهُ يريدُ بِلازم الماهيَّة ؛ لازمَ النَّوع ، وَبِلازم الوجودِ ؛ لازم الشَّخصِ، فَإِنَّ السَّوادَ لِلحبشي إنَّما يلزمُ صنفيَّتَهُ الَّتي هِيَ مِنْ جملةِ مَا اعتبر في تَشخُصِهِ، فيكونُ لازماً لِتَشخُصِهِ؛ لَا لِمَاهِيَّتِهِ، وفي العبارة المنقولة إشعار بذلك؛ حيثُ قالَ : لوجودِهِ وتَشخُصِهِ، فهذا تقسيم آخَرُ سوى التقسيم الذي ذكرناه، فإنَّ مُحصل هذا التقسيم أَنَّ اللَّازِمَ إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ لازماً لكلا الوجودَيْنِ، أو لوجودٍ مُعيَّنٍ، فَهُما تَقسيمانِ مُتغايرانِ، إِلَّا أَنَّ القسم الأوَّلَ في كليهما يُسمَّى لازم الماهية ا.هـ. قال عبد الحكيم : ويرد عليهِ أنَّ المقسَّمَ لَازم الماهية؛ فكيف يندرج فيه لازمُ الشَّخص؟ وأنَّ التَّقسيمَ غَيرُ حاصرٍ ؛ لأنَّ اللَّازِمَ بِاعتبارِ الوجودين ليس لازماً لِلنَّوعِ وَلَا لِلشَّخص ا.هـ.
The necessary concomitant is either a concomitant of existence, like blackness for the Abyssinian, for it is necessary for his existence and person, not for his quiddity; because his quiddity is man, and if blackness were necessary for man, then every man would be black, but it is not so. Or a concomitant of quiddity, like evenness for four. ... Al-Dawwani said: You know that blackness, just as it does not necessarily accompany the quiddity of man, it also does not necessarily accompany his existence either, because a white man is abundant. Rather, it necessarily accompanies the specific quiddity, meaning the Abyssinian, according to its existence externally. So his statement apparently implies that blackness is not necessary for the quiddity of man, but rather necessary for the existence of the species under it. And it is not hidden that this is not consistent and that the comparison between concomitant of quiddity and concomitant of existence is lost. For what befits the context is to mention something that is not necessary for the quiddity but necessary for the existence of that quiddity. ... Abd al-Hakim answered: The meaning of concomitant of existence is the concomitant of quiddity considered from the perspective of its external existence, either absolutely, like occupying space, or taken with an accident, like blackness for the Abyssinian; for it is necessary for the quiddity of man considered from the perspective of his existence and specific individuation, not for the quiddity as such, nor for existence absolutely, otherwise all its individuals would be black. Or considered from the perspective of its mental existence, such that its apprehension necessitates the apprehension of it, either absolutely or taken with an accidental consideration external to the quiddity. He did not aim to cover all divisions of concomitant of existence, but sufficed with giving an example of the specific external concomitant of existence which is more subtle, because that is the domain of wisdom; the logician's purpose, namely acquisition, does not pertain to it, for what is acquired is the concomitant of quiddity, since that is used in definitions, and he only mentioned concomitant of existence incidentally. ... Then al-Dawwani said: The verification is that by concomitant of quiddity he means concomitant of the species, and by concomitant of existence he means concomitant of the person. For blackness for the Abyssinian necessarily accompanies his species-specificity which is part of what is considered in his individuation, so it is necessary for his individuation, not for his quiddity. And in the transmitted expression there is an indication of that, where he said: for his existence and his individuation. So this is another division besides the division we mentioned. The sum of this division is that the concomitant is either necessary for both existences or for a specific existence. So they are two distinct divisions, except that the first division in both is called concomitant of quiddity. ... Abd al-Hakim said: It is objected to him that what is being divided is the concomitant of quiddity, so how can the concomitant of person be included under it? And that the division is not exhaustive, because the concomitant with respect to both existences is not necessary for the species nor for the person.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion of the division of necessary concomitants (lazim) into two types: concomitant of existence (lazim al-wujud) and concomitant of quiddity (lazim al-mahiyya). The author begins by defining the two types with examples: blackness for the Abyssinian as a concomitant of existence, and evenness for four as a concomitant of quiddity. He justifies the first example by noting that blackness does not follow from the quiddity of man (since not all men are black), but it follows from the existence of a specific individual or class (the Abyssinian). 

Al-Dawwani then raises an objection: blackness does not even necessarily accompany the existence of the Abyssinian in all cases (e.g., a white man is abundant), so the example is not precise. He suggests that blackness is actually a concomitant of the specific quiddity (the species Abyssinian) in external existence. This leads to a breakdown in the neat division, as the example does not clearly contrast a quiddity-concomitant with an existence-concomitant.

Abd al-Hakim responds by reinterpreting 'concomitant of existence' as a concomitant of the quiddity considered under the condition of external existence, either absolutely (like occupying space) or qualified by an accidental attribute (like blackness for the Abyssinian). He further explains that the logician's main concern is with quiddity-concomitants used in definitions, while existence-concomitants are mentioned incidentally. 

Al-Dawwani then offers a refined understanding: 'concomitant of quiddity' means concomitant of the species, and 'concomitant of existence' means concomitant of the person. Blackness for the Abyssinian is a concomitant of the species (the Abyssinian as a type) and thus of the individuation (person) but not of the quiddity (man). He introduces a new division: the concomitant may be necessary for both existences (mental and external) or for a specific existence (either mental or external). 

Abd al-Hakim objects to this new division, arguing that the thing being divided is the concomitant of quiddity, so it cannot include the concomitant of person. Moreover, the division is not exhaustive because a concomitant necessary for both existences is neither purely of the species nor of the person.

Key Terms
اللازم
لازم الوجود
لازم الماهية
الماهية
التشخص

A) The quiddity of the species

C) The external existence only
D) The mental existence only

Key Takeaway
The division between quiddity and existence concomitants is nuanced: a property may appear to be an existence concomitant but actually belong to the quiddity of a species when considered under existence.

Hadd and Rasm

Evident and Non-evident Necessary Concomitants
Commentary explains the division of necessary concomitants into evident and non-evident, with example and objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the division of necessary concomitants into evident and non-evident and defend it against the objection that all mental concomitants are evident.

Core Idea
Not every necessary concomitant of a quiddity is consciously perceived.

This commentary expands on the division of necessary concomitants. The author defines 'evident' and gives an example. He then addresses a potential objection: if a necessary concomitant is mental, shouldn't it always be evident? The resolution clarifies that mental presence does not entail conscious awareness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division being made
The passage divides necessary concomitants (lazim) into two types: evident (bayyin) and non-evident (ghayr bayyin). Note that this division applies to both lazim al-mahiyya and lazim al-wujud.
The commentator emphasizes that this division is universal.
2
Understand the definition of 'evident'
An evident necessary concomitant is one whose conception follows immediately from the conception of its subject alone, without need for proof. The definition is: 'that whose conception follows from the conception of the subject'.
The key phrase is 'from the conception of the subject alone'.
3
Examine the example given
The example is 'two is the double of one'. When you conceive of two and one, you immediately conceive the relationship of doubling. This illustrates an evident concomitant.
Other examples could include correlatives like father and son.
4
Parse the objection
The objection states: if a necessary concomitant is a lazim al-mahiyya, it must exist in the mind whenever the quiddity is conceived. Therefore, it should always be evident (in the specific sense), making the division into evident/non-evident invalid for such concomitants.
This objection questions the basis of the division.
5
Analyze the response
Al-Sayyid replies that mental existence does not guarantee conscious perception. One can conceive a quiddity without being aware of all its necessary concomitants. Thus, a lazim al-mahiyya may be non-evident if the necessary relation is not immediately grasped.
This preserves the validity of the division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ اللازم سواء كانَ لازم الماهيَّة، أو لازم الوجود . (ثمَّ - إِمَّا بَيِّنُ) وهو الَّذِي يَلْزَمُ تَصَوُّرُهُ مِنْ تَصَوِّرِ المَلْزُوْمِ) فقط، كَكُونِ الاثنين ضعف الواحد، (قَوْلُهُ : سَوَاءٌ كَانَ لَازِمَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ :أي: لازماً بالنَّظر للماهِيَّةِ، أو بالنَّظر للموجود. (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَلْزَمُ تَصَوُّرُهُ ... إلخ) (تصوره) بالرفع؛ فاعل (يلزم)، وقوله : (مِن تَصوُّرِ الملزوم مُتعلّق بيلزم ؛ أي: ما يلزمُ مِن تصورِ الملزومِ تصورُه . (قَوْلُهُ: ثُمَّ اللَّازِمُ أي : مُطلقاً كما في شرح المصنفِ لِلأصل، ويدلُّ له كلامه هنا ؛ حيثُ سَكَتَ عن ذكرِ المقسَّم في التقسيم الثَّاني، ولذلكَ عَمَّمَ الشَّارِحُ ، ومِثْلُهُ في شرح الدَّوَّاني، فقال مير زاهد: أشار بِهِ إلى أنَّ الماهية والوجود في هذا التقسيم غير معتبر كما في التقسيم الأوَّلِ، وأنَّ كُلا من المعنيين يصلح لأن يكونَ مُقسَّماً هذا التقسيمِ كَمَا يدلُّ عليه لفظه، أو في كلام المصنفِ ا.هـ. فإنْ قُلْتَ: لازم الماهية من حيثُ هِي؛ يجب أن يكونَ لازماً ذهنياً ; لأنَّ الماهيَّةَ إِذا وُجِدَتْ في الذِّهْنِ؛ وجب أن يوجد ذلك اللازم فيه أيضاً ، فيكونُ لازم الماهية لازماً ذهنيًا قطعاً، فيكونُ بَيْنَاً بالمعنى الأخص ، فَلَا يجوز انقسامه إلى اللازم البَيِّنِ بِالمعنى الأعم وغيرِ البَيِّنِ، وأجابَ السَّيِّدُ بأنَّ الواجب في لازم الماهيَّةِ أنْ يكون بحيثُ إِذا وُجِدَتِ الماهيَّةُ في الذهن؛ كانَتْ مُتَّصِفَةٌ بِهِ، وَلَا يلزمُ مِن ذلكَ أنْ يكونَ اللَّازِمُ مُدركاً مَشعوراً بِهِ، فليسَ كُلُّ مَا كانَ حَاصِلاً للماهية المدركة في الذهن يجبُ أنْ يكونَ مُدركاً، فَإِنَّ كونَ الماهِيَّةِ مُدركةً؛ صِفَةٌ حاصلةٌ لَهَا هناكَ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لا يجبُ الشُّعورُ بِهِ، وَإِلَّا ; لزمَ مِن إدراك أمرٍ واحدٍ إدراك أمورٍ غير متناهية، بل يجوز أن يكون لازم الماهية بحيثُ يلزمُ مِن تَصورِهما الجزمُ بِاللُّزوم بينَهُما ، وأن لا يكون كذلك، فصح الانقسام إلى البَيِّنِ بالمعنى الأعم وغيرِ البَيِّن، ويجوز أن يكون بحيثُ يلزمُ مِن تَصورِ الملزوم؛ أي: الماهية؛ تَصوُّرُهُ، فيكونُ بَيَّنَاً بالمعنى الأخصّ، وأن لا يكون بهذهِ الحيثية ا.هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : كَكَوْنِ الاِثْنَيْنِ ضِعْفَ الوَاحِدِ وَكَأحد المتضايفين بالنسبة إلى الآخَرِ.
Then the necessary concomitant, whether it is necessary of quiddity or necessary of existence. (Then — it is either evident) and it is that whose conception follows from the conception of the subject alone, like that two is the double of one. (His saying: whether it is necessary of quiddity) i.e., necessary with respect to quiddity, or with respect to existence. (His saying: and it is that whose conception follows... etc.) 'its conception' is nominative, subject of 'follows'; and his saying: 'from the conception of the subject' is attached to 'follows'; i.e., that from whose conception of the subject its conception follows. (His saying: Then the necessary) i.e., absolutely, as in the author's commentary on the original. His statement here indicates this, since he omitted mention of the divided term in the second division, so the commentator generalized. Similarly in al-Dawwani's commentary. Mir Zahid said: He indicated by this that quiddity and existence are not considered in this division as in the first division, and that both meanings are suitable to be the divided term for this division, as his wording indicates, or in the author's words. To the end. If you say: The necessary of quiddity in itself must be mental, because when the quiddity exists in the mind, that necessary must also exist in it, so the necessary of quiddity is necessarily mental, so it is evident in the specific sense (bi al-ma'na al-akhass), and therefore it cannot be divided into evident in the general sense (bi al-ma'na al-amm) and non-evident. Al-Sayyid answered: The necessary thing in the necessary of quiddity is that when the quiddity exists in the mind, it is qualified by it, but it does not follow that the necessary is perceived and felt. Not everything that is present to the perceived quiddity in the mind must be perceived. Indeed, the quiddity's being perceived is a quality that exists for it there, yet it is not necessary to be aware of it. Otherwise, perceiving one thing would entail perceiving infinite things. Rather, it is possible for the necessary of quiddity to be such that from their conception the certainty of the necessary relation between them follows, or not. So the division into evident in the general sense and non-evident is valid. And it is possible for it to be such that from the conception of the subject (i.e., the quiddity) its conception follows, then it is evident in the specific sense, or not. To the end. (His saying: like that two is the double of one, and like one of the two correlatives with respect to the other.)
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second division of the necessary concomitant: it is either evident (bayyin) or non-evident (ghayr bayyin). Evident means that its conception follows immediately from the conception of the subject alone, without need for proof. The example given is that two is the double of one (the concept of 'double' is understood as soon as one conceives of two and one). The commentary first clarifies that this division applies universally to both lazim al-mahiyya and lazim al-wujud, unlike the first division which separated them. There is an important objection: a lazim al-mahiyya, being necessarily mental (since when the quiddity is conceived in the mind, its necessary concomitant is also conceived), should always be evident in the specific sense (bayyin bi al-ma'na al-akhass), meaning its conception follows from the conception of the quiddity itself. So how can it be divided into evident and non-evident? The response (from al-Sayyid) is that not every mental attribute is consciously perceived. A quiddity can be perceived without awareness of all its necessary concomitants. For example, one may conceive of 'human' without immediately conceiving of 'risible' (capable of laughter), though risibility is a necessary concomitant of humanity. Therefore, it is possible for a lazim al-mahiyya to be non-evident if the necessary relation is not immediately grasped. This preserves the validity of the division. The passage concludes with additional examples: the correlatives (al-mutadā'ifayn) such as father and son, where conceiving one immediately leads to the concept of the other (though this is a specific case). The commentary emphasizes that the division into evident and non-evident is sound even for lazim al-mahiyya.

Key Terms
بيِّن (bayyin): Evident: a necessary concomitant whose conception follows immediately from the conception of its subject without need for proof. [Note: In earlier logic, bayyin may be subdivided into bayyin bi al-dharuri (self-evident) and bayyin bi al-iktisab (evident through acquisition); here it is about immediate conception.]
لازم الماهية (lazim al-mahiyya): A necessary concomitant that follows from the quiddity (essence) of a thing, e.g., risibility follows from humanity. [Note: Stable.]
تصور (tasawwur): Conception: the mental formation of a concept, as opposed to assent (tasdiq). [Note: Stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The evident necessary concomitant is one whose conception follows from the subject alone; even mental necessary concomitants may not be consciously perceived, preserving the division.

The Most Specific Evident Concomitant
Defines the two types of evident concomitant and distinguishes them via examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the most specific evident concomitant by its condition: conceiving the subject alone suffices to conceive the concomitant.

Core Idea
The most specific evident concomitant is immediately known from the subject alone.

In this lesson, we examine the first type of evident concomitant defined by the logicians: the one that follows from merely conceiving the subject. This type is used in implicative signification (dalāla iltizāmiyya) and is the stricter sense. We will see its definition and a classic example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
The passage divides the concomitant into evident and non-evident. Focus on the evident type.
2
Extract the condition for the most specific
The text says: 'whose conception follows from conceiving only the subject.' This is the defining condition.
No additional knowledge is needed beyond the subject itself.
3
Examine the example
Two being double of one: from conceiving 'two' alone, you immediately conceive 'double of one'. Verify this.
4
Note the usage
The passage states that this sense is the one considered in implicative signification by the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn).
Implicative signification is central in logic and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ اللَّازِمُ سَوَاءً كَانَ لَازِمَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ أَوْ لَازِمَ الْوُجُودِ. إِمَّا بَيِّنٌ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَلْزَمُ تَصَوُّرُهُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْمَلْزُومِ فَقَطْ كَكَوْنِ الِاثْنَيْنِ ضِعْفَ الْوَاحِدِ. فَإِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الاثْنَيْنِ فَقَطْ تَصَوُّرُهُ لِأَنَّ مَنْ أَدْرَكَ الِاثْنَيْنِ أَدْرَكَ أَنَّهُمَا ضِعْفُ الْوَاحِدِ وَهَذَا هُوَ اللُّزُومُ الْبَيِّنُ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَصِّ الْمُعْتَبَرِ فِي الدَّلَالَةِ الِالْتِزَامِيَّةِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ. وَاللَّازِمُ الْبَيِّنُ يُطْلَقُ بِالاشْتِرَاكِ عَلَى مَا يَلْزَمُ تَصَوُّرُهُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْمَلْزُومِ فَقَطْ وَهُوَ اللُّزُومُ الْبَيِّنُ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَصِّ وَعَلَى مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ اللَّازِمِ وَالْمَلْزُومِ جَزْمُ الْعَقْلِ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا كَالِانْقِسَامِ بِمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ لِلْأَرْبَعَةِ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ فَقَطْ تَصَوُّرُ الِانْقِسَامِ لَكِنْ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ وَتَصَوُّرِ الِانْقِسَامِ جَزْمُ الْعَقْلِ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا وَهَذَا هُوَ اللُّزُومُ الْبَيِّنُ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمِّ.
Then the concomitant, whether it is a concomitant of the essence or a concomitant of existence. It is either evident, which is that whose conception follows from conceiving only the subject, like two being double of one. For it is a concomitant that conceiving only two gives its conception, because whoever perceives two perceives that they are double of one. This is the evident concomitance in the most specific sense, considered in implicative signification according to the verifiers. And the evident concomitant is applied by homonymy to that whose conception follows from conceiving only the subject — that is the evident concomitance in the most specific sense — and to that from conceiving the concomitant and the subject there follows certainty of the mind of the concomitance between them, like divisibility into two equals for four. For from conceiving only four, the conception of divisibility does not follow, but from conceiving four and conceiving divisibility follows certainty of the mind of the concomitance between them. This is the evident concomitance in the more general sense.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage defines the division of the concomitant (lāzim) into evident (bayyin) and non-evident. The evident is further subdivided into two types: (1) the most specific sense (al-maʿnā al-akhass), where merely conceiving the subject yields the conception of the concomitant — e.g., from conceiving two, one immediately conceives that it is double of one. This is the kind used in implicative signification (dalāla iltizāmiyya). (2) The more general sense (al-maʿnā al-aʿamm), where conceiving the subject alone does not give the concomitant, but conceiving both subject and concomitant together yields certainty (jazm) of the relation between them — e.g., from conceiving four and conceiving divisibility into two equal parts, the mind is certain that four implies that property. The author notes that the term 'evident concomitant' is applied homonymously to these two types. The commentary in the margin (by al-Dasūqī and al-ʿAṭṭār) clarifies that the distinction is not a genuine generality but a conventional labeling, and that the evident concomitant in the specific sense is the one relevant to implicative signification in logic.

بين
ملزوم

D) From conceiving 'smoke', one conceives 'fire'.

Key Takeaway
The most specific evident concomitant is immediately conceived from the subject alone, as 'double of one' from 'two'.
