The More General Evident Concomitant
Defines the two types of evident concomitant and distinguishes them via examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the more general evident concomitant and distinguish it from the specific sense by its need for both terms to be conceived to produce certainty.

Core Idea
The more general evident concomitant requires conceiving both subject and concomitant for certainty.

In this lesson, we explore the second type of evident concomitant, where merely conceiving the subject is insufficient; one must also conceive the concomitant itself, and then the mind becomes certain of the relation. This type is called the more general sense (al-maʿnā al-aʿamm).

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types
The passage divides evident concomitant into two homonymous senses. Focus on the second.
2
Extract the condition for the more general
The text says: 'from conceiving the concomitant and the subject there follows certainty of the mind (jazm al-ʿaql) of the concomitance between them.'
Certainty here is a mental judgment, not just conception.
3
Examine the example
Four and divisibility into two equal parts: conceiving 'four' alone does not give divisibility; but when you also conceive divisibility, you become certain that four necessarily has that property.
4
Note the difference from the specific sense
The specific sense gives conception alone; the general sense gives certainty after both terms are known. The passage notes homonymous usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ اللَّازِمُ سَوَاءً كَانَ لَازِمَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ أَوْ لَازِمَ الْوُجُودِ. إِمَّا بَيِّنٌ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَلْزَمُ تَصَوُّرُهُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْمَلْزُومِ فَقَطْ كَكَوْنِ الِاثْنَيْنِ ضِعْفَ الْوَاحِدِ. فَإِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الاثْنَيْنِ فَقَطْ تَصَوُّرُهُ لِأَنَّ مَنْ أَدْرَكَ الِاثْنَيْنِ أَدْرَكَ أَنَّهُمَا ضِعْفُ الْوَاحِدِ وَهَذَا هُوَ اللُّزُومُ الْبَيِّنُ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَصِّ الْمُعْتَبَرِ فِي الدَّلَالَةِ الِالْتِزَامِيَّةِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ. وَاللَّازِمُ الْبَيِّنُ يُطْلَقُ بِالاشْتِرَاكِ عَلَى مَا يَلْزَمُ تَصَوُّرُهُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْمَلْزُومِ فَقَطْ وَهُوَ اللُّزُومُ الْبَيِّنُ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَخَصِّ وَعَلَى مَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ اللَّازِمِ وَالْمَلْزُومِ جَزْمُ الْعَقْلِ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا كَالِانْقِسَامِ بِمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ لِلْأَرْبَعَةِ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ فَقَطْ تَصَوُّرُ الِانْقِسَامِ لَكِنْ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ وَتَصَوُّرِ الِانْقِسَامِ جَزْمُ الْعَقْلِ بِاللُّزُومِ بَيْنَهُمَا وَهَذَا هُوَ اللُّزُومُ الْبَيِّنُ بِالْمَعْنَى الْأَعَمِّ.
Then the concomitant, whether it is a concomitant of the essence or a concomitant of existence. It is either evident, which is that whose conception follows from conceiving only the subject, like two being double of one. For it is a concomitant that conceiving only two gives its conception, because whoever perceives two perceives that they are double of one. This is the evident concomitance in the most specific sense, considered in implicative signification according to the verifiers. And the evident concomitant is applied by homonymy to that whose conception follows from conceiving only the subject — that is the evident concomitance in the most specific sense — and to that from conceiving the concomitant and the subject there follows certainty of the mind of the concomitance between them, like divisibility into two equals for four. For from conceiving only four, the conception of divisibility does not follow, but from conceiving four and conceiving divisibility follows certainty of the mind of the concomitance between them. This is the evident concomitance in the more general sense.

The commentary in the margin (by al-Dasūqī and al-ʿAṭṭār) clarifies that the distinction is not a genuine generality but a conventional labeling, and that the evident concomitant in the specific sense is the one relevant to implicative signification in logic.

Types of Evident and Non-Evident Necessary Concomitance
Author defines three types of necessary concomitance with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of necessary concomitance (evident-specific, evident-general, non-evident) and classify given examples accordingly.

Core Idea
Necessary concomitance either springs from a single concept, from two concepts, or needs external proof.

In this passage, the author breaks down the concept of necessary concomitance (luzum) into three precise categories. Understanding these distinctions is key to evaluating arguments based on implicative signification (dalala iltizamiyya). The examples of 'two being double of one', 'four being divisible into two', and 'the world being temporal' illustrate how the mind's judgment varies depending on the clarity of the entailment. Let's walk through each type step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first type: evident in the more specific sense
When merely conceiving the subject (two) immediately yields the predicate (double of one). No other concept is needed.
This is the strongest form of luzum.
2
Understand the second type: evident in the more general sense
When conceiving both subject (four) and predicate (divisible) together is required for the mind to affirm the entailment. Neither alone suffices.
Example: four and division into two.
3
Understand the third type: non-evident
Even when both terms are conceived (world, temporality), the mind does not spontaneously judge the relation; an external intermediary (change) is needed.
This type requires a syllogism or other evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات فإنَّه لازم يلزم من تصوُّر الاثنين فقط تصوُّرُه؛ لأنَّ مَن أدركَ الاثنين؛ أدركَ أنَّهما ضعفُ الواحد، وهذا هو اللُّزوم البين بالمعنى الأخص المعتبر في الدَّلالةِ الالتزاميَّةِ عند المحققين. (أَوْ) يلزمُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِهِمَا)؛ أي: تصوّرِ اللازم والملزومِ، (الجَزْمُ) فاعلُ يلزم المقدَّر؛ أي: اللازمُ البيِّنُ يُطلَقُ بالاشتراكِ على ما يلزمُ تصوره من تصوُّرِ الملزوم فقط، وهوَ اللُّزوم البيّن بالمعنى الأخصّ، وعلى ما يلزم من تصورِ اللازم والملزومِ جَزْمُ العقلِ بِاللُّزُومِ) بينهما، كالانقسام بمتساويين للأربعة، فإنَّه لا يلزم من تصوُّرِ الأربعةِ فَقط تصوُّرُ الانقسام، لكن يلزمُ من تصوُّرِ الأربعةِ وتصورِ الانقسامِ جَزْمُ العقل باللُّزوم بينهما، وهذا هوَ اللُّزوم البين بالمعنى الأعم. (أَوْ غَيْرُ بَيِّنِ بالرَّفْعِ، عطفٌ على قوله: «بين»؛ أي: اللازم إِمَّا بيَّن وإِمَّا غيرُ بَيِّن (وَهُوَ بِخِلَافِهِ)؛ أي: بخلاف البيّن. (قَوْلُهُ: وَهُوَ بِخِلَافِهِ) أي: فهو ما لا يلزمُ مِن تَصوُّرِ اللَّازم والملزومِ جزمُ العقلِ بلزومه، بل لا بُدَّ في جزم العقل بلزومِهِ مِن واسطةٍ زيادة على تصورِ اللازم والملزوم، وذلك كَلزومِ الحدوثِ لِلعالَم، فإنَّ جزمَ العقلِ بِهِ يتوقَّفُ على أمرٍ خارج وهو التَّغير؛ إذْ لَا يلزمُ مِن تَصوُّرِ الحدوثِ والعالَمِ جزم العقل بلزومِ الحدوثِ للعالم، فهذا لازم غيرُ بَيْن.
Section: On the principles of concepts. For it is a necessary concomitant whose conception follows merely from conceiving the two [i.e., two and one]; because whoever perceives two perceives that they are twice the one, and this is the evident necessary concomitance in the more specific sense considered in implicative signification according to the verifiers. (Or) there follows from conceiving them both—i.e., conceiving the necessary concomitant and that which it accompanies—(the judgment) [as the subject of the estimated verb 'follows']; i.e., 'evident necessary concomitant' is used homonymously for that whose conception follows from conceiving the accompanied only—this is the evident necessary concomitance in the more specific sense—and for that from whose conceiving both the necessary concomitant and its accompanied the mind's judgment of the concomitance follows, like the division into two equals for four, for the conception of the division does not follow from merely conceiving four, but from conceiving four and conceiving division the mind's judgment of the concomitance between them follows—this is the evident necessary concomitance in the more general sense. (Or non-evident, in the nominative, coordinated with his statement 'evident'; i.e., the necessary concomitant is either evident or non-evident. (And it is the opposite of it); i.e., opposite of the evident. (His statement: And it is the opposite of it) i.e., it is that from whose conceiving both the necessary concomitant and its accompanied the mind's judgment of the concomitance does not follow; rather, for the mind's judgment of its concomitance, there must be an intermediary in addition to conceiving the necessary concomitant and its accompanied, such as the temporality's concomitance for the world, for the mind's judgment of it depends on an external matter which is change; since from conceiving temporality and the world, the mind's judgment that temporality necessarily accompanies the world does not follow. So this is a non-evident necessary concomitant.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage defines types of necessary concomitance (luzum) relevant to concepts, a key topic in the principles of conceptualization (mabadi' al-tasawwurat). The text begins by explaining one type of evident necessary concomitant (luzum bayyin) in the more specific sense (bi al-ma'na al-akhass): when merely conceiving the subject (e.g., two) yields the conception of the predicate (e.g., being twice one). The example given: whoever understands 'two' immediately understands that it is double of one. This is the strongest type of concomitance used in implicative signification (dalala iltizamiyya) according to the muhaqqiqun (verifiers). Then it introduces a second type of evident necessary concomitant (luzum bayyin bi al-ma'na al-a'amm): here, conceiving both terms (subject and predicate) together is required for the mind to judge that the predicate necessarily accompanies the subject. The example is 'four' and its 'division into two equal halves.' Merely conceiving 'four' does not give the idea of division; but when one conceives both 'four' and 'division,' the mind immediately affirms the relation. Finally, the text discusses the non-evident necessary concomitant (luzum ghayr bayyin): even after conceiving both terms, the mind does not spontaneously judge the entailment; an external intermediary (wasita) is needed. The example is the world's temporality (huduth): from just the concepts 'world' and 'temporality' the mind does not judge that the world is temporal; additional evidence (like change or dependence on a cause) is required. This passage thus distinguishes three types of necessary concomitance, crucial for understanding how definitions and arguments based on conceptual content work in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
اللزوم البين بالمعنى الأخص
اللزوم البين بالمعنى الأعم
اللزوم غير البين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Necessary concomitance varies in evidentness: some relations are grasped from a single concept, others from two, while some require external proof – a critical nuance for evaluating conceptual arguments.

The Evident and Non-Evident Concomitant
Division of lazim into evident and non-evident with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an evident (bayyin) and a non-evident (ghayr bayyin) necessary concomitant and identify which type applies to given examples from Islamic kalam.

Core Idea
An evident concomitant is known by mere conceptualization; a non-evident one needs a middle term or other evidence.

In this passage, the author divides the necessary concomitant (lazim) into two types: evident (bayyin) and non-evident (ghayr bayyin). This division is crucial for evaluating proofs in kalam and usul al-fiqh, as some inferences are immediate while others require intermediate steps. The classic example given is the temporality (huduth) of the world, which is non-evident because it requires the concept of change to be understood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Find the terms 'bayyin' and 'ghayr bayyin' in the Arabic text. Notice that they are presented as a division of 'lazim' (necessary concomitant).
The division is introduced by 'إِمَّا بيَّن وإِمَّا غيرُ بَيِّن'.
2
Extract the condition for evident (bayyin)
The text states that for an evident concomitant, merely conceptualizing the two sides (the subject and the concomitant) is sufficient for the mind to judge the concomitance. Write down this condition.
The term used is 'تصور الطرفين'.
3
Extract the condition for non-evident (ghayr bayyin)
For a non-evident concomitant, the mind requires something additional beyond conceptualization, such as a middle term, sense, experience, or intuition. Identify this condition and note the example provided (the world's temporality requires 'change' as a middle term).
The text says 'لا بد في جزم العقل بلزومه من واسطة'.
4
Apply the definitions to a new Islamic example
Consider the statement: 'The Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud) exists.' Is the existence of the Necessary Being an evident or non-evident concomitant of its essence? Reflect on whether mere conceptualization suffices or whether additional reasoning is needed.
In Islamic kalam, the existence of the Necessary Being is often considered self-evident in the sense that its essence entails existence, but some arguments treat it as non-evident.
5
Verify the example with the conditions
For the world's temporality, the text explicitly says that mere conceptualization of 'world' and 'origination' does not yield the judgment; the mind needs the concept of 'change' (taghayyur). Confirm that this matches the non-evident condition.
The phrase 'لأنه متغير' indicates the middle term 'change'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ غَيْرُ بَيِّنِ بالرَّفْعِ، عطفٌ على قوله : «بين»؛ أي: اللازم إِمَّا بيَّن - وهو ما ذكرنا - وإِمَّا غيرُ بَيِّن (وَهُوَ بِخِلَافِهِ)؛ أي : بخلاف البيّن . وقال بعضُهم : اللازم الأعم ؛ ما جزم العقلُ بلزومه عند تصورِ الطَّرفين ؛ سواة كانَ تَصوُّرُ الملزوم كافياً في جزم العقل بلزومِهِ أو لا، واللازم البيِّنُ بالمعنى الأخص ; ما كان تصور الملزوم كافياً في جزم العَقلِ بلزومه، وعلى هذا فالتسمية بأخصّ وأعم ظاهرة.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ بِخِلَافِهِ) أي : فهو ما لا يلزمُ مِن تَصوُّرِ اللَّازم والملزومِ جزمُ العقلِ بلزومه، بل لا بُدَّ في جزم العقل بلزومِهِ مِن واسطةٍ زيادة على تصورِ اللازم والملزوم، وذلك كَلزومِ الحدوثِ لِلعالَم، فإنَّ جزمَ العقلِ بِهِ يتوقَّفُ على أمرٍ خارج وهو التغير ; إذْ لَا يلزمُ مِن تَصوُّرِ الحدوثِ والعالَمِ جزم العقل بلزومِ الحدوثِ للعالم، فهذا لازم غيرُ بَيْن .
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ غَيْرُ بَيّن) وَهُوَ مَا افتقر إلى وسط قالَ الرَّازِيُّ : وَهَهُنَا نَظَرُ ، وهو الوسط على ما فسَّرَهُ القومُ ; مَا يُقرَنُ بِقَولِنَا : لأنَّهُ حينَ يُقالُ : لأَنَّهُ كَذَا ، مثلاً إذا قُلْنَا : العالَمُ مُحدَثٌ لأنَّهُ مُتغيّر ; فَالمقارنُ لِقولِنَا : لأنَّهُ ، وهو المتغير وسط، وليس يلزم من عدم افتقار اللزومِ إلى وسط أنَّه يكفي فيهِ مُجرَّدُ تَصوُّرِ اللازم والملزوم؛ لِجَوازِ تَوقُفِهِ على شيءٍ آخَرَ مِنْ حدس أو تجربة أو حِس أو غيرِ ذَلِكَ، فَلَو اعتبرنا الافتقار إلى وسط في مفهوم غيرِ البَيِّنِ ; لم ينحصر لازم الماهية في البَيِّن وغيره .ا.هـ. قالَ السَّيِّدُ : وَمَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّ مَقصودَهُم مَنْعُ الجمع لا الانفصال الحقيقي؛ لم يأتِ بِمَا يُعتَدُّ بِهِ لِفَواتِ الانضباط حينئذ ا.هـ. يعني أَنَّهُ قدْ قِيْلَ: إِنَّ المنفصلة الواقعة في التقسيم مانعةُ الجمع التي يمكن عدمُ تَحقُّقِ طَرفَيْهَا، فيمكنُ أنْ يكونَ هناكَ قِسْمٌ ثالث، لا أنَّها مُنفصلةً حقيقية لا يمكن عدمُ تَحقُّقِ طَرفَيْهَا ; بَلْ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَحقُّقِ واحدٍ مِنْهُمَا، فَلَا يمكن قسم ثالثُ، وهو كلامٌ بعيدٌ عَنِ التَّحقيقِ; فَإِنَّ انضباط الأقسام مقصود في التقسيم، وعلى تقدير أن تكون المنفصلة مانعة جمع ; يفوتُ ذلك .ا.هـ. والمصنِّفُ لم يعتبر في غيرِ البَيِّن الافتقار إلى الوسط; ليشمل المستقرَّ إلى غير الوسط وينحصر اللازم في القسمين المذكورين.
Or 'non-evident' in the nominative, as a conjunct to his statement 'evident'; that is: the necessary concomitant is either evident – which we mentioned – or non-evident (and it is the opposite of it); i.e., opposite of the evident. And some said: the more general necessary concomitant is that the mind judges its concomitance upon conceptualizing the two sides, whether the conceptualization of the subject is sufficient for the mind to judge its concomitance or not, and the evident in the more specific sense is that whose conceptualization of the subject is sufficient for the mind to judge its concomitance, and accordingly the naming as more specific and more general is apparent.
(His statement: and it is the opposite of it) i.e., it is that from the conceptualization of the concomitant and the subject, the mind does not necessarily judge its concomitance, but rather for the mind to judge its concomitance, a middle term is necessary in addition to the conceptualization of the concomitant and the subject. That is like the concomitance of temporality to the world, for the mind's judgment of it depends on an external matter which is change; since from the conceptualization of temporality and the world, the mind does not necessarily judge the concomitance of temporality to the world. So this is a non-evident concomitant.
(His statement: or non-evident) and it is what is in need of a middle term. Al-Razi said: there is a consideration here, and the middle term as explained by the people is that which is conjoined with our statement 'because'. For example, when we say: the world is originated because it is changing; then what is conjoined with our statement 'because' – and it is change – is the middle term. And it does not follow from the non-need of the concomitance for a middle term that mere conceptualization of the concomitant and the subject suffices; because it may depend on something else like conjecture, experience, sense, or other than that. So if we considered the need for a middle term in the concept of non-evident, then the necessary concomitant of the quiddity would not be confined to evident and non-evident. End quote. Al-Sayyid said: and whoever claims that their purpose is to prevent conjunction, not real disjunction, has not brought what is reliable because then precision is lost. End quote. He means that it was said: the disjunctive occurring in the division is of the type that prevents conjunction, whose two sides can both be unrealized, so a third division is possible, not that it is a real disjunction whose two sides cannot both be unrealized, but rather one of them must be realized, so no third division is possible. This is a statement far from verification; because precision of divisions is intended in division, and if it is assumed to be a disjunction that prevents conjunction, that precision is lost. End quote. And the author did not consider need for a middle term in non-evident, to include what is based on other than the middle term and so that the necessary concomitant is confined to the two mentioned divisions.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage defines and expands the division of the necessary concomitant (lazim) into two types: evident (bayyin) and non-evident (ghayr bayyin). It begins by stating the basic division: the concomitant is either evident (bayyin) or non-evident (ghayr bayyin). The author clarifies that 'evident' here is in the more specific sense, meaning that merely conceptualizing the subject (malzum) and the concomitant itself sufficies for the mind to judge that the concomitant necessarily follows. In contrast, the 'non-evident' requires something additional (a middle term) for the mind to accept the concomitance. The text then gives an example: the concomitance of temporality (huduth) to the world. The mind does not immediately judge that the world is originated simply from the concepts of 'world' and 'origination'; it needs the intermediate concept of 'change' (taghayyur). Thus, this is a non-evident concomitant. The commentary then quotes al-Razi, who notes a consideration: the middle term is what is attached to the word 'because' (li-annahu) in a proof statement. The text also clarifies that the lack of need for a middle term does not necessarily mean that mere conceptualization suffices; the concomitance may depend on other things like sense, experience, or intuition. The passage also discusses a potential objection: if we define non-evident by need for a middle term, then some concomitants that do not need a verbal middle term (but need something else) would not fall under non-evident, breaking the division. The author (al-Sayyid) and the commentator handle this by noting that the author intentionally did not restrict 'non-evident' to need for a middle term, precisely to include those based on other factors. Finally, the text addresses a subtle point about disjunctive divisions: the division of lazim into evident and non-evident is a real disjunction (with no third division), not a mere preventing of conjunction, because precision of divisions is crucial.

Key Terms
اللازم
البيِّن
غير البيِّن
الوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes an evident concomitant (lazim bayyin) from a non-evident one (lazim ghayr bayyin)?
A) The evident concomitant is always true, while the non-evident may be false.
B) The evident concomitant is known by the mind without any additional evidence, while the non-evident requires a middle term or other evidence.
C) The evident concomitant is separable from the subject, while the non-evident is not.
D) The evident concomitant is found only in material things, while the non-evident is in abstract concepts.

Key Takeaway
The necessary concomitant is divided into evident (immediately known) and non-evident (requiring evidence), a distinction that underlies many proofs in Islamic kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the statement: 'The world is originated because it is changing.' Identify the middle term and explain why this makes the temporality of the world a non-evident concomitant.
?
Provide another example from Islamic theology where a concomitant is evident (e.g., the necessity of existence for the Necessary Being). Justify your choice using the definitions from this lesson.

5 min
The Role of the Middle Term and the Nature of the Division
Discussion of middle term and disjunction type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Razi's consideration regarding the middle term and assess whether the division of lazim into bayyin and ghayr bayyin is a real disjunction.

Core Idea
The division is a real disjunction; precision of categories is preserved by not restricting 'non-evident' to a verbal middle term.

This lesson examines a subtle debate within the passage. Al-Razi raises a consideration about defining 'non-evident' solely by need for a middle term, and the author clarifies that it includes other types of evidence. Furthermore, al-Sayyid asserts that the division is a real disjunction (mani'ah jami'ah), not just preventing conjunction, to maintain precision.

Guided Path
1
Locate al-Razi's statement
Find the phrase 'قال الرازي: وههنا نظر' in the Arabic text. Read his point about the middle term.
Al-Razi notes that if ghayr bayyin is defined as needing a verbal middle term, then some cases that depend on sense, experience, or intuition would not fit.
2
Understand the implication
If 'non-evident' is strictly defined by a verbal middle term, what would happen to a concomitant that requires only sense experience? It would fall outside both categories, breaking the division.
The text gives examples: حدس (conjecture), تجربة (experience), حس (sense).
3
See the author's solution
The author (al-Sayyid or the commentator) states that the original author did not restrict ghayr bayyin to the middle term. This expands the definition to include any additional evidence, thus preserving the comprehensiveness of the division.
The phrase 'والمصنف لم يعتبر في غير البين الافتقار إلى الوسط' indicates the solution.
4
Analyze al-Sayyid's comment on the disjunction
Read from 'قال السيد: ومن زعم...' to the end. Al-Sayyid rejects the idea that the division is a 'man'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction) only, asserting it is a real disjunction. Explain the difference.
In a real disjunction, the two sides cannot both be absent; one must be true. This ensures no third category.
5
Conclude the importance of precision
Why does al-Sayyid insist on a real disjunction? Because precision of divisions (انضباط الأقسام) is essential in logical division. If it were a mere 'man'at al-jam', a third possibility could exist, undermining the classification.
The text says 'فإن انضباط الأقسام مقصود في التقسيم'.

Objection and Answer: The Separable Accident's Duration
Author presents an objection to the definition of separable accident and provides a response distinguishing potential from actual separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and respond to an objection regarding the definition of a separable accident by distinguishing actual from potential separation.

Core Idea
A separable accident is defined by possibility, not actuality – permanence does not negate separability.

This passage exemplifies a standard dialectical move in Islamic logic: an objector raises a tension between permanence and separability, and the respondent resolves it by clarifying the modal nature of the definition. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to defend definitions against apparent counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening 'فإن قيل' clause. The objector argues that if an accident is permanent, it cannot be separable because separability implies eventual cessation.
The objection is based on a literal reading of 'separable' as 'actually separates'.
2
Locate the answer
After 'قلتَ', the respondent clarifies: by 'separable' we mean separable in possibility (بحسب الإمكان), not in actual fact (بالفعل).
The term 'إمكان' is key: it indicates potentiality.
3
Distinguish the two senses
Separate actuality (الواقع) from possibility (الإمكان). The objector conflates them. The respondent shows that permanence in actuality does not contradict separability in possibility.
This is a classic modal distinction in Islamic logic and ontology.
4
Evaluate the response
Assess whether the response resolves the objection. Yes, because it shifts the definition from occurrence to capacity. The accident remains separable as long as it is possible for it to cease, even if it never does.
The response is valid and preserves the definition's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : العَرَضُ المُفارِقُ كيفَ يدومُ ، فإنَّه لو كان دائماً لم يكن مفارقاً ؟! قلتَ : المراد بالمفارِقِ المفارقُ بحَسَبِ الإمكان، سواة وقعت المفارقة بالفعل، أو لم تقع أصلاً، فالدَّوامُ بحَسَبِ الواقع لا يُنافي المفارقة بحسب الإمكان.
If it is said: How can the separable accident be permanent? For if it were permanent, it would not be separable? I said: By 'separable' is meant separable in terms of possibility, whether separation actually occurs or not at all. So permanence according to reality does not contradict separability according to possibility.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This is a dialectical exchange that addresses a potential objection to the definition of a separable accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-mufāriq). The objection argues that if an accident is permanent, it cannot be separable because separability implies eventual cessation. The response clarifies that separability is a modal property: an accident is considered separable if it is possible for it to cease, even if it never actually does. This preserves the definition by distinguishing between actual permanence and potential separability. For example, whiteness in paper is permanent but can be removed by bleaching agents; thus it remains separable in possibility. The response uses a common logical distinction in Islamic ontology.

Key Terms
العرض المفارق
المفارقة بحسب الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes an accident 'separable' (mufāriq)?
A) It actually separates from its subject.
B) It is possible for it to separate, even if it never does.
C) It is inseparable.
D) It is permanent.

Key Takeaway
A separable accident is defined by the possibility of separation, not by actual occurrence, so permanence does not contradict separability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a property like 'the ability to be burned' for paper. Paper may never burn, but we still call it flammable. Explain how this parallels the separable accident case, using the terms 'bi-ḥasab al-imkān' and 'bil-fi‘l'.
?
In a fiqh context, the definition of 'traveler' (musāfir) for shortening prayer: is it defined by actual distance covered or by the intention to travel a certain distance? Determine which sense is used and argue for its validity based on the modal distinction from this lesson.

The Three Universals: Natural, Logical, and Rational
al-Dasuqi defines the three universals and al-‘Attar refines the explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the natural universal (kullī ṭabī‘ī), logical universal (kullī manṭiqī), and rational universal (kullī ‘aqlī) using the example 'Animal is universal' and identify why the fourth consideration (quiddity alone) is omitted.

Core Idea
One term, three aspects: subject, attribute, and their composite.

When we say 'Animal is universal,' we are doing something more than just describing animal. We are implicitly invoking three distinct conceptual layers: the reality of animal as a subject that receives universality, the attribute of universality itself, and the mental composite of both. This lesson unpacks those layers and clarifies a common confusion about the fourth consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition and its predication type
Locate the example 'الحيوان كلي' (Animal is universal) and note that al-Dasuqi specifies it is a consensual predication (ḥaml muwāṭa'a). Ask yourself: what is being predicated of what?
Consensual predication means the predicate (كلي) and subject (الحيوان) share the same essential meaning at the conceptual level.
2
Extract the three considerations from the statement
From the single statement, recognize three elements: (1) the concept of animal as the subject qualified by universality, (2) the concept of universality as the predicate, and (3) the combination of both as a single composite mental form. These three are the natural, logical, and rational universals respectively.
Don't confuse the composite (rational universal) with just the subject (natural universal).
3
Define each universal using the passage's criteria
Natural universal: the concept of animal insofar as it is qualified by universality. Logical universal: the concept of universality considered by itself, i.e., 'that whose conception does not prevent co-participation'. Rational universal: the composite of both concepts (e.g., 'animal that is universal').
The logical universal is the attribute; it can be predicated of many without regard to what it is predicated of.
4
Understand the fourth omitted consideration
Al-Dasuqi notes that a fourth consideration—the quiddity (māhiyya) of animal considered by itself, without any attribute—is omitted because it is not relevant to the author's purpose. This is the essence stripped of universality.
The quiddity alone is not a universal; it is the raw concept before any qualification.
5
Follow al-‘Attar's critical refinement
al-‘Attar corrects a marginal commentator who restricted the example to when 'animal' is a predicate. He insists that the natural universal is taken as a substrate (ma‘rūḍ), not as an accident. This prevents mislocating the natural universal in the predicate position.
The natural universal is the subject that receives universality, not the attribute itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات اعلَمْ أَنَّ لِلكُلِّي ثلاثَ اعتبارات : قال الدسوقي : قَوْلُهُ : إِعْلَمْ أَنَّ لِلْكُلِّي أي: الواقع محمولاً على شيء حمل مواطأة كـ: «الحيوانُ كُلّي». قَوْلُهُ : ثَلَاثَ اعْتِبَارَاتٍ وبقي اعتبار رابع : وهو ماهية الحيوان من حيث هي، لكنْ لَمَّا لم يكن غرضُهُ مَنوطاً بِهِ ; أسقطه عن درجة الاعتبار . والحاصل : أَنَّكَ إذا قُلْتَ: الحيوانُ كُلّيّ ; كانَ مَفهوم الحيوانِ موصوفاً بالكُلِّيَّةِ، وكلّى وصفاً له، فَمفهوم الحيوانِ من حيثُ كونُهُ موصوفاً بالكُلِّيَّة; كُلّي طبيعي ومفهوم الكُلّيّ الواقع صفةً وهو ما لا يمنعُ نفس تصورِهِ مِن وقوع الشركة فيهِ مِن غيرِ ملاحظةِ كَونِهِ حيواناً أو إنساناً أو غير ذلك; كُلّي منطقي، ومجموع الموصوف وهو الحيوان، والصفة وهو الكُلّيُّ ; أي: الهيئة المركَّبة من مجموع مفهوميْهِمَا ; أعني : الجسمَ النَّامي الحسَّاسَ المتحرك بالإرادة، الذي لا يمنعُ نَفْس تصورِهِ مِن وقوعِ الشركةِ فيه ; كُلّى عقليّ، هذا توضيحه . قال العطار: قَوْلُهُ : لِلْكُلِّي ثَلَاثَ اعْتِبَارَاتٍ قالَ الجماعةُ في هذا المقامِ : إذا قُلْنَا الحيوانُ كُلّيّ ; فَهَهُنَا ثلاثُ اعتبارات ... إلخ، قاصدين التمثيل والتَّوضِيحَ بِذِكْرِ المثالِ، فَفَهِمَ المحشّي التَّقييد فقال : أي : إذا وقع محمولاً على شيءٍ حَمْلَ مُوَاطَأَةٍ كَالحيوانِ كُلِّيَّ ; فتعقب بأنَّه قد وضعَهُ في غير موضعِهِ الَّذي هو قول المصنفِ : مَفهومُ الكُلّيّ لما أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي يُؤخَذُ مِن حيثُ إِنَّهُ مَعروضٌ ; أي: موضوع لا عارض; أي : محمول ولا المجموع ، وقَوْلُ المحشّي أيضاً : بقي اعتبار رابع ، وهو مَاهِيَّةُ الحيوانِ مِنْ حيثُ هِيَ ، لكنْ لَمَّا لم يكن لهم غرضُ مَنُوطٌ بِهِ ; أسقطوه عن درجة الاعتبار .
A Section on the Principles of Conceptualizations. Know that the universal has three considerations. al-Dasuqi said: His statement: Know that the universal, i.e., that which occurs as a predicate on something by way of consensual predication like 'Animal is universal.' His statement: three considerations. A fourth consideration remains: the quiddity of animal considered by itself, but since his purpose was not dependent on it, he dropped it from the level of consideration. The conclusion: When you say 'Animal is universal,' the concept of animal is qualified by universality, and 'universal' is a description of it. So the concept of animal insofar as it is qualified by universality is the natural universal. The concept of the universal that occurs as a description—which is that the very conception of it does not prevent co-participation in it, without observing whether it is animal or human or otherwise—is the logical universal. The combination of the described (animal) and the description (universal), i.e., the composite form composed of both concepts, namely the body growing, sensing, moving by will, whose very conception does not prevent co-participation in it, is the rational universal. This is its clarification. al-‘Attar said: His statement: The universal has three considerations. The group (of scholars) in this place said: if we say 'Animal is universal,' then here are three considerations, etc., intending illustration and clarification by mentioning the example. Then the marginal commentator understood the restriction and said: i.e., when it occurs as a predicate on something by way of consensual predication like 'Animal is universal.' Then it was objected that he placed it in other than its proper place which is the statement of the author: the concept of the universal, because the natural universal is taken insofar as it is a substrate, i.e., subject, not an accident, i.e., predicate, nor the composite. And the statement of the marginal commentator also: A fourth consideration remains: the quiddity of animal considered by itself, but since they had no purpose dependent on it, they dropped it from the level of consideration.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage defines the three considerations of the universal (kulli) and discusses a fourth that is omitted. It is a definitional passage with a critical refinement by al-‘Attar. The author begins by stating that the universal has three considerations. Al-Dasuqi explains that the phrase 'the universal' in the matn refers to something that occurs as a predicate via consensual predication (haml muwāṭa'a), like 'Animal is universal.' He then notes that a fourth consideration remains: the quiddity (māhiyya) of animal considered by itself, but it is dropped because the author's purpose does not depend on it. The 'conclusion' (al-ḥāṣil) then distinguishes the three: the natural universal (kullī ṭabī‘ī) is the concept of animal considered as qualified by universality—it is the subject that receives the attribute of universality. The logical universal (kullī manṭiqī) is the concept of universality itself, which is an attribute that does not prevent co-participation (shirka) in its conception, regardless of what it is predicated of. The rational universal (kullī ‘aqlī) is the composite of both subject and attribute, i.e., the actual concept of 'animal' plus the attribute of universality, resulting in a compound concept that also does not prevent co-participation. Al-‘Attar then provides a critical refinement: some scholars (al-jamā‘a) explained the three considerations using the example 'Animal is universal.' The marginal commentator (al-muḥashshī) understood a restriction that this only applies when 'animal' is a predicate via consensual predication. Al-‘Attar objects that this placement is incorrect because the natural universal is taken as a substrate (ma‘rūḍ) not as an accident (māhiyya), and the fourth consideration (the quiddity itself) is omitted as not relevant to the purpose. This guards against confusing the natural universal (subject) with the logical universal (attribute) or the composite rational universal. All examples must be from Islamic sciences, so we can note: when we say 'Man is an animal,' the concept 'animal' is a natural universal when considered as a reality, the logical universal is the concept 'animal' as a predicate that applies to many, and the rational universal is the combined concept 'animal capable of rationality'.

Key Terms
كلي طبيعي
كلي منطقي
كلي عقلي
حمل مواطأة

Key Takeaway
The same universal term has three distinct aspects: the subject that receives universality (natural), the attribute of universality (logical), and the mental composite of both (rational).

The Subject of Universality: Natural Universal vs Logical Universal
Author defines the second principle of concepts: the subject of universality and the natural universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the natural universal (كلي طبيعي) and the logical universal (كلي منطقي) by identifying their respective definitions and aspects.

Core Idea
The natural universal is the subject of the logical universal, not the essence itself.

In this passage, the author defines the second principle of concepts: the subject of universality (معروض الكلية). You will learn that the natural universal is not the concept of universality but the individuals or natures that bear that concept. This distinction is crucial for understanding how definitions work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term 'معروض' (subject) in the Arabic. It is defined as 'that to which universality attaches'. Note that it is the second principle of concepts.
2
Extract the definition of the natural universal
The natural universal is 'the subject of the logical universal', i.e., the individuals (like 'human', 'animal') that fall under the logical universal. But it is not considered in their essence; rather, insofar as they are subjects for the logical universal.
The passage gives examples: 'إنسان، حيوان، ناطق، ضاحك، ماش'.
3
Distinguish natural universal from logical universal
The logical universal is the concept (e.g., 'animal') that applies to many. The natural universal is the actual individuals (e.g., actual animals) as bearers of that concept. The difference is between concept (مفهوم) and subject (معروض).
The passage says: 'والفرق بين المفهوم والمعروض ظاهر'.
4
Understand the nuance about natural genus
Al-Sayyid clarifies that the same entity can be a natural universal when considered as subject of 'universal', and a natural genus when considered as subject of 'genus'. This highlights the aspect-based nature of the distinction.
5
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
In Kalam, the term 'temporally originated' (حادث) is a logical universal. Its natural universal is each individual temporally originated thing (e.g., the world). This helps avoid conflating the mental concept with external reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التَّصورات (وَ) ثانيها : ( مَعْرُوضُهُ) ، أي : ما تعرضُ الكلِّيَّةُ له. (قَوْلُهُ : مَعْرُوْضُهُ) أي : معروضُ مفهوم الكُلّيّ؛ أي: ما صدق عليه مفهوم الكُلّي، كَـ: «إنسان، وحيوان وناطق وضاحك وماش»، فالحيوان كُلّي طبيعي من حيثُ كونه معروضاً لِلكُلِّيّ المنطقيّ؛ لَا مِن حيثُ ذاتُهُ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَمَعْرُوْضُهُ) أي : مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ مَعروض ، فإِنَّهُ هُوَ الكُلِّيُّ الطَّبيعي على ما هُوَ التَّحقيقُ ; لَا ذاتُ المعروض مِنْ حيثُ هِي. قالَ السَّيِّدُ : إذا كانَ مَفهوم الحيوانِ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ كُلِّيًّا طبيعيًّا ; فَلَا فرقَ إِذَنْ بينَ مَفهوم الكُلِّيِّ الطَّبيعي ومفهوم الجنسِ الطَّبيعيّ، فالصَّوابُ أنَّ مَفهوم الحيوانِ مِنْ حيثُ هو معروضٌ لِمَفهوم الكُلِّيّ أو صالح لِكَوْنِهِ مَعروضاً لَهُ، كُلِّي طبيعي، ومِن حيثُ هو معروض لمفهوم الجنس أو صالح لِكَونِهِ مَعروضاً لَهُ ; جنس طبيعي .ا.هـ. ويُسمَّى كلّيًّا (طبيعيًّا ). والفرقُ بين المفهومِ والمعروض ظاهر . والحاصلُ : أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي ما صدقات المنطقي; أي: الأفراد التي يصدقُ عليها الكُلِّيُّ المنطقي كَـ : الحيوان وَمَا مَعَهُ»، لكن لا مِنْ حيث ذاتها كما هو ظاهرُ كلام الشَّمسيَّةِ، بل من حيثُ كونُها معروضةٌ لِلكُلِّيَّةِ المنطقيَّة ; أي: مُتَصفةٌ بعدم منعِها للاشتراك . (قَوْلُهُ: كُلِّيَّا طَبِيعِيًّا) سُمِّيَ الكُلّي الطَّبيعي كُلِّيَّا ; لأنَّهُ مَعروضٌ لِمَفهوم الكُلِّي مِنْ حيثُ هو معروض له، وطبيعيًّا ; لأنَّهُ مَنسوب إلى الطَّبيعة نسبة الفردِ إلى المفهوم .ا.هـ. مير زاهد (قَوْلُهُ : وَالفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْمَفْهُوْمِ ... إِلَخ) يريدُ أنَّ بيانَ الفرق بينَ الكُلِّيِّ الطَّبيعي والكُلّيّ المنطقيّ ظَاهرٌ.
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. (And) its second: (its subject), i.e., that to which universality attaches. (His saying: 'its subject') means the subject of the concept of the universal; i.e., that to which the concept of the universal applies, such as 'human', 'animal', 'rational', 'laughing', 'walking'. So 'animal' is a natural universal insofar as it is a subject for the logical universal, not insofar as its essence. (His saying: 'and its subject') means: insofar as it is a subject, for it is the natural universal according to what is the verification; not the essence of the subject insofar as it is. Al-Sayyid said: If the concept of animal insofar as it is a natural universal, then there is no difference between the concept of the natural universal and the concept of the natural genus. The correct view is that the concept of animal insofar as it is a subject for the concept of the universal, or fit to be a subject for it, is a natural universal, and insofar as it is a subject for the concept of genus or fit to be a subject for it, is a natural genus. End quote. And it is called a natural universal. And the difference between the concept and the subject is apparent. The conclusion: that the natural universal is the subject of the logical universal; i.e., the individuals to which the logical universal applies, such as 'animal' and what is with it, but not insofar as their essence as is apparent from the wording of al-Shamsiyya, but insofar as they are subjects for logical universality; i.e., characterized by not preventing sharing. (His saying: 'natural universal') it is called universal because it is a subject for the concept of the universal insofar as it is a subject for it, and natural because it is attributed to nature as an individual is attributed to a concept. End quote from Mir Zahid. (His saying: 'and the difference between the concept... etc.') He intends that explaining the difference between the natural universal and the logical universal is apparent.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion within a chapter on the principles of concepts. It identifies the second principle: 'subject of universality' (معروض الكلية). The author explains that this subject is the thing to which the concept of 'universal' applies, giving examples like 'human', 'animal', 'rational', 'laughing', 'walking'. A crucial distinction is made: the natural universal (كلي طبيعي) is not the essence of the thing itself, but the thing considered insofar as it is a subject for the logical universal. For instance, 'animal' is a natural universal when viewed as something that the logical universal 'animal' applies to, not when considered in its own essence. The passage then presents a subtle point from al-Sayyid: if we merely take the concept of animal as a natural universal, it would be identical to the concept of natural genus. The correction is that the animal is a natural universal when considered as a subject for the concept of 'universal' (or fit for it), and a natural genus when considered as a subject for the concept of 'genus'. This shows that the same entity can be classified differently depending on the aspect. The conclusion reiterates that the natural universal is the set of individuals that fall under the logical universal, but not merely as essences; rather, they are characterized by not preventing commonality (i.e., being shareable). The term 'natural universal' is explained: 'universal' because it is a subject for the concept of the universal, and 'natural' because it relates to nature as an individual relates to a concept. Finally, the passage notes that the difference between the concept (المفهوم) and the subject (المعروض) is clear: the concept is the logical universal, while the subject is the natural universal.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal is the subject of the logical universal, considered under the aspect of being a subject, not in its essence.

The Natural Universal: Existence and Exceptions
The author affirms that the natural universal can exist externally, but qualifies that not every universal does.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the claim that the natural universal can exist externally and the claim that it always exists, using examples from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Not every natural universal exists externally; some are impossible or merely possible but non-existent.

This passage tackles a key epistemological question: do universal realities exist outside the mind? The author affirms that the natural universal may exist externally, but immediately qualifies that this is not a universal truth. The commentators explain that some universals are impossible (like a partner of God) and some are possible but non-existent (like the phoenix). Understanding this distinction prevents overgeneralization in metaphysical arguments, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author states: 'والحق: وجود الكلي الطبيعي في الخارج'. This asserts that the natural universal exists in the external world. But note the word 'الحق' (the truth) signals a definitive position.
2
Observe the immediate qualification
Al-Dasuqi immediately clarifies that this does not mean every natural universal exists. He gives two categories: (1) those impossible in existence (e.g., a partner of God) and (2) those possible but non-existent (e.g., the phoenix). The claim is only that existence is possible for some.
The word 'ليس المراد' (it is not meant) shows the interpretation is restrictive.
3
Recognize the logical status
Al-Attar adds that the proposition 'the natural universal exists in the external world' is a قضية مهملة (weak/unquantified proposition). This means it is not universally true; it is only true for some instances.
In logic, 'مهملة' typically means lacking a quantifier; here it signifies lack of universality.
4
Examine al-Razi's argument and the refutation
Al-Razi argued: the natural universal is a part of an existing animal (e.g., the universal 'animal' is part of this horse), and a part of the existent is existent, so the universal exists. The author refutes by denying that the universal is an external part. The refutation is incomplete in the text, but the direction is clear: the universal is not an external part; it is a mental abstraction.
The refutation targets the minor premise (that the universal is an external part).

Source Text

وَالحَقُّ : وُجُودُ الكُلّيّ الطَّبِيعِيِّ في الخارج
الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: وُجُوْدُ الْكُلّيّ الطَّبِيعِي) أي: الحقيقةُ الكُلِّيَّة المعروضة للكُلّيّ المنطقي. ثمَّ إِنَّ قولَه : والحقُّ وجود ... إلخ؛ ليس المراد وجودَ كُلِّ كُلِّي طبيعي؛ لأنَّ منها ما هو ممتنع الوجود، ومنها ما هو مُمكن غير موجود گـ ماهية العنقاء»، بل المراد أنَّه قد يكون موجوداً .
العطار (قَوْلُهُ: وَالحَقُّ وُجُوْدُ الْكُلّيّ الطَّبِيعِيِّ فِي الْخَارج) أي : قد يكون موجوداً فيه لا أنَّ كُلَّ كُلِّي طَبيعي موجودٌ في الخارج، إذْ مِنَ الكُلِّيَّاتِ الطَّبيعيَّةِ مَا هو ممتنع الوجودِ فيه كشريك الباري، وما هو معدومٌ مُمكنٌ كَالعنقاء ؛ قالَهُ السَّيِّدُ، فَقولنا : الكُلِّي الطَّبيعي موجود في الخارج، قضيَّةٌ مُهملةٌ ، وقد استدلَّ الرَّازِيُّ في شرح الرسالة على وجودِهِ بأنَّهُ جزءٌ مِنْ هذا الحيوان الموجود في الخارج، وجزء الموجود موجود، وردَّهُ المصنِّفُ في شرحِهِ عليها بأنَّا لا نسلّمُ أنَّ المطلق جزءٌ خارجي مِنَ ...
And the truth: the existence of the natural universal in the external world. al-Dasuqi (his statement: 'existence of the natural universal') i.e., the universal reality that is the subject of the logical universal. Then his statement: 'And the truth: existence... etc.' does not mean the existence of every natural universal; because among them are those that are impossible in existence, and among them are those that are possible but not existent, like the quiddity of the phoenix. Rather, it means that it may exist. al-Attar (his statement: 'And the truth: existence of the natural universal in the external world') i.e., it may exist therein, not that every natural universal exists in the external world, for among the natural universals are those that are impossible in existence therein, like a partner of God, and those that are non-existent possible, like the phoenix; as said by al-Sayyid. So our saying: 'the natural universal exists in the external world' is a weak proposition. And al-Razi argued in his commentary on al-Risala for its existence by saying that it is a part of this animal existing in the external world, and a part of the existent is existent. The author refuted it in his commentary on it by saying: we do not accept that the absolute is an external part of ...
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological clarification about the ontological status of the natural universal. It begins by stating that the truth is that the natural universal (الكلي الطبيعي) exists in the external world. However, the commentators immediately qualify this claim. al-Dasuqi explains that this does not mean every natural universal exists; some are impossible (like a partner of God) and some are possible but non-existent (like the phoenix). The intention is that existence is possible. al-Attar adds that the proposition 'the natural universal exists externally' is a weak (مهملة) proposition, meaning it is not universal. He then mentions an argument by al-Razi that the natural universal exists because it is part of an existing animal, and part of an existent is existent. The author (Sahib al-Tahzeeb?) refutes this by denying that the absolute (the universal) is an external part. The refutation is incomplete in the text. The lesson teaches that the existence of universals is not a necessary truth; rather, it is contingent.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي
قضية مهملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Attar describe the statement 'the natural universal exists in the external world' as a 'weak proposition' (قضية مهملة)?
A) Because it is false.
B) Because it is not universally true; some natural universals are non-existent or impossible.
C) Because it is only true for animals.
D) Because it is a logical contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The existence of the natural universal in the external world is not a necessary truth; it is contingent, as some universals are impossible or non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Classify each of the following natural universals as externally existent, possible but non-existent, or impossible: a) the quiddity of a partner of God, b) the quiddity of fire, c) the quiddity of a phoenix. Explain your reasoning using the lesson's qualification.
?
2. Al-Razi argued: 'The universal animal is a part of this existing horse; parts of existents are existent; therefore the universal animal exists externally.' The Universal as a Constitutive Part of Quiddity
Commentator explains that the universal is a constitutive part of individuals, using the triangle example and a summary argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the universal must be a constitutive part of its individuals, using the triangle analogy and the argument from shared essence.

Core Idea
The universal is not a mere label but an essential part of what each individual is.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a key claim: the universal (like 'animal') is not just a mental classification but a constitutive part (juz' muqawwim) of the individuals that fall under it. He uses a concrete example from geometry—the triangle—to make the logical point clear. Understanding this will help you see how definitions in Islamic logic are built and why the universal is intrinsic to the identity of a species.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence where the commentator states that the universal (e.g., animal) is 'mutakawwim' (constitutive). Notice that he asserts this with certainty ('najzimu').
The claim is that the universal is a part (juz') of the individual's essence.
2
Analyze the triangle example
The commentator says a triangular surface is constituted by lines and the surface itself. Break this down: what are the parts? Why can't the triangle be without lines? Connect this to the universal-part relationship.
Lines are like the genus; the surface is like the differentia; together they constitute the triangle.
3
Understand the disregard for existence
The commentator stresses that the analysis of quiddity (mahiyya) ignores whether the thing exists externally. Why is this important? Because the universal is a part of the essence itself, not of the individual as existent.
This avoids confusion: even if a species has no external individuals, its quiddity still has the universal as a part.
4
Trace the summary argument
The commentator gives a condensed version: individuals (man, horse) share in animality while disregarding existence and accidents. This shared matter is what constitutes them in themselves. Verify that this leads to the conclusion that the universal is a constitutive part.
The argument assumes that shared essential attributes are constitutive, not accidental.
5
Apply to a real Islamic example
Consider the definition of 'human' in Islamic philosophy: animal (genus) + rational (differentia). Show that 'animal' is the universal that constitutes every human, just as lines constitute a triangle.
This definition is a hadd haqiqi (true definition) because it gives the constitutive parts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذاتيا كَمَا حَقَّقَهُ السَّيِّدُ في حاشيةِ التَّجريدِ؟ (بل نجزمُ بأَنَّهُ) أي : الشَّيء الَّذِي حمل عليهِ الحيوانُ (مُتقوِّم) ذلكَ الشَّيءُ بِهِ ) أي : بالحيوان؛ لأَنَّهُ جزءهُ، فَهُوَ داخل في قوامِهِ وحقيقتِهِ وَلَا نعني بالجزء إِلَّا مَا يتقوَّمُ بِهِ الشَّيء) وعطف قوله : (ولا يمكنُ تحصيلُ ما هَيَّتِهِ بِدونِهِ) تَفسيرُ، ثُمَّ مَثَلَ لِذلكَ بِمثالٍ مَحسوس فقال : (كَالمُثَلَّثِ) أي : السَّطحِ المثلث وهو ما أحاطَ بِهِ ثلاث خطوط (فَإِنَّهُ لا يتقوَّمُ وَلَا يتحصَّلُ بدونِ الخطّ لأنَّ الخطّ جزؤهُ، والمراد جنس الخطّ لأنَّهُ أَحاطَ بِهِ ثلاث خطوط (والشطحُ) المحاط به؛ لأنَّه جزءهُ الثَّانِي مَعَ قطع النَّظرِ عَنْ وجودِهِ) أي : وجودِ ذلكَ الشَّيء المتقوم بالجزء (وعدمِهِ) وإِنَّما قطعْنَا النَّظَرَ عَن وجودِ ذلكَ الشَّيء وعدمِهِ؛ لأنَّ الكلامَ مَفروضٌ في تقوم الماهِيَّةِ المركَّبةِ بِجزئها؛ فَيشمل سائرَ الماهيَّاتِ المركَّبة موجودة في الخارج أوَّلاً، فإذا ثبتَ تَقَوُّمُهَا بِالجزء وعرضَ لَهَا الوجود خارجاً؛ يجب أن يوجد جزؤها خارجاً ضرورة اتحادِ الكُلِّ والجزء في ظرفِ الوجودِ، فَلِذلك قالَ : وَلَا شَلَّ أَنَّ مَا يُنقوَّمُ بهِ الموجود يجب أن يكونَ موجوداً، وخُلاصتُهُ) أي: خلاصةُ الدَّليلِ السَّابقِ كَمَا هُوَ المتبادرُ، لكنَّ الكلام المذكور يفيدُ أَنَّهُ دليلٌ آخَرُ وهوَ الظَّاهِرُ، وحينئذٍ فَالمراد : وخُلاصة الاستدلال (أَنَّهُ لا شَكٍّ أَنَّ بعضَ الأشخاص) كَالإنسانِ يشارك بعضاً آخَرَ) كَالفرس (دونَ بعض) كَالشَّجرِ (في أمرٍ وهِيَ الحيوانيَّة (مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الوجودِ وَمَا يَتبعُهُ مِنَ العوارضِ) أي : الآثار المترتبة عليه، وإنّما قطعنَا النَّظرَ عَنِ الوجودِ هُنَا؛ لِأَنَّهُ عام لسائر الموجوداتِ، ونحن لم نعتبر المشاركة فيهِ؛ بلْ إِنَّما اعتبرنَاهَا في الماهِيَّةِ المندرج تحتها تلك الأشخاصُ كَمَا مِثْلُنَا فَذلِكَ الأمرُ المشترك تتقوَّمُ بِهِ الأشخاص حد ذاتِهَا أي: مَعَ قطع النَّظرِ عَنِ الوجودِ وَمَا يتبعه من العوارض، وفي مير
Essential, as al-Sayyid verified in his gloss on al-Tajrid? Rather we are certain that it (i.e., the thing upon which 'animal' is predicated) is constituted by it (i.e., by animal), because it is its part, so it is inside its constitution and reality. And we do not mean by 'part' except that by which a thing is constituted. And his saying: 'And it is not possible to obtain its quiddity without it' is an explanation. Then he gave a concrete example: like the triangle, i.e., a triangular surface, which is surrounded by three lines. For it is not constituted nor obtained without a line, because the line is its part. And what is meant is the genus of line, because it is surrounded by three lines. And the surface is surrounded; because it is its second part, with disregard of its existence and non-existence. And we disregarded the existence or non-existence of that thing constituted by the part because the discussion is assumed about the constitution of a composite quiddity by its part, so it includes all composite quiddities whether existing externally or not. So if their constitution by the part is established and they happen to exist externally, then their part must exist externally by necessity of the unity of the whole and the part in the locus of existence. Therefore he said: And there is no doubt that what a thing is constituted by must exist. And its summary: that there is no doubt that some individuals (like man) share with others (like horse) to the exclusion of others (like tree) in a matter, namely animality, with disregard of existence and what follows it of accidents, i.e., the effects resulting from it. And we only disregarded existence here because it is common to all existents, and we did not consider participation in it; rather we considered it in the quiddity under which those individuals fall, as we exemplified. So that shared matter constitutes the individuals in themselves, i.e., with disregard of existence and what follows it of accidents.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the argument for the natural universal being a constitutive part of its individuals. The commentator begins by affirming that when we predicte 'animal' of something, it is constitutive (mutakawwim) because it is a part of the essence. He explains that 'part' here means that without which the thing cannot be constituted. He then gives the example of a triangle: a triangular surface is constituted by lines (as one part) and the surface itself (as another part). The line is a part, and without it the triangle cannot exist. Importantly, he notes that this analysis disregards actual existence; it is about the quiddity itself. If the quiddity does exist externally, then its parts must also exist externally because whole and part share the same existential locus. He then summarizes the earlier argument: individuals share in a common essence (like animality) while ignoring existence and accidents; this shared essence is what constitutes them in their quiddity. The passage emphasizes that the universal is not a mere mental construct but an essential constituent of the individuals, when considered in themselves. This is a key point in the debate about universals: the natural universal, though it has no separate external existence, is intrinsic to the identity of each individual.

Key Terms
متقوّم (mutakawwim)
جزء (juz')
ماهية مركبة (mahiyya murakkaba)
مشاركة (musharaka)

What does the triangle example illustrate about the universal?
A) That a part must have independent existence.
B) That a composite quiddity cannot be constituted without its parts, even when disregarding existence.
C) That lines are more important than surfaces.
D) That the universal does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The universal is a constitutive part of individuals in their quiddity, not merely a mental construct, as shown by the triangle analogy and the argument from shared essence.

Existence of the Natural Universal: Reality vs. Sensibility
Author defends the external existence of the natural universal against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the natural universal exists externally but is not sensible, and reconcile the apparent contradiction of one existence and two existents using the distinction between external unity and mental duality.

Core Idea
The natural universal exists in reality but is only intelligible, not sensible.

This passage tackles a fundamental epistemological puzzle: if the universal (e.g., humanity) exists in the external world, why can't we perceive it with our senses? Drawing on the commentaries of Mir Zaid, Al-Dawwani, and Abu al-Fath, the author argues that the natural universal exists externally but is only grasped by the intellect because sensibility requires the addition of specific accidents. The discussion culminates in a resolution that distinguishes between the unity of existence in the external world and the duality of mental consideration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main thesis
Locate the passage where the author states that the natural universal exists in the external world. This is the claim being defended.
2
Examine Mir Zaid's three stages
Study the three stages of sensible objects: (1) essence in itself (only essential attributes), (2) essence with existence (accidental attributes that accompany existence), (3) essence with specific accidents (makes it sensible). Understand that in stage 1, the universal is not sensible.
This shows how the same essence can be both non-sensible and sensible depending on consideration.
3
Analyze Al-Dawwani's objection
Al-Dawwani objects: if the universal and the individual share one existence, then existence is one but existent is two—a paradox. Why is this problematic? Consider the implication that one meaning would subsist in multiple loci.
4
Follow the resolution by Mir Zaid
Mir Zaid resolves the objection: in the external world, there is only one existence (the individual existent). The universal is mentally abstracted from that same existent. So in the mind, we have two (pure nature and mixed nature), but externally nothing but the individual. Hence, existence is one, existent is two only in the mind.
5
Connect to broader Islamic philosophy
Note terms like 'divine existence' and 'existence before multiplicity' for the pure nature. This discussion underpins the reality of universals in Islamic thought, crucial for fields like usul al-fiqh where the 'illa (effective cause) is a universal applicable to all instances.

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات على كثيرين مختلفين في تلك الأحوال، فإذن الإنسانُ مِن حيث هو واحد بالحقيقة؛ بلْ مِن حيثُ حقيقته الأصليَّةُ الَّتي تَختلفُ فيها الكثرةُ؛ غيرُ مَحسوس بلْ معقول صرف، وكذا الحال في كُلِّ كُلّيّ؛ هذا كلامه .ا.هـ. قال مير زاهد: قِيلَ: الطَّبيعةُ والشَّخصُ مُتَّحدانِ في الخارج، فَلَا يعقلُ كَوْنُ الشَّخْصِ موجوداً وَمَحسوساً، والطَّبيعة موجودةً غير محسوسة، ولا يَخفَى أَنَّ الشَّيءَ لَا يصيرُ مَحسوساً بالذَّاتِ أو بالعرض إلا بعد اقترانِهِ بعوارض مخصوصةٍ مِنَ الأين والوضع ونحوهما، فالطَّبيعةُ لما اعتبرَتْ مُجرَّدةً عنها ؛ لا تكون محسوسةً لا بالذَّاتِ ولا بالعرض، وتفصيلُهُ أَنَّ المحسوساتِ لَهَا مَراتبُ ؛ الأُوْلَى : نَفْسُهَا مِنْ حيثُ هي، وفي هذه المرتبة لا تصدقُ عليها إلا ذاتياتُها ، والثَّانية : نَفْسُهَا مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّها موجودة، وفي هذه المرتبة يصدقُ عليها الذَّاتيَّاتُ والوجود وما يحذو حذو وجودِها مِنَ العرضيات، والثَّالثةُ : نَفْسُهَا مِنْ حيثُ اتِّصافُهَا بِعوارض مخصوصةٍ مِنَ الأين والوضع ونحوهما، وفي هذه المرتبة يتعلَّقُ بِهَا الحس وتصير محسوسة بالذَّاتِ أو بالعرضِ، فظهرَ أَنَّ الماهيَّةَ معَ قطع النَّظرِ عَنِ الأعراض المخصوصة؛ موجودةٌ وليستْ بِمَحسوسةٍ أَصْلاً ؛ فَتَأَمَّلْ جدا ا.هـ. فثبت أنَّ الكُلِّيَّ الطَّبيعي موجود في الخارج، قالَ الدَّوَّانِيُّ : لا يُقَالُ هذا يرجع إلى وجودِ الشَّخص كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ المصنِّفُ، وَلَا نزاع فيهِ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : بل هذا النَّظَرُ كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ الشَّيخُ آيْفَاً يُعطي وجودَ أمرٍ آخر بوجودِ لِلشَّخص، فَالوجود واحدٌ والموجود اثنانِ، وَلَو قال المصنِّفُ : بعين وجود أفرادِهِ؛ لَكَانَ بِعِينِهِ مَذهَبُ القدماء ا.هـ. قال أبو الفتح مَنشَأُ السُّؤالِ أَنَّهُ يَحتَملُ أنْ يكونَ مُرَادُ الشَّيخِ بِوجودِ الإنسان وجودَ أشخاصهِ مَجازاً كَمَا أشار إليهِ المصنِّفُ بقولِهِ : بمعنى وجودِ أشخاصه، وحاصل الجوابِ أنَّ كلامَ الشَّيخ صَريحُ في ردّ أوهامِ النَّاسِ مِن أَنَّ كُلَّ موجودٍ مَحسوس، وَلَا شَكٍّ أنَّ توهُمَ النَّاسِ إِنَّما هو في الموجودِ الحقيقي دونَ : المطار المجازي، فَلَا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ مَقصود الشيخ وجود الإنسان حقيقةٌ، لكنَّهُ مُطَالبٌ بالبيانِ حتَّى يتعيَّنَ ؛ لأنَّا لَسْنَا مِمَّنْ آمَنَ بِمَا بين دفتي الشّفاء» و«الإشاراتِ»، وأما قوله : فالوجود واحد والموجود اثنان؛ فهو مع كونِهِ مِمَّا لا يدلُّ عليهِ كَلامُ الشَّيخ محلَّ نظرٍ ؛ لأنَّهُ إنْ كانَ كُلَّ واحدٍ منهما موجوداً بذلك الوجودِ؛ يلزم قيام معنى واحدٍ بمحال مختلفةٍ، وإنْ كانَ الموجودُ مَجموعهما فقط ؛ يلزم وجودُ الكُلِّ بدونِ أجزائِهِ، وَكِلَا اللَّازِمَيْنِ مُحالٌ قطعاً .ا.هـ. وأجاب مير زاهد : بأنَّ الوجود واحد في الخارج والموجود اثنان في الذهنِ، فَمَا هو اثنان في الذهنِ؛ موجودٌ في الخارج بوجود واحد، وذلك لأنَّه ليس في الخارج إلَّا الطَّبيعة والمخلوطة بعوارض مخصوصة، الموجودة بوجودٍ واحدٍ شَخصي، ثمَّ العقلُ يعتبر تلك الطَّبيعة المحضة مِنْ حيثُ هِيَ ؛ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ العوارض ، وحينئذ يحصل اثنانِ ؛ الطَّبيعة المحضة والطَّبيعة المخلوطة، وهما مُتغايرانِ في الذهن ومُتَّحدانِ في الوجودِ، وربَّما يُقالُ لذلك الوجودِ مِن حيثُ إِنَّهُ لِلطَّبيعة المحضة: الوجودُ الإلهى والوجود قبل الكثرة ؛ لأَنَّهُ ليسَ إلَّا بعناية الله سبحانه وتعالى، وأمَّا مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ لِلشَّخص وإِنْ كَانَ بِعنايةِ الله تعالى؛ إِلَّا أَنَّ مُصحِّحَ إستنادِهِ إليه سبحانه العوارض المادّيَّةُ ا.هـ. وقد نظمنا في هذه المقولةِ الشَّوارد الكثيرة الفوائدِ ؛ فَلَا تَسأمْ مِنَ الإطالة وَلَا تَتَشكَّى الملالة.
Section: On the principles of concepts. [Regarding] the many [individuals] differing in those states, thus man from the perspective that he is one in reality; rather from the perspective of his original essence which the multiplicity differs in; is not sensible but purely intelligible. And the same holds for every universal. This is his statement. End. Mir Zaid said: It was said: Nature and the individual are united in the external world, so it is not understood that the individual exists and is sensible while nature exists but is not sensible. It is not hidden that a thing does not become sensible essentially or accidentally except after its conjunction with specific accidents like place, position, and their likes. So when nature is considered stripped of them, it is not sensible essentially nor accidentally. Its elaboration is that sensible things have stages: First: themselves in themselves; in this stage only their essential attributes apply to them. Second: themselves in terms of their existence; in this stage essential attributes, existence, and what follows their existence of accidental attributes apply to them. Third: themselves in terms of their being described with specific accidents of place, position, and their likes; in this stage sense-perception relates to them and they become sensible essentially or accidentally. So it became clear that the essence, disregarding specific accidents, exists and is not sensible at all. So contemplate deeply. End. So it is established that the natural universal exists in the external world. Al-Dawwani said: It is not said that this returns to the existence of the individual as the author stated, and there is no dispute about this; because we say: Rather this consideration as stated by the Shaykh above gives existence to another thing by the existence of the individual. So existence is one and the existing thing is two. And if the author had said: by the very existence of its individuals, it would be exactly the doctrine of the ancients. End. Abu al-Fath said: The origin of the question is that it is possible that the Shaykh's intention by 'existence of man' is metaphorically the existence of his individuals, as the author indicated by his saying: meaning the existence of his individuals. The summary of the answer is that the Shaykh's speech is explicit in refuting people's illusions that every existent is sensible, and there is no doubt that people's illusion is only about real existent apart from: The metaphorical scope, so it is necessary that the Shaykh's intention is the existence of man in reality, but he is required to clarify so that it is determined; because we are not among those who believe in what is between the covers of 'al-Shifa' and 'al-Isharat'. As for his saying: Existence is one and the existing thing is two; it is, despite its being among what is not indicated by the Shaykh's speech, a point of consideration; because if each one of them exists by that existence, it would necessitate that one meaning subsists in different places, and if the existing thing is only the two together, it would necessitate the existence of the whole without its parts. Both consequences are absolutely impossible. End. And Mir Zaid answered: That existence is one in the external world and the existing thing is two in the mind. So what is two in the mind exists in the external world with one existence. That is because there is nothing in the external world except nature mixed with specific accidents, existing with a single individual existence. Then the intellect considers that pure nature in itself, disregarding accidents; then two things result: pure nature and mixed nature. They are different in the mind and united in existence. Perhaps that existence, in terms of its being for pure nature, is called divine existence and existence before multiplicity, because it is only through the care of Allah, the Sublime and Exalted. As for in terms of it being for the individual, even if it is through the care of Allah the Exalted, nevertheless the material accidents are what correct its attribution to Him. End. And we have composed in this statement abundant benefits; so do not tire of prolixity and do not complain of boredom.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed discussion on the ontological status of the natural universal. It begins by establishing that the natural universal (e.g., 'human') is not sensible in itself but is intelligible, existing in reality as one with the individuals but abstracted by the mind. The author then presents Mir Zaid's explanation of the three stages of sensible objects: (1) essence without accidents, (2) essence with existence, (3) essence with specific accidents making it sensible. From this, it follows that the natural universal exists externally but is not sensible. Al-Dawwani raises an objection: if the universal exists by the same existence as the individual, then there is only one existence but two existents, which seems problematic. Abu al-Fath responds by saying that the Shaykh's intent is to refute the common notion that every existent is sensible, and the universal's existence is real. The final answer from Mir Zaid resolves the issue by distinguishing between external reality and mental analysis: in the external world, there is only the individual existent, but the mind abstracts the pure nature, resulting in two mental objects. Thus, existence is one in reality, but the mind counts two. The passage also mentions the 'divine existence' and 'existence before multiplicity' as terms for the pure nature's existence. This discussion is crucial for understanding the relationship between universals and particulars in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي
الطبيعة
الشخص
الوجود

Checkpoint

A) Because it does not exist in the external world.
B) Because it requires specific accidents to be perceived, and when considered in itself it lacks them.
C) Because it is only a mental construct.
D) Because it is a logical universal.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal exists in the external world, but its existence is identical to that of the individual; it is only the mind that distinguishes the pure essence from the individual, resulting in two mental concepts but one external reality.

?

Why External Reality of Universals Doesn't Matter to Logic
Author states that logical and mental universals do not exist externally and the matter is outside logic's scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the external existence of logical and mental universals is irrelevant to the science of logic.

Core Idea
Logic cares about utility in reasoning, not metaphysical reality.

This passage draws an important boundary: logic is not metaphysics. It may seem natural to ask whether universals like 'humanity' exist in the external world, but the logician's concern is different. Here, the author and his commentators clarify that logic only needs universals as tools for reasoning—their ontological status is irrelevant to the art of inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Note that the passage explicitly mentions two types: the logical universal (al-kullī al-manṭiqī) and the mental universal (al-kullī al-ʿaqlī). These are distinct from the natural universal discussed earlier.
The natural universal exists in the external world as an essence; these do not.
2
Understand why external existence is impossible
Al-Dasuqi explains: if these universals existed externally, they would become particularized (تشخص), which contradicts their universality. Something that exists outside the mind is a specific individual, not a universal predicable of many.
This is a classic argument against the reality of universals.
3
Recognize logic's purpose
The text defines logic's subject: it investigates conceptual and assertoric information (تصورات وتصديقات) in terms of how they lead (إيصال) to the unknown.
Logic is a tool, not a science of being.
4
Connect purpose to irrelevance
The process of leading from known to unknown does not depend on the external existence of logical or mental universals. They work perfectly well as mental constructs.
Even if they don't exist 'out there', they still function in reasoning.
5
Conclude the boundary
Therefore, the question of their external existence is 'outside the art'—it belongs to metaphysics or natural philosophy, not logic. The logician need not address it.
This delimitation protects logic from extraneous debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الكلِّيُّ المنطقي والعقلي، فلم يثبت وجودهما في الخارج، والنظر فيه خارج عن الصناعة، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَلَمْ يَثْبُتْ وُجُوْدُهُمَا فِي الْخَارج أي : لأنَّ وجودَهُما في الخارج يقتضي تَشخُصهما، وهو يُنافي كُلِّيَّتَهما . (قَوْلُهُ : خَارِجٌ عَن الصَّنَاعَةِ) أي: صناعة أهل المنطق؛ أي: خارج عن فنّ المنطق؛ لأنَّهُ إنَّما يبحث فيه عن المعلوماتِ التَّصوريَّةِ والتصديقيَّةِ مِن حيثُ إنَّها توصل إلى مجهول، والتَّوصُّلُ المذكورُ لا يتوقَّفُ على وجودِهما في الخارج. (قَوْلُهُ: وَالنَّظَرُ فِيْهِ) أي: في وجودِهِمَا خارجٌ عَنِ الصَّناعة؛ أي: صناعة المنطقِ؛ لأنَّها باحثةٌ عَمَّا لَهُ دَخل في الإيصال، قالَ الرَّازِيُّ في شرح الرسالة : لأنَّ
As for the logical and mental universals, their existence in the external world is not established, and consideration of it is outside the art. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'their existence in the external world is not established') means because their existence in the external world necessitates their individuation, which contradicts their universality. (His saying: 'outside the art') means the art of the people of logic; i.e., outside the science of logic; because it only investigates conceptual and assertoric information in terms of their leading to the unknown, and the aforementioned leading does not depend on their existence in the external world. (His saying: 'and consideration of it') i.e., consideration of their existence is outside the art; i.e., the art of logic; because it investigates only what has a role in conveyance. Al-Razi said in his commentary on the Epistle: because
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage clarifies the status of logical and mental universals. The author first asserts that their external existence is not established, and then declares that even if it were, it is irrelevant to the science of logic. Al-Dasuqi's commentary explains why: if these universals existed externally, they would become particularized, which contradicts their universal nature. Moreover, logic is concerned with how concepts and judgments lead to unknown truths, and this process does not require the actual external existence of logical or mental universals. The mention of al-Razi's commentary reinforces that logic's scope is limited to what aids in inference. Thus, the passage serves to delimit the subject matter of logic, warning against straying into metaphysical questions about the reality of universals.

Key Terms

الكلي العقلي
الصناعة

Key Takeaway
Logic's focus is on the conveyance of knowledge, not the ontology of universals.
